				- [Scattergories](https://swellgarfo.com/scattergories/)
				  collapsed:: true
					- Alright, folks, it’s nearly energizer time already! For today’s energizer, we’d suggest playing a round of ) in your team. Pick a person to share their screen, use the -/+ buttons in the top right to set the number of categories (we’d recommend 5-6), set the time for 60 sec, press restart to apply all the settings, and see how well you do! Then make sure to have a break before you crack on for the rest of the afternoon.As for the rest of the afternoon, coaches will be circulating around the rooms to check in and see how you’re getting on. At about 4:30, we’d like you to spend about 20min in your team having a retro discussion on how today went and learnings to take into tomorrow. We’d recommend a simple format such as stop/start/continue or the four Ls.We’ll come together again at 4:50 as usual, where we’ll pick some groups to share key points from your retro with the wider cohort.

		- Curriculum weekly overview

			- Overview
				- **1) Computational Thinking**
				  id:: 633b752e-536f-48e7-8ad6-b395a7afa8d6
				  collapsed:: true
					- 1) Intro to the School of Code way - Computational Thinking and Problem-Solving with JavaScript
				- **2) JavaScript Foundations** - getting up to speed with the JavaScript language and interacting with APIs
				  id:: 633b752e-2851-4cc3-b5c5-984c3d314c15
				- **3) Front-end Foundations** - creating applications with the user in mind - user-driven design, UI/UX, and an introduction to Agile
				  id:: 6341c13f-ce42-40b0-ad1d-e6cc2c0fdd77
				- **4) Back-end** - the world of server-side programming - creating APIs, learning NodeJS, communicating with data
				  id:: 634bc1c4-4300-4444-a0d2-99d31c95436b
				- **5) Databases and Conventions** - databases, SQL, and RESTful APIs
				  id:: 63515211-a13a-4cea-9354-e2a4ec1d20ef
				- **6) Testing** - Robust code in systems - testing and modern software engineering practices
				  id:: 635eb261-68c0-4ead-a676-ba6c419c33b0
				- **7) React Fundamentals** - component-based thinking and frameworks - React introduction and advanced user interfaces
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 6341c13f-a059-4f33-9173-e52bbcc9e2c8
					- React has
					  collapsed:: true
						- job market demand
						- Employer feedback is that they want React experience
						- Tech roadmap - is likely to stay current for years
						- Extensibility of knowledge - it's not a niche tool, it helps you get better at JavaScript too
				- **8) React Advanced** - developing good, user-centric products in teams - deeper dive into React, agile project management, and great user experiences
				  id:: 637210c7-2f70-48a8-9abe-0a7707a15fd0
				- **9) Project Week**
				  id:: 6377ecf2-ff9d-4af0-bce3-87e9cc3ae525
				- **10) Retrospectives and Authentication** - learning from project experiences and Agile best practices, plus authentication
				  id:: 6377ecf2-de49-473e-9e84-f157cde8b08d
				- ^^Not covered^^
					- **10+) Active recruitment prep** - getting ready with everything you'll need to join the recruitment process at the end of the course
					  id:: 638d0629-c28d-474e-b0fe-1f8550820501
						- Related: ((629ccb26-a280-407e-86ec-f53f4085d664))
				- **11-13) Adding depth to understanding** - going deeper into advanced computer science and software engineering concepts
					- **11) Deeper Dives**- Deploying apps and React Advanced 2
					  id:: 639b2694-b404-427f-9a4c-71098d1b3383
					- **12) Languages and Architecture** - TypeScript, debugging
					  id:: 63974fb1-a706-4294-ad37-500e8b9ee362
				- **13) Start of four-week final project** - working in teams and layering in more knowledge and skills through targeted sessions
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 63a9bb72-b05b-490b-863f-25b1d35c533d
					- 4 week projects
					  collapsed:: true
						- Small teams; Live demos; Self-managed and self-driven; real world problem; a chance to realy put into practice everything from teamwork to architecture to agile to engineering
						- 13)
						- 14)
						- 15)
						- 16)
						- 16) Final projects finish - you'll have created your best work yet, and have learned a lot along the way
				- **17+) Career Launchpad** - presenting final projects to employers and support from the recruitment team into interviews and job roles, along side support from the coaching team and fellow bootcampers to continue your learning
				  id:: 63c581ca-87b8-4d1c-bc72-cbab5ea2a604
				- 1st february - Exclusive Partner Demo Day
					- private partners visit day in Kumospace
					- This is going to be an invite only Demo Day where employer partners will be assigned slots to visit your project teams in Kumospace! This is going to be a fantastic opportunity for discussion time with employers, and to get your projects seen!
				- 7th march - Launch Day
					- launch day in Kumospace - welcome to everyone, businesses, mentors, family, friends, anyone
					- This will be an event that is open to all and will again be hosted in Kumospace. At this event, everyone is welcome! Family, friends, other businesses, mentors, and many people from the School of Code community will attend to meet you and learn about your projects.
				- Overview of the end
					- The time between these two events can be used to continue working on your projects and make sure they are the best they can be! This period will also give you the change to apply for roles with our Employer Partners and focus on improving your employability profile, including your CV, Github profiles, and more. We'd also love for you to shout far and wide about launch day! The more the merrier!
					- We have an EventBrite link for that first event with employer partners here: [https://www.eventbrite.co.uk/e/school-of-code-demo-day-130-tickets-489671569847](https://www.eventbrite.co.uk/e/school-of-code-demo-day-130-tickets-489671569847) for you to go ahead and sign up.

		- To ask
		  id:: 6347aca7-c231-4480-9314-3a03d4569e1b
		  collapsed:: true
			- Is there a more succinct way of writing? Have tried ((adf5843f-68dc-4059-9634-3059f69a6635)) and ((6343d976-fbe8-45fe-bc74-1fe1b5309a59))
			  id:: 6347acac-fc18-424a-9d4f-f9e14aba1e30
			  collapsed:: true
				- ```javascript
				  let string = "BANANA";
				  
				  function testString() {
				    if (string[0] === "B" || string[0] === "A" || string[0] === "N" || string[0] === "E" || string[0] === "S" || string[0] === "T") {
				  	console.log('true')
				    }
				  }
				  ```
					- ```javascript
					  let string = "BANANA";
					  let testLetters = "BANEST";
					  
					  function testString() {
					    for (var i = 0; i < string.length; i++) {
					      if (string[0].includes(testLetters[i])) {
					        console.log('true')
					      }
					    }
					  ```
					- Doesn't work
						- ```javascript
						  let string = "BANANA";
						  
						  function test1() {
						    if (string[0] === "G" || "C" || "A") { // doesn't work
						  	console.log('true')
						    }
						  }
						  ```

		- Post-Launch Day Support
		  collapsed:: true
			- Announcement
			  collapsed:: true
				- We understand that the job search process can be emotionally tough, and we are committed to supporting you every step of the way. We know what works, we've seen hundreds of bootcampers get into their first roles in tech and we're committed to helping you all achieve the same outcome ![:muscle:](https://a.slack-edge.com/production-standard-emoji-assets/14.0/google-medium/1f4aa@2x.png)So... In addition to our [SLA](https://schoolofcode-bc13.slack.com/archives/C04L8MPBJL9/p1678365737623559) with regard to our Partner Roles, we're excited to offer the following support post-launch day ![:drum_with_drumsticks:](https://a.slack-edge.com/production-standard-emoji-assets/14.0/google-medium/1f941@2x.png)**Assigned Career’s Coaches ****![:rocket:](https://a.slack-edge.com/production-standard-emoji-assets/14.0/google-medium/1f680@2x.png)**Every bootcamper will be assigned a Career’s Coach. This team member will be your number one point of contact for SOC support during your search for your first role in tech and the person you keep updated on your progress.**As your Career’s Coaches we will:**
				- **Assign you to an Accountability Group**
				- Accountability Groups are small teams (~5) of bootcampers who can work together to support each other during this job search phase. We know the process can be tough and your support of each other will be invaluable in maintaining a positive attitude.
				- **Provide weekly Recruitment Clinic Meetings (1 hr)**
				- These weekly meetings will replace our employability sessions, they'll be set at regular times each week and during these we will help you stay focused and motivated. They will provide a focus for your Accountability Group activity to work on interview preparation, job search strategies, CV tailoring, networking, personal branding and sharing potential opportunities with each other.
				- These meetings will also be a time where you can review your targeted actions from the previous week and set goals for the following week.
				- **Offer regular “Recruitment Office Hours”**
				- Each week we will make available two hours worth of 20 minute, 1:1 bookable slots to help you with any individualised support needs for your job search. This can include help with any questions or concerns you may have during your job search, or guidance on everything from crafting your CV to preparing for interviews.
				  
				  **We want to see you all in roles, thriving in the tech industry! So we can help support you, we ask that you commit to:**
				- **Staying engaged:**
				- **Apply for a minimum of 10 roles each week**. Commit to the activities and goals set by us (these will be individualised to help support you). Job hunting is a numbers game, and not every application will result in an offer. It's important to stay positive and not take rejection personally, using it as an opportunity to learn and improve your skills for future opportunities. Just like on the bootcamp, results **will** follow persistent, patient and focused application!
				- **Attend accountability group sessions**. This will help you stay motivated, receive support from your peers, and learn valuable skills that will help you succeed in your job search. We understand that job searching can be a stressful and isolating process, and we are here to help you through it.
				- We also ask that, once you have secured your role in tech, that you continue to engage with your Accountability Group (even if it needs to be asynchronously) - some of the best advice, insights, support and opportunities come from each other!
				- **Staying active on Huntr**
				- Make sure your **Huntr is up to date** by Friday morning so we can review your progress.
				- **Save any roles** you apply for using the Huntr plugin (this will avoid us posting any duplicate roles on your board)
				- **Move the job cards** if you’ve been invited to interview or rejected, as it will help us track your progress and provide feedback and support where needed.
				- Track your **goals**
				  
				  **If you do happen to secure an interview or role, forward any evidence of interviews and/or job offers:**
				- If you have any evidence of interviews or job offers - including external to School of Code - send it to us at [evidence@schoolofcode.co.uk](mailto:evidence@schoolofcode.co.uk). We have to report all positive outcomes to the DfE, in line with your Learner Agreement.
				- For **interviews** we need confirmation of:
				- Job Title
				- Interview Date
				- Company
				- (eg an email or letter from the company with the invite/a printout of your e-calendar entry showing the company contact)
				- For **job offers** (even if you don’t accept) we need details of the:
				- Job Title
				- Salary
				- Company
				- Start date
				- (Copies of offer letters/contracts are the ideal here but we can accept an email with these details from the company/you)
				  
				  ***There's strength in numbers, and we want to be there to give you that high-quality support and guidance when you really need it. By staying engaged, we can help you in your journey to achieving your career goals and building a successful future in tech. Your Careers Coach will add you to a slack channel shortly, where you can ask questions and be given more information about what's to come !
			- [[2023-03-20 Monday]] Jessica message
				- Following the slack post from Chris re our post-Launch Day SLA, I’m excited to let you know that I’ll be your Careers Coach for the rest of your job search process here at the School of Code. We’ll be having our first Recruitment Clinic this week on Wednesday at 11am, where we’ll all get together as a group - an invite is winging its way to you!
				  We know that everyone's job search journey is unique, and we want to provide tailored support to each of you. That's why we've also made office hours available, where you can book in a 1:1 session with me. Here's my calendly link: [Calendly - Jessica Roach](https://calendly.com/jessica_schoolofcode/officehours)
				  Before then, I’ll be reaching out to each of you individually to get a clear picture of where you are in your job search to inform our planning and how to best support you.
				  If you have any questions before then, feel free to reach out!
	- By Week
		- Week 0: ((6320936d-e229-43fc-9e1f-0fda6be30259))
		  id:: 63642a2c-6fb5-4667-921c-ffd38ded341b
		  collapsed:: true
			- File transfers are split into packets to increase reliability + speed
			- TCP/UDP/protocols are what controls the reordering of the received packets so the file is correct
			- UDP is used for video, real-time gaming etc and it's able to deliver streams quicker than TCP, because it can degrade and work with many dropped packets (e.g. pixelated video calls, server-side prediction for gaming)
			- `img` tags are closed differently (self-closing) e.g. 
			  ```html
			  <img src="https://google.com/image.jpg" />
			  ```
				- ^^note: my existing notes close `img` tags differently^^
					- ((632092ff-fe21-4610-815c-ab9f2fe3576f))
					  id:: 632b58e5-a46f-493e-95d9-8088d94980ee
					- ((632092fc-bec5-4603-965c-be0f6a847289))
					  id:: 632b58e8-50d1-407d-9d96-b68bcc19a05f
			- Style tags review - ((62d7c01f-4565-4e14-aaf3-4abe0957e79a))

		- Week 1: ((633b752e-536f-48e7-8ad6-b395a7afa8d6))
		  collapsed:: true
			- Week partner: Isabelle Francis, room 41
			- Day 1
			  collapsed:: true
				- Morning
					- 125k people a year tech jobs are left unfilled (worldwide?)
					  id:: e9850429-580f-47d4-9919-cf024023bf05
					- School of Code vs other paths
					  id:: 6331720c-45b7-4f4e-ab67-65ae5a7ef822
						- Paid bootcamps are comprised of mosty of students who are already programmers, they're just retraining to a different language
						- School of Code are incentivised to make us employable - because their courses are free and they're sponsored by companies/government
						- Unlike self-taught you learn teamwork and collaboration
					- Course sponsors - they hire students also
					  ![2022-09-26_10-56.png](../assets/2022-09-26_10-56_1664186187358_0.png)
					-
					- https://i.imgur.com/h8ZQKbr.png
						- Gratitude Diary - 3 things each day
						- Mistake List - journal in a table what mistake you made so you can improve
					- Pair Programming
						- A navigator and a driver
						- Helps with interviews as well as day-to-day work at a job where you'll have to explain code as you write it
					- [CSS Diner](https://flukeout.github.io/) for practicing using CSS selectors
					  id:: 63317807-bd1e-430c-bf63-985d824439f1
					  collapsed:: true
						- Type Selector `A`
						  id:: 633851f6-ffe1-420f-8dc6-6742a5eb763a
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select elements by their type
							- Selects all elements of type `A`. Type refers to the type of tag, so `<div>`, `<p>` and `<ul>` are all different element types.
							- Examples
							  collapsed:: true
								- `div` selects all `<div>` elements.
								- `p` selects all `<p>` elements.
						- ID Selector `#id`
						  id:: 63385257-ecbd-4de6-a42b-d414d91b5ec2
						  collapsed:: true
							- Selects the element with a specific `id`. You can also combine the ID selector with the type selector.
							- Examples
							  collapsed:: true
								- `#cool` selects any element with `id="cool"`
								- `ul#long` selects `<ul id="long">`
						- Descendent Selector `A  B`
						  id:: 633852d9-a42c-4d95-a507-d3107214895f
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select an element inside another element
							- Selects all B inside of A. B is called a descendant because it is inside of another element.
							- Examples
								- `p  strong` selects all `<strong>` elements that are inside of any `<p>`
								- `#fancy  span` selects any `<span>` elements that are inside of the element with `id="fancy"`
								- Combination of ((63385257-ecbd-4de6-a42b-d414d91b5ec2)) with ((633852d9-a42c-4d95-a507-d3107214895f))
								  collapsed:: true
									- `#cool span` selects all `<span>` elements that are inside of elements with `id="cool"`
						- Class Selector `.class`
						  id:: 6338541f-c4f1-4722-8ebc-54a74d89825c
						  collapsed:: true
							- The class selector selects all elements with that class attribute. Elements can only have one ID, but many classes.
							- Examples
								- `.neato` selects all elements with `class="neato"`
								- Combination of ((633851f6-ffe1-420f-8dc6-6742a5eb763a)) with ((6338541f-c4f1-4722-8ebc-54a74d89825c))
									- `ul.important` selects all `ul` elements that have `class="important"`
								- Combination of ((63385257-ecbd-4de6-a42b-d414d91b5ec2)) with ((6338541f-c4f1-4722-8ebc-54a74d89825c))
									- `#big.wide` selects all elements with `id="big"` that also have `class="wide"`
						- Comma Combinator `A, B`
						  collapsed:: true
							- This selects all A and B elements. You can combine any selectors this way, and you can specify more than two.
							- Examples
							- `p, .fun` selects all `<p>` elements as well as all elements with `class="fun"`
							- `a, p, div` selects all `<a>`, `<p>` and `<div>` elements
						- Universal Selector `*`
						  id:: 63387521-bab0-4f30-8d14-c53af609423a
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements
							- Examples
								- `*` selects all elements.
								- `p *` selects any element inside all `<p>` elements.
								- Combination of ((63387521-bab0-4f30-8d14-c53af609423a)) with ((633851f6-ffe1-420f-8dc6-6742a5eb763a)) and ((6338541f-c4f1-4722-8ebc-54a74d89825c))
									- `ul.fancy *` selects every element inside all `<ul class="fancy">` elements.
						- Adjacent Sibling Selector `A + B`
						  collapsed:: true
							- This selects all `B` elements that directly follow `A`. Elements that follow one another are called siblings. They're on the same level, or depth.
							- In the HTML markup for this level, elements that have the same indentation are siblings.
							- Examples
								- `p + .intro` selects every element with `class="intro"` that directly follows a `<p>`
								- `div + a` selects every `<a>` element that directly follows a `<div>`
						- General Sibling Selector `A ~ B`
						  collapsed:: true
							- You can select all siblings of an element that follow it. This is like the Adjacent Selector (A + B) except it gets all of the following elements instead of one.
							- Examples
								- `A ~ B` selects all `B` that follow a `A`
						- Child Selector `A > B`
						  collapsed:: true
						  id:: 634710a5-ca4f-43cc-ae61-96d0ae9b762b
							- You can select elements that are direct children of other elements. A child element is any element that is nested directly in another element.
							- Elements that are nested deeper than that are called descendant elements.
							- Examples
							- `A > B` selects all `B` that are a direct children `A`
							- Examples
						- First Child Pseudo-selector `:first-child`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select a first child element inside of another element
							- You can select the first child element. A child element is any element that is directly nested in another element. You can combine this pseudo-selector with other selectors.
							- `:first-child` selects all first child elements.
							- `p:first-child` selects all first child `<p>` elements.
							- `div p:first-child` selects all first child `<p>` elements that are in a `<div>`.
						- Only Child Pseudo-selector `:only-child`
						  collapsed:: true
							- You can select any element that is the only element inside of another one.
							- Examples
								- `span:only-child` selects the `<span>` elements that are the only child of some other element.
								- `ul li:only-child` selects the only `<li>` element that are in a `<ul>`.
						- Last Child Pseudo-selector `:last-child`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select the last element inside of another element
							- You can use this selector to select an element that is the last child element inside of another element.
							- Pro Tip → In cases where there is only one element, that element counts as the first-child, only-child and last-child!
							- Examples
								- `:last-child` selects all last-child elements.
								- `span:last-child` selects all last-child `<span>` elements.
								- `ul li:last-child` selects the last `<li>` elements inside of any `<ul>`.
						- Nth Child Pseudo-selector `:nth-child(A)`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select an element by its order in another element
							- Selects the `nth` (Ex: 1st, 3rd, 12th etc.) child element in another element.
							- Examples
								- `:nth-child(8)` selects every element that is the 8th child of another element.
								- `div p:nth-child(2)` selects the second `p` in every `div`
						- Nth Last Child Selector `:nth-last-child(A)`
						  id:: 6338883c-5378-46a8-8cb1-9912224c8e52
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select an element by its order in another element, counting from the back
							- Selects the children from the bottom of the parent. This is like nth-child, but counting from the back!
							- Examples
								- `:nth-last-child(2)` selects all second-to-last child elements.
							- Related: ((6396fb63-919c-49a7-af3e-dcdd75f2f6a1))
						- First of Type Selector `:first-of-type`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select the first element of a specific type
							- Selects the first element of that type within another element.
							- Examples
								- `span:first-of-type` selects the first `<span>` in any element.
						- Nth of Type Selector `:nth-of-type(A)`
						  collapsed:: true
						  id:: 636d5ee0-2b8b-4794-99e9-d68cf1fc26c6
							- Selects a specific element based on its type and order in another element - or even or odd instances of that element.
							- Examples
								- `div:nth-of-type(2)` selects the second instance of a div.
								- `.example:nth-of-type(odd)` selects all odd instances of a the example class.
						- Nth-of-type Selector with Formula `:nth-of-type(An+B)`
						  collapsed:: true
							- The nth-of-type formula selects every nth element, starting the count at a specific instance of that element.
							- Examples
								- `span:nth-of-type(6n+2)` selects every 6th instance of a `<span>`, starting from (and including) the second instance.
						- Only of Type Selector `:only-of-type`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select elements that are the only ones of their type within of their parent element
							- Selects the only element of its type within another element.
							- Examples
								- `p span:only-of-type` selects a `<span>` within any `<p>` if it is the only `<span>` in there.
						- Last of Type Selector `:last-of-type`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select the last element of a specific type
							- Selects each last element of that type within another element. Remember type refers the kind of tag, so p and span are different types.
							- I wonder if this is how the last dinosaur was selected before it went extinct.
							- Examples
								- `div:last-of-type` selects the last `<div>` in every element.
								- `p span:last-of-type` selects the last `<span>` in every `<p>`.
						- Empty Selector `:empty`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select elements that don't have children
							- Selects elements that don't have any other elements inside of them.
							- Examples
								- `div:empty` selects all empty `<div>` elements.
						- Negation Pseudo-class `:not(X)`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements that don't match the negation selector
							- You can use this to select all elements that do not match selector "X".
							- Examples
								- `:not(#fancy)` selects all elements that do not have `id="fancy"`.
								- `div:not(:first-child)` selects every `<div>` that is not a first child.
								- `:not(.big, .medium)` selects all elements that do not have `class="big"` or `class="medium"`.
						- Attribute Selector `[attribute]`
						  id:: 63388a77-0652-43b3-bdeb-eebaf8e76939
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements that have a specific attribute
							- Attributes appear inside the opening tag of an element, like this: `<span attribute="value">`. An attribute does not always have a value, it can be blank!
							- Examples
								- `a[href]` selects all `<a>` elements that have a `href="anything"` attribute.
								- `[type]` selects all elements that have a `type="anything"` attribute
								- Combination of another selector with ((63388a77-0652-43b3-bdeb-eebaf8e76939))
									- `[value]` selects all elements that have a `value="anything"` attribute.
									- `a[href]` selects all `<a>` elements that have a href="anything" attribute.
									- `input[disabled]` selects all `<input>` elements with the `disabled` attribute
						- Attribute Value Selector `[attribute="value"]`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements that have a specific attribute value
							- Attribute selectors are case sensitive, each character must match exactly.
							- Examples
								- `input[type="checkbox"]` selects all checkbox input elements.
						- Attribute Starts With Selector `[attribute^="value"]`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements with an attribute value that starts with specific characters
							- Examples
								- `.toy[category^="Swim"]` selects elements with class `toy` and either `category="Swimwear` or `category="Swimming"`.
						- Attribute Ends With Selector `[attribute$="value"]`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements with an attribute value that ends with specific characters
							- Examples
								- `img[src$=".jpg"]` selects all images display a `.jpg` image.
						- Attribute Wildcard Selector `[attribute*="value"]`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select all elements with an attribute value that contains specific characters anywhere
							- A useful selector if you can identify a common pattern in things like `class`, `href` or `src` attributes.
							- Examples
								- `img[src*="/thumbnails/"]` selects all image elements that show images from the "thumbnails" folder.
								- `[class*="heading"]` selects all elements with "heading" in their class, like `class="main-heading"` and `class="sub-heading"`
						- Related:
							- [[CSS]]

				- Afternoon
					- To solve a problem it should be broken down into multiple smaller steps, because
					  id:: 63315fdf-4acf-42ca-817d-6ab03c4cc362
						- It changes one hard problem into multiple easier ones
						- It's needed to identify exact what first step you can or should take
						- Oftentimes when the problem is small enough you'll be able to easily use a search engine to find a solution
					- Zoom: press SPACEBAR to temporarily unmute yourself
					- Feedback loops
						- ![2022-09-26_15-02.png](../assets/2022-09-26_15-02_1664200988433_0.png)
						- Get feedback with a balance of frequency, so not too often nor too infrequently to take advantage of it
						- Get feedback from coding partners, bosses, etc
						- Pair programming allows you to get feedback in seconds, so is very valuable compared to release plans which can take months to get feedback
					- Imposter syndrome
						- If you don't have it then you probably aren't challenging yourself
						- If you have it then you're having the same feeling as others who are pushing themselves out of their comfort zone

							- Journaling at the end of the day what worked, what didn't work, and how to improve

							- Ask for help regularly
							- Network with people if you want feedback on your code, struggling to learn something

						- Not taking advantage of all resources e.g. mentors
						- Letting other good habits degrade
						- Being proactive about learning - so either challenging yourself with more materials outside of class or catching up to revise materials

			- Day 3
			  collapsed:: true
				- Morning
					- Remember to email ((6333121d-35a6-4bbe-b130-c1759feb5d7f)) + filled out the shared Notion page
					- Google V8 JavaScript engine in Chromium was important for various reasons, such as being the base for Node.js
					- Expressions, Statements and Declarations
						- Expressions = questions we ask JavaScript and it gives us back an answer
						- Statements = a set of instructions
						- Declarations = where we define something
					- 5 Pillars of Programming
						- Operators
							- JavaScript operators
							  collapsed:: true
								- `+`
								- `-`
								- `/`
								- `*` multiply operator
								- `%`
								- `>`
								- `>=`
								- `<`
								- `<=`
								- `&&`
								- `||`
								- `!`
								- `++`
								- `=` assigment operator
								- `--`
								- `===`
								- [Learning Resources]
									- https://www.w3schools.com/js/js_assignment.asp
						- Variables and Types
							- Types
							  id:: 63e40da1-0e5b-41d5-b1d6-36c450435fed
								- number
								- string
								- boolean = true or false
								- undefined = not declared
								- null = empty
								- bigint (rarely used)
								- symbol (rarely used)
								- object
								- function
								- Related: ((afddb9fb-9d0f-456f-b505-32fee333e255))
							- Variables
								- `typeof` can check the type of a variable
									- e.g. `typeof(var)`, `typeof('string')`
								- Think of them as pointers or buckets of information
								- `let` declares a variable (a pointer)
									- e.g. `let myAge;` which declares an undefined variable
								- Reassigning variables
								  collapsed:: true
									- ```javascript
									  let myAge = 31;
									  
									  myAge = 32;
									  ```
								- Updating variables
								  collapsed:: true
									- ```javascript
									  let myAge = 31;
									  
									  myAge = myAge + 1;
									  ```
									  Which sets `myAge` as `32`
								- Assigning variables
								  collapsed:: true
									- ![Screenshot_20220928_105158.png](../assets/Screenshot_20220928_105158_1664358828462_0.png)
									- Same for using `var`
									- When you point a variable to another variable, it only sets it as their value for the moment, not permanently linked
									- Related: A005-0103. Functions being pointed to other functions sets it permanently?
										- ```javascript
										  function b() {
										    a + 1;
										  }
										  ```
										-
						- Logic Flow
							- If statements
							- Loops
						- Loops
							- `while` loops
							  id:: 63342535-face-4c2c-975e-b3811f5b0f10
								- ```javascript
								  while (condition) {
								    // code block;
								  }
								  ```
								- `while` will do an infinite loop
									- ```javascript
									  while (true) {
									    console.log('hi');
									  }
									  ```
								- Example
									- ```javascript
									  let count = 0;
									  while (count < 10) {
									    console.log('hi');
									    count = count++;
									  }
									  ```
								- `setTimeout()` native function can be used to set a delay
							- Note the below are all the same
								- ```javascript
								  count = count + 1
								  count += 1
								  count++
								  ```
							- `for` loops
								- Unlike ((63342535-face-4c2c-975e-b3811f5b0f10)) it stores the conditions for looping all on one line
								-
						- ^^Need to update Notion shared sheet^^
						- `<script src="main.js"></script>` can be used to link external JS files to the HTML file
							- `<script src="https://github.com/something.js"></script>` for external JS files
							- A good way to test if the file is linked properly is to put `console.log("HELLO WORLD!");` in the JS file, then refresh the page and check the console
						- Functions
							- Native functions
							  collapsed:: true
								- `alert`
								- `prompt`
									- Can be used within variable declaration so that whatever you write in the text box prompt will be stored
								- `confirm`
									- If you press confirm it'll be stored as true
								- ![Screenshot_20220928_110849.png](../assets/Screenshot_20220928_110849_1664360158367_0.png)
								- `prompt` stores values as a string. Your can use the native function `Number()` to change the type to number
									- ```javascript
									  let input = prompt('What is your age?');
									  let yourAge = Number(input); // This will convert `input` to a number type, because prompt stores the value as a string
									  console.log(typeof input);
									  ```
							- Function declaration is when you create the function. Starts with `function`
							- Function invocation is when you call the function
							- If you leave an argument empty, it returns `undefined`
								- id:: 63344748-4f0e-4a56-b2ec-f399156912c7
								  ```javascript
								  function greet(name) {
								    console.log('hello ' + name);
								  }
								  ```
								  Will show `'hello undefined'`
							- How to check what type something is
								- ```javascript
								  function greet(name) {
								    if(typeof name === 'string) {
								      console.log('string');
								    else {
								      console.log('ERROR');
								    }
								  }
								  ```
							- Remember to add `return` if you want to run something
								- ```javascript
								  function add(x, y) {
								    return x + y;
								  }
								  ```
								- `return` is needed so that the results are saved and thus accessible to other functions, variables, etc
							- Alternative ways of writing the above
								- ```javascript
								  const add = (x, y) => {
								    return x + y;
								  }
								  ```
								- Fat arrow function implicit return
								  id:: 634fd63d-5c76-475b-a55f-5aca80b10559
									- ```javascript
									  const add = (x, y) => x + y;
									  ```
									- Examples
										- ```javascript
										  app.get('/', (req, res) => { // the request asks for the / endpoint, rather than for example /about-me
										  	res.send('Hello World!')
										  })
										  
										  // Non fat arrow
										  app.get('/', function(req, res) {
										  	res.send('Hello World!')
										  })
										  ```
										- app.listen(port, function() { // enables web server to listen for client requests
										  	console.log(`Example app listening on port ${port}`)
										  })
										  
										  // Non fat arrow
										  app.listen(port, () => { // enables web server to listen for client requests
										  	console.log(`Example app listening on port ${port}`)
										  })
									- Related: ((636d5df2-5117-4f9e-8113-83bbb5eff6c1))
							- `return` ONLY works if the top-level is a function. It can be inside a loop or if statement or whatever inside of a function, but not in an if statement/loop/etc on it's own

				- Mindset coach
				  collapsed:: true
					- ![The Feedback Fallacy.pdf](../assets/The_Feedback_Fallacy_1664440392762_0.pdf)
						- ((6335585d-fb3b-4c10-9cc9-e482c2a29bb2))
						- ((63355881-3be6-47ed-951c-bd7988f362f3))
						- ((63355894-d707-4b24-8e87-bceaac6f69e8))
							- Source of truth
								- ((633558a3-81e3-41b3-b3ab-c62eb6fc10ef))
									- Wrong - because you're the source of a different perspective, not an objective truth
							- Theory of learning
								- ((633558cc-71a9-47cb-8a4d-7289c88fa459))
							- Theory of excellence
								- ((63355913-45c1-4a2f-a32d-ff98a51286b4))
					- Seeking and Receiving Feedback
						- People tend to find it difficult to give it due to not wanting to offend, and it's difficult to receive because of ego
						- Best mindset to go into a discussion is "Here's why I think I'm right, but I'm probably missing something. Can you help me to see what that might be?" instead of "I am right"
						- "Thanks for your feedback, here's why you're wrong" - avoid this
						- It boils down to staying curious, wanting to find out if there are any gaps in your understanding
						- LAER model - Listen > Acknowledge > Explore > Respond. Also Explore can skip the Respond stage for several cycles before actually giving a full Response
						- Personal guidelines for seeking and receiving feedback
							- Note down Pros/Cons for my approach before receiving feedback to open myself to criticism
							- Note down their feedback as Cons to my approach, but feel free to point out if a particular Pro counterbalances it or if there is a limitation to their Cons
							- Listen and be present in the moment, rather than stuck in analysis
							- Record feedback either in real-time with video or audio, or immediately after by noting it down
							- Review feedback on a regular basis until it becomes habitual
						- Personal development: what I want to get feedback on from others
					- Giving Feedback
						- Guidelines
							- Aim to assist - come in with the motivation to help the person
								- If you are promoting a culture of radical honesty, you have to get rid of the jerks on your team. Because they'll likely stab their colleagues in the front and then just say "I was just being honest"
								- Accept if they choose to reject your gift of feedback
							- Actionable advice
							- Appreciate
							- Accept or discard - be fine with them not accepting it
							- Act - it's a gift, they can choose what they want to do with it
						- Personal principles for giving feedback
							- Be outcome independent - if they choose to not take the advice on board then that's fine
							- Positive, warm and friendly approach
							- Sandwich method with positive, then negative/neutral, then positive feedback. Has to be genuine otherwise they know you're just leading up to a negative
					- Personal guidelines for a team meeting
						- Before
							- Note down Pros/Cons in advance
							- Know who the person is advance ideally so you're prepared to hear from them
							- Know the agenda in advance, that you are going to get feedback rather than by blindsided by it
						- During
							- Balance out positive and negative feedback if it's genuine (doesn't have to be 50:50)
							- Read the room, see how people are reacting
							- If there's multiple people giving feedback then try to to have multiple people receive feedback, so they don't feel singled out. Not relevant in 1:1
						- After
							-
				- freeCodeCamp + CodeAcademy recommended sources for fast learning
				- Recap fast-forward

					- Declaring Variables
						- `let`
						  id:: 63356894-3964-4b65-b307-8146a5f058f4
						  collapsed:: true
							- Relatively new
							- Predictable, `let` variables inside of functions can't be accessed outside of it
						- `const` variable
						  id:: 63356897-365d-4ecd-a48d-05a66c8afda6
						  collapsed:: true
							- Relatively new
							- You can't reassign it
							- Example - `myAge` will throw an error here
							  collapsed:: true
								- ```javascript
								  const myName = 'chris';
								  
								  myName = 'bob';
								  ```

						- `var`
						  id:: 6335689e-8de6-4cf2-ba84-29f008f811da
						  collapsed:: true
							- Old way
							- Unlike ((63356894-3964-4b65-b307-8146a5f058f4)) it ignores scope. `var`s inside of functions can be accessed outside of it
						- Block-scoped vs function-scoped
						  collapsed:: true
							- Difference between ((63356894-3964-4b65-b307-8146a5f058f4)) and ((6335689e-8de6-4cf2-ba84-29f008f811da))

					  id:: 63356c8e-baa4-4742-820f-dd86833f2c01
					  collapsed:: true
				- Objects
				  collapsed:: true
					- Properties
						- Example
							- ```javascript
							  { name: "Chris", age: 25, favouriteFood: "all of it" }`
							  ```
							- Stored in a variable
							  ```javascript
							  let person1 = { 
							    name: "Chris", age: 25, favouriteFood: "all of it" 
							  }
							  ```
						- Key/value pairs
							- Key is a string
							- Key points to a value, which can be anything (including another object)
						- Calling it
							- Example1
								- ```javascript
								  let person1 = { 
								    name: "Chris", age: 25, favouriteFood: "all of it" 
								  }
								  ```
								  
								  Call it with this expression
								  ```javascript
								  person1.favouriteFood
								  ```
								  
								  Returns:
								  ```javascript
								  "all of it"
								  ```
							- These two are interchangeable. The latter is called "bracket notation"
								- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664446429772_0.png)
						- Setting a key/value pair
							- `name` is a property with the value `"Chris"`
							  ```javascript
							  let person1 = { 
							    name: "Chris", age: 25, favouriteFood: "all of it" 
							  }
							  ```
						- Created nested objects, then calling values within them or reassigning them
						- `{} === {}` is false
							- Can't use ((63357756-dd26-4e83-8229-5176dd293241)) between objects because they're pointing to different objects in memory even if all their values are the same (unlike primitives)
						- If you set ((63356897-365d-4ecd-a48d-05a66c8afda6)) as an object, you can still reassign values inside it. It just means you can't point it to a different object or primitive
					- Exercises:
						- Add a new property and value to an object
						- Add a new property that contains an object with multiple properties
						- Reassign a value within a nested object
						- ((63357756-dd26-4e83-8229-5176dd293241)) between objects
						- Use `const` as variable for object and reassigning values
				- ((63209272-1088-4824-a762-4ac7ded04b0a)) {{embed ((633a1738-6a4c-414b-bfda-1e537b0ba941))}}
				- Arrays
				  id:: 633591d9-aed0-4848-8c27-f0202fb15239
				  collapsed:: true
					- `typeof` is an operator, so we can write it like `typeof (varName);`
					- Arrays are objects, but with some extra functionality for lists
						- It also loses some functionality like dot notation (but it keeps bracket notation, though it's not a string)
					- `typeof (varName);` shows it as an object. Instead need to use `Array.isArray`
						- Example
						  ```javascript
						  let varName = [1, 2, 3];
						  
						  Array.isArray(varName);
						  ```
					- Arrays should try to keep items all of the same type. If it's mixed, use an object instead since there is less confusion that way as each item is labelled with a property name
					- Accessing an array of objects
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664457343207_0.png)
					- Loop over the items of an array
						- Example
							- Multiplying the variable by 10
							  ```javascript
							  const numbers = [100, 352, 800, 400];
							  
							  for (let count = 0; count < numbers.length; count++) {
							    numbers[count] = numbers[count] * 10
							    console.log(numbers[count]);
							  }
							  ```
					- `find` and `findLast` are useful methods for finding a specific item in an array

					- `Math.random()` can be used to produce a random number between `0` and `1` e.g. `0.12341253`
						- `Math.floor(Math.random())` can be used to produce a random integer
							- `Math.floor(Math.random() * 3) ;` will produce an integer between `0` and `2`

						- Highlight a line and do CTRL + D. Holding `CTRL`, then tap `D` several more times to highlight adjacent lines. Then type in whatever to change those simultaneously

					- Alphabet War
						- ```javascript
						  function alphabetWar(fight) {
						    return "Let's fight again!";
						  }
						  ```
						- 1) Read the question fully before starting
						- 2) Make a plan before starting. The plan should be good enough that you can delegate it and trust they'd be able to do it correctly
						  collapsed:: true
							- ```javascript
							  // PLAN:
							  // Arguments: Take in a string of lowercase letters called fight
							  
							  // Set up right score = 0;
							  // Set up left score = 0;
							  
							  // Have a dictionary of letters -> values
							  
							  // Loop through the string
							    // Check the dictionary for the letter
							    // If there isn't a value, move on
							    // If there is a value, if it is right team, add to the right score
							    // If there is a value, if it is left team, add to the left score
							  
							  // Add the right and left hand values separately
							  // Compare them at the end to see who wins
							    // if left > right 
							      // return left side wins!
							    // if right > left
							      // return right side wins!
							    // else 
							      // return let's fight again
							  ```
					- [CodeWars walkthroughs](https://vimeopro.com/schoolofcode/codewars) - password: `bootcamp`
					- Modulus/Remainder operator `%` - good for finding out if a number is odd or even
				- Document Object Model (DOM)
				  id:: 6341c13e-9cba-4756-9b60-3b84063c1061
				  collapsed:: true
					- Related: ((175b1085-fd7e-479c-934f-eabc5f88e45b))
					- Manipulating DOM doesn't modify the underlying HTML elements
					- DOM is constructed as a tree of objects e.g. `document.html.body.script`
					- `console.dir(document)` shows all properties for `document` e.g. `document.title` will show the webpage title
					- DOM selectors
						- CSS selectors can also be used to easily select HTML elements
						- `document.querySelector`
						  collapsed:: true
							- It uses strings for the query e.g.
							  ```javascript
							  document.querySelector('#main-heading')
							  ```
								- This will select elements with `id='main-heading'`
							- Instead of having to write `document.querySelector` repeatedly, we can assign the selected element to a variable, e.g.
							  ```javascript
							  let mainHeading = document.querySelector('#main-heading')
							  ```
							- HTML elements are objects with many properties natively e.g. `textContent`
								- Example
									- This will change all elements in the variable `items` to have the string text
									  ```javascript
									  for (let i = 0; i < items.length; i++) {
									    items[i].textContent = 'this was with a for loop#'
									  }
									  ```
							- `console.dir` can be used to show these properties e.g. 
							  ```javascript
							  console.dir(document.querySelector('#main-heading'));
							  ```
							- This selector is newer and works for any type of element. Replaces `document.getElementById` and `document.getElementByClass`
							- `querySelectorAll` will return all elements that match the query. `querySelector` will only return the first matching element
					- It's *convention* to use `i` as the variable name in for loops, because it stands for index
					- You can't select `innerText` of say all H2 elements. You have to do a loop over every element that is a H2. H2 itself doesn't have the property innerText
						- console.dir(document.querySelectorAll('h2')); doesn't have innerText
						- console.dir(document.querySelector('h2')); does have, because you're selecting a single one
					- `style` property of elements can be used to manipulate the CSS of elements
						- Warning: it uses camel case in JavaScript whereas kebab case in CSS for the same properties. This is because it'll otherwise see it as a `-` operator
					- `document.createElement()` is a method we can use to make new elements
						- Example
							- ```javascript
							  let newParagraph = document.createElement('p') 
							  ```
							  
							  `newParagraph` is equal to:
							  ```javascript
							  <p></p>
							  ```
						- In order to make it visible on the page, we need to append it to the `document` using [appendChild](((633ae1df-5a8b-47ae-b59c-f9a3babbff92)))
						- `appendChild` can be used to add new elements onto the document at the bottom of the selector
						  id:: 633ae1df-5a8b-47ae-b59c-f9a3babbff92
							- Example
							  ```javascript
							  document.body.appendChild(newParagraph)
							  ```
						- `removeChild` can be used to remove an element that exists in the selector
							- Example
							  ```javascript
							  document.body.removeChild(newParagraph)
							  ```
					- ((6342ef4e-a9f4-4123-91da-601a76ee2f25))
					- String interpolation more examples
						- ```javascript
						  let pennies = 1;
						  const output = document.querySelector("output");
						  
						  function handleClick() {
						    console.log("I'm just to prove it's working!");
						    // Double the number of pennies
						    pennies = pennies * 2;
						    // display in the output tag
						    output.textContent = `${pennies} pennies`
						  }
						  ```
						  prints
						  ```javascript
						  '2 pennies'
						  ```
					- `value` is the attribute on input `type='text' which holds the user-inputted text
					- instead of `.addEventListener` in JavaScript you can use the HTML attribute `onclick='handleClick()'` for example
					- `createElement` and `appendChild`
						- Examples
							- ```javascript
							  let body = document.querySelector('body');
							  let button1 = document.createElement('button');
							  button1.textContent = 'Click for cat';
							  body.appendChild(button1);
							  ```
			- Day 2
			  collapsed:: true
				- [[CodeWars - JavaScript]]
				  id:: 633b752e-0d41-47a6-b28e-34ddd75ff555
				- Event Listeners
				  id:: 633bf5fd-e89b-4b5a-b92d-16210350ae21
				  collapsed:: true
					- Related: ((175b1085-fd7e-479c-934f-eabc5f88e45b))
					- Typical structure is a function which takes an event as an argument, e.g:
					  ```javascript
					  function handleMessage(event) // `event` is an object 
					  ```
					- Can create event listeners either in HTML or JavaScript:
						- Event listener in JavaScript
							- Example
								- ```javascript
								  bootcamper.addEventListener('message', handleMessage);
								  ```
								- ```javascript
								  bootcamper.addEventListener('click', handleClick);
								  ```
							- Pros
								- Separation of concerns - makes most sense to have JavaScript only in JS file, rather than also in HTML
						- Event listener in HTML
						  ```html
						  <bootcamper onmessage='handleMessage()'/>Billy Bootcamper</bootcamper>
						  ```
					- Example usage
						- ```javascript
						  let h1 = document.querySelector('#road-heading'); // selecting a specific H1 element
						  
						  button.addEventListener('click', handleClick); // event listener which triggers when button is clicked
						  
						  function handleClick(e) { // the parameter is the object created when the event listener is triggered. Convention is `e` or `event`
						    console.log(e); // console.log the the event object
						    h1.textContent = 'ROARRRR'; // change the H1 text to show that the event has triggered
						  }
						  ```
						- How to select an event listener on an input textbox element
						  ```javascript
						  console.log(e.target.value)
						  ```
					- We were stuck for ages trying to do 
					  ```javascript
					  document.querySelector('h1') = event.target.value
					  ```
					  When it should really be
					  ```javascript
					  document.querySelector('h1').textContent = event.target.value;
					  ```
					- Related: ((632092fe-45c6-48af-965e-ca89cd23b482))
				- interactions are through elements in the HTML/on the DOM rather than via confirm, alert, and prompt.
					- [`confirm()`](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/API/Window/confirm)
				- Note: if doing a selector like `document.querySelector()` then you have to finish the line with that function. Can't do something like 
				  ```javascript
				  document.querySelector().style.backgroundColor
				  ```
				- [https://frontendmasters.com/courses/deep-javascript-v3/](https://frontendmasters.com/courses/deep-javascript-v3/) A good supplement video based course to fill in the blanks of your knowledge. Frontend Masters is free for 6 months when using your GitHub student pack.
			- Day 3
			  collapsed:: true
				- `event.target`is the property on the `event` object that contains the DOM element on which the event was dispatched (where the event happened)
				  id:: 633d3b76-e928-42b4-a40a-926edc25dfd8
				- Asynchronous Code
				  collapsed:: true
					- Native functions for async code
						- `setTimeout()`
						  id:: 633d48ae-95c5-40da-8539-b838740a1524
						  collapsed:: true
							- Code runs after a set period of time
							- Example
								- This will console.log `Hello,` `You're looking for?` <3-SECONDS> `Is it me`
								  ```javascript
								  console.log('Hello');
								  
								  setTimeout(function () {
								  	console.log('Is it me');
								  }, 3000);
								  
								  console.log('You\'re looking for?');
								  ```
						- `setInterval()`
						  id:: 633d4907-7bb0-4e0e-858c-d4ebf99427d1
						  collapsed:: true
							- Code runs repeatedly after a specific amount of time (no need for a separate loop)
							- Template
							  id:: 6342e6bc-6391-4db8-8410-b2b6d12115fe
								- ```javascript
								   setInterval(<function-name>, <milliseconds>)
								  ```
							- Example
								- ```javascript
								  const hand = document.querySelector("#hand");
								  
								  function tick() {
								      console.log('ticked');
								  }
								  
								  setInterval(tick, 1000);
								  ```
							- `clearInterval()` can be used to stop a ((633d4907-7bb0-4e0e-858c-d4ebf99427d1))
								- Template
									- ```javascript
									  let myInterval = setInterval(<function-name>, <milliseconds>)
									  
									  clearInterval(<function-name>);
									  ```
								- Examples
									- Creating a variable for `setInterval` allows us to put the `clearInterval` within the `setInterval function`
										- ```javascript
										  let counter = 0;
										  
										  let myInterval = setInterval(function() {
										    console.log(counter);
										    counter--;
										    if (counter === 0) {
										      console.log("Time\'s up!");
										      clearInterval(myInterval);
										    }
										  }, 1000);
										  ```
										- ```javascript
										  let counter = document.querySelector('#count');
										  
										  let countChecker = function incrementCount() {
										      if (counter.textContent == 12) {
										          clearInterval(countChecker);
										      } else {
										          counter.textContent++;
										      }
										  }
										  
										  setInterval(countChecker, 1000);
										  ```
									- ```javascript
									  let intervalId = setInterval(incrementCount, 1000);
									  
									  clearInterval(intervalId);
									  ```
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664963834376_0.png)
									- Note: when using `addEventListener` method, if you use parentheses on the function then it'll execute it immediately on page load
										- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664964126897_0.png)
											- Instead you should create a function, so that can be called instead
											  ![image.png](../assets/image_1664964294304_0.png)
					- `console.time();` sets a timer. Useful for debugging
						- `console.timeEnd()` will return the value of the timer
					- `1000` =  1000 milliseconds = 1 second
					- At it's base, JavaScript is a synchronous language. However there are means to do async code
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664961373316_0.png)
						- ((633d4907-7bb0-4e0e-858c-d4ebf99427d1)) adds functions to a separate queue called the `Callback queue`. Separate from normal `Call stack`, which is used for normal JavaScript functions
						- When the timer is ready, it'll add the function back into the `Call stack`
					- ((633d48ae-95c5-40da-8539-b838740a1524)) even of `0` will still cause a small delay e.g. 0.02ms
					  collapsed:: true
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1664962179213_0.png)
					- ```javascript
					  console.log('Hello,');
					  let response;
					  setTimeout(function () {
					    response = prompt('Is it me');
					  }, 3000);
					  console.log(response);
					  console.log('You\'re looking for?');
					  ```
						- This will return the prompt after 3 seconds, after everything else has executed
					- You can alternatively define the function in-line, rather than creating an anonymous function
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```javascript

					- Fetch API
					  id:: 6341c13e-501b-4f83-b935-37520a9c31d4
					  collapsed:: true
						- Related: ((20601e62-d46b-40e1-af45-f64e727d2757))
						- Usage
							- Basic usage
								- ```javascript
								  fetch("https://api.kanye.rest");
								  ```
							- Always **must** add these parts:
								- Add`await`to make the request asynchronous, because the API returns a Promise (this is a response object) and we're not sure how long the API will take to respond
								  ```javascript
								  await fetch("https://api.kanye.rest");
								  ```
									- Promise response object includes the properties `Prototype`, `PromiseState`, `PromiseResult`
								- Wrap it all in an `async function`, because `await` is only allowed to be used within async functions
								  ```javascript
								  async function getQuote() {
								    let response = await fetch("https://api.kanye.rest");
								    console.log(response);
								  }
								  ```
								- All later expressions that reference the response object must also use `await` because we don't know how long it'll take the API to respond. This is the **working version**
									- ```javascript
									  async function getQuote() {
									    // awaits promise from fetch and gets response object
									    let response = await fetch("https://api.kanye.rest");
									    // get the JSON out of the response object - data!
									    let data = await response.json();
									    console.log(data);
									  }
									  
									  getQuote();
									  ```
								- `.json()` function
							- Using `fetch` values in other functions
							  id:: 63503849-47d8-4851-afc5-abdc758eb0d6
								- Example
									- ```javascript
									  async function fetchQuestions() {
									    let response = await fetch('https://api.thecatapi.com/v1/images/search');
									    let jsonData = await response.json();
									    return jsonData;
									  }
									  
									  async function printQuestions() {
									    let questions = await fetchQuestions();
									    console.log(questions.results);
									  }
									  
									  printQuestions();
									  ```
							- Examples
								- Setting an image to a random cat
								  ```javascript
								  let image = document.querySelector('#cat-here');
								  
								  async function getCat() {
								      let catFetch = await fetch('https://api.thecatapi.com/v1/images/search');
								      let catJson = await catFetch.json();
								      image.setAttribute('src', catJson[0].url);
								  }
								  
								  getCat();
								  ```
								- Fetching a dad joke
								  ```javascript
								  export async function fetchDadJoke() { 
								    const url = "[icanhazdadjoke](https://icanhazdadjoke.com/";) 
								    const response = await fetch(url, { 
								      headers: { 
								        Accept: "application/json", 
								      }, 
								    }); 
								    const data = await response.json(); 
								    return data.joke;
								  }
								  
								  console. log("this is the dad joke", await fetchDadJoke());
								  ```

								- Information (Observant : Intuitive)
								  id:: 633e94c5-578c-487b-9307-a653f2b7f40b
								  collapsed:: true
									- This trait shows where we direct our mental energy.
									- Observant AKA Sensing
									- Observant - want to see all the detailed concrete steps
										- Pros
											- Consistent results
										- Weakness
											- They can't do well without having steps/tasks to do
											- Lack of ability to improvise
									- Intuitive - focus on big picture, concepts, experiences
										- Pros
											- They can create meals with a lot of enjoyable variety
										- Weakness
											- Poor at giving instructions
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665045905540_0.png)

								- Planning (Judging : Prospecting)
								  id:: 633e9559-afb9-40f1-85ce-dfb06e806ff5
								  collapsed:: true
									- This trait reflects our approach to work, planning and decision-making.
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665047141947_0.png)
									- e.g. deciding what show to watch, what to eat tonight
									- Judger
										- Pros
											- Good planning for deadlines, plan structure
										- Cons
											- Can find it difficult to adapt to new ideas/events/etc if it may require changes in the existing plan
											- Might be too rigid
									- Perceiver
										- Pros
											- Might be better in a crisis. However judgers might already have a plan for every crisis, or can plan their way out of a crisis
											- Good at brainstorming
										- Cons
											- May get distracted

				- Navigating data
					- All items of an array have an `index` property, useful for finding what number they are in the array
					- ((63679853-bf0c-44a1-9fba-087790dbcc45))
					  id:: 633ea3e7-92cc-42c7-b4c7-fd5ac68a6041
					  collapsed:: true
						- Example - also uses fat arrows
							- ```javascript
							  function marieDavid2ndFriend() {
							    let people = SOCBook.data.people; // this is an array that contains separate objects for each person
							    let marieDavid = people.find(o => o.name === 'Marie David');
							    return marieDavid.name;
							  }
							  ```
							- Can alternatively be written:
							  ```javascript
							  function marieDavid2ndFriend() {
							    let people = SOCBook.data.people; // this is an array that contains separate objects for each person
							    let marieDavid = people.find(o) { // native find() locate object for the person
							    	o.name === 'Marie David');
							    }
							    return marieDavid.name;
							  }
							  ```
						- https://stackoverflow.com/questions/12462318/find-a-value-in-an-array-of-objects-in-javascript
						- `o` is the parameter (which is `obj`)
						- ```javascript
						  function testName(o) {
						  	o.name === 'Marie David'
						  }
						  ```
						- ```javascript
						  () => {}
						  ```
				- Research and Presentation task
					- 4pm presentation, 4 mins | everyone has a speaking role
					- Topic: **Accessibility**
					  collapsed:: true
						- https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Learn/Accessibility/What_is_accessibility
							- Accessibility is the practice of making your website usable by as many people as possible e.g. those with slow internet, mobile devices, visually impaired people, etc
							- This is a legal requirement in some places
							- Semantic HTML, which improves accessibility, also improves SEO, making your site more findable.
							- Categories of disability
								- People with visual impairments
								- People with hearing impairments
									- Textual alternatives to pictures or video
									- Captions for videos
									- Text simplification as well so it doesn't require high education
								- People with mobility impairments
									- Solutions
										- Keyboard accessibility to navigate websites
								- People with cognitive impairments
					- Talk
					  collapsed:: true
						- 1) What is accessibility + why is it important
						- 2) Impairment 1 - Hearing
						- 3) Impairment 2 - Mobility (my topic)
							- Mobility is another common form of impairment that can make it difficult to use software **(next slide please)**
							- and the first type of this that developers commonly target is mild mobility impairment
							  id:: 633ed6bc-25ce-4249-a561-0edef11a506c
								- Which,  in this context means the inability to make precise mouse movements
								- the standard solution for this is keyboard accessibility
								- Many programs implement keyboard shortcuts for various actions, such as Visual Studio Code
								- Better examples of these programs also allow you to customise these keyboard shortcuts
								- And the best of these programs allow for keyboard control of everything, such as the infamous text editors emacs and vim
							- **(next slide please)** The second type of mobility impairment is severe
								- This might be loss of limbs or paralysis
								- In these cases a head pointer is commonly used, which allows tapping on screens or keyboard by just moving your head
								- Developers can keep these types of people in mind by taking into account that
									- people may only be able to type a few words per minute,
									- ~~to not require too many keys to be used in combination for various shortcuts~~
									- and to allow customising their keyboard shortcuts
								- **No time for: **
									- Very severe e.g. Stephen Hawkings
										- In 2014 Intel and SwiftKey partnered to produce an open-source software package called [Assistive Context Aware Toolkit (ACAT)](https://github.com/intel/acat/releases)
										- Previously Stephen was using 20-year old software called EZ Keys. An infrared sensor mounted to his glasses detected small pulses of movement in his cheek

				- Learning from other talks
				  collapsed:: true
					- map() and set() - new data structures
					  collapsed:: true
						- map() contains an ordered list of key/value pairs
						- set()
						- keys must be unique
						- Can be useful for personal profiles for example
						- map.size is a useful property that objects don't have
						- objects can only have strings as data type, maps can have any data type as the key to a value
					- Responsive Web Design and CSS
					  collapsed:: true
						- flexible boxes
						- CSS units
					- Extra promises types
					  collapsed:: true
						- Promise.all - if even one is rejected then it will be unfulfilled
						- Promise.race - race between promises, will settle as soon as the first promise is fulfilled
						- Promise.any() -
					- Technical Committee 39 (TC39) governs ECMAScript
					- Obscure HTML tags
					  collapsed:: true
						- `<iframe>` allows creating a document within a document e.g. YouTube videos, Airtable embeds
						- `<svg>` for scalable vector graphics. Can have a lot of attributes for modifying it
						- `<canvas>` is a rectangular area used for drawing graphics in real-time via scripting
						- `<progress>` represents completion of a task. Useful for showing the user journey
					- Other loops in JS
					  collapsed:: true
						- Do ...While loops
						  ```javascript
						  do { // it'll do this at least once
						    something
						  } while (something) 
						  ```
						- For ...in loop
						  ```javascript
						  for (let i in letters) {
						    console.log(letters[i]);
						  }
						  ```
							- Shorter but less flexible
						- For ...of loop
						  ```javascript
						  for (let x to cars) {
						    text += cars[x];
						  }
						  ```
					- Dev tools tabs we haven't used yet
					  collapsed:: true
						- Recorder - records all user actions in this session, useful for checking performance
						- Performance tab - shows how long it take for various resources to load, in terms of network, CPU and memory usage
						- Security tab - tells you about HTTPS
					- CSS animations
						- Transitions - used to make elements move. Used for simple movements
						- Animations - elements can gradually change from one style to another. Requires at least two keyframes which hold what styles the element has at certain times

					- How might you write this If statement more succinctly?
					  id:: 63452279-b225-492b-a205-b9d7c6305edf
					  collapsed:: true
						- Related: ((6347b540-6c5c-4871-b4b7-cbebe82269ec))
						- ```javascript
						  let randomArray = ['a', 'b', 'c'];
						  
						  if (randomArray[3] === 'a' ||
						  	randomArray[6] === 'e' ||
						      randomArray[13] === 'i' ||
						      randomArray[21] === 'o' ||
						      randomArray[36] === 'U') {
						  		console.log('true');
						  	}
						  ```
							- Solutions
								- Using a for loop to go over the random array and use a nested for loop to check if it is equal to another array with the vowels in. In terms of if statements you could maybe look into a switch statement, same logic just sometimes easier to read
									- ```javascript
									  let randomArray = ['a', 'b', 'c'];
									  let vowels = ['a', 'e', 'i', 'o', 'u'];
									  let matchArray = [];
									  
									  for (let i = 0; i < randomArray.length; i++) {
									  	for (let j = 0; j < vowels.length; j++) {
									        	if (randomArray[i] === vowels[j]) {
									          	matchArray.push('match');
									          }
									      }
									  }
									  if (matchArray[3] || matchArray[6] || matchArray[13] || matchArray[21] || matchArray[36] === 'match') {
									  	console.log('true');
									  }
									  ```
								- you could also look into [RegExp](https://www.w3schools.com/jsref/jsref_obj_regexp.asp) & [test()](https://www.w3schools.com/jsref/jsref_regexp_test.asp). It took me a bit of time to wrap my head around but it means you can test strings really succinctly.
									- ((6345ae08-b3a7-40fb-bd6a-35c0918159e5))
									- ((85fecdc2-4e32-4a5d-a102-51e4adb6069c))
									- Example
									  collapsed:: true
										- WIP
										  ```javascript
										  let randomArray = ['a', 'b', 'c'];
										  let vowels = "aeiou";
										  let pattern = /e/;
										  let result = 
										      
										  for (let i = 0; i < randomArray.length; i++) {
										  
										  	vowels[i]
										  }
										  
										  for (let j = 0; j < vowels.length; j++) { // loop over the vowels string
										  pattern = `/`
										    
										      if (randomArray[i].includes(vowels[j])) { // check each array item to see if it contains a vowel
										  		console.log(`${vowels[j]} is a match at index ${[i]}`);
										  		matchArray.push(true);
										  
										  if (randomArray[3] === 'a' ||
										  	randomArray[6] === 'e' ||
										      randomArray[13] === 'i' ||
										      randomArray[21] === 'o' ||
										      randomArray[36] === 'U') {
										  		console.log('true');
										  }
										  ```
										- ```javascript
										  let text = "The best things in life are free"; let pattern = /e/;
										  let result = pattern.test(text);
										  
										  pattern.source
										  ```
								- Cos Regex does my head in...
								  collapsed:: true
								  You can use the methods 'includes' and 'charAt' to check a string, eg. let vowels = "AEIOU"
									- ((d0a968a6-4f22-48e4-ae07-45629e7372db))
									  collapsed:: true
										- {{embed ((d0a968a6-4f22-48e4-ae07-45629e7372db))}}
									- ((adf5843f-68dc-4059-9634-3059f69a6635))
									  collapsed:: true
										- {{embed ((adf5843f-68dc-4059-9634-3059f69a6635))}}
									- ((6345ae08-f477-4d93-8a9e-e1825a4dbf30))
									- Example
									  id:: 6345af41-073a-483d-9722-9f210bbefee1
										- ```javascript
										  let randomArray = ['a', 'b', 'c', 'd', 'e'];
										  let vowels = "aeiou";
										  let matchArray = [];
										  
										  for (let i = 0; i < randomArray.length; i++) { // loop over the array
										      for (let j = 0; j < vowels.length; j++) { // loop over the vowels string
										          if (randomArray[i].includes(vowels[j])) { // check each array item to see if it contains a vowel
										  			console.log(`${vowels[j]} is a match at index ${[i]}`);
										  			matchArray.push(true);
										          }
										      }
										  }
										  if (matchArray[3] && matchArray[6] && matchArray[13] && matchArray[21] && matchArray[36] === true) {
										  	console.log('true')
										  }
										  ```
						- Example2
							- ```javascript
							  if (dna[i] === "G" || dna[i] === "C" || dna[i] === "A") {
							  	rna = `${rna}${dna[i]}`;
							  ```
								- Alternatives
									- ```javascript
									  switch (dna[i]) {
									    case 'G':
									      rna = `${rna}${dna[i]}`;
									      break;
									    case 'C':
									      rna = `${rna}${dna[i]}`;
									      break;
									    case 'A':
									      rna = `${rna}${dna[i]}`;
									      break;
									  }
									  ```
										- ((6343d976-fbe8-45fe-bc74-1fe1b5309a59))

				- Agile project management / software development
				  id:: 646349f7-6566-4e93-8cef-e371ac4e6816
				  collapsed:: true
					- Agile is an iterative approach to project management and software development that helps teams deliver value to their customers faster and with fewer headaches. Instead of betting everything on a "big bang "launch, an agile team delivers work in small, but consumable, increments. Requirements, plans, and results are evaluated continuously so teams have a natural mechanism for responding to change quickly.
					- 4 core values
						- Individuals and interactions over processes and tools
						- Working software over comprehensive documentation
						- Customer collaboration over contract negotation
						- Responding to change over following a plan
					- The 12 Principles of the Agile Manifesto
						- The highest priority is to satisfy the customer through early and continuous delivery of valuable software.
						- The most efficient and effective method of conveying information to and within a development team is face-to-face conversation.
						- At regular intervals, the team reflects on how to become more effective, then tunes and adjusts its behavior accordingly.
						- ...
						- 1) Our highest priority is to satisfy the customer through early and continuous delivery of valuable software.
						- 2) Welcome changing requirements, even late in development. Agile processes harness change for the customer’s competitive advantage.
						- 3) Deliver working software frequently, from a couple of weeks to a couple of months, with a preference to the shorter timescale.
						- 4) Business people and developers must work together daily throughout the project.
						- 5) Build projects around motivated individuals. Give them the environment and support they need, and trust them to get the job done.
						- 6) The most efficient and effective method of conveying information to and within a development team is face-to-face conversation.
						- 7) Working software is the primary measure of progress.
						- 8) Agile processes promote sustainable development. The sponsors, developers, and users should be able to maintain a constant pace indefinitely.
						- 9) Continuous attention to technical excellence and good design enhances agility.
						- 10) Simplicity–the art of maximizing the amount of work not done–is essential.
						- 11) The best architectures, requirements, and designs emerge from self-organizing teams.
						- 12) At regular intervals, the team reflects on how to become more effective, then tunes and adjusts its behavior accordingly.
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665407937177_0.png)
					- Waterfall - alternative approach
						- Pros/Cons
							- Pros
								- Useful when you know the solution perfectly
							- Cons
								- You only start hearing feedback in the Production stage, which is second-to-last
						- Stages
							- Feasibility
							- Plan
							- Design
							- Build
							- Test
							- Production
							- Support
					- Minimum Viable Project
						- Build minimum amount possible > measure feedback metrics > learn to improve next build stage
					- Related: ((646349e3-6a96-4413-ac4d-ba6a35bf1b7e))
				- LATER https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ifTF3ags0XI
				- LATER https://www.thisisservicedesigndoing.com/

					- "Something that looks great but is difficult to use is exemplary of great UI and poor UX. While something very usable that looks terrible is exemplary of great UX and poor UI." - Helga Moreno

					  id:: 63515210-a522-44b7-ada9-7f3e205cf0c1
						- make it personal to you. Even if you don’t think you’ve an interesting story - you ended up here out of thousands of applicants, and even more who would never apply. You are interesting, so share more info about where you’ve come from

						- don’t take it or yourself too seriously, it’s a learning exercise to give a different perspective on how you can improve the experience for the employers you meet

					- flexbox is the de facto for 1 dimension designs (horizontal / vertical). CSS Grid is for 2D designs when you need things laid out horizontal and vertical. so if you have already have a site design css grid usually is better, but within each section using flexbox
