			  collapsed:: true
				- Design Thinking
				  collapsed:: true
					- [5-stage design process](https://www.interaction-design.org/literature/article/5-stages-in-the-design-thinking-process)
						- Steps
							- **Empathize:** research your users' needs.
							  collapsed:: true
								- Market research - Google Trends, ads,
								- Customer research - surveys, focus groups, heatmaps for app/website to empathise with their frustration/confusion/clarity, video or in-person viewing their usage of your app/website, upvote feature forums
							- **Define:** state your users' needs and problems.
							  collapsed:: true
								- User Story
								  id:: 634543b9-bf0c-4dc9-81ab-21869acf6426
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665483730816_0.png)
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665483762799_0.png)
								- Personas
									- Putting a name and face to a user story
									- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665483918539_0.png)
									- Popular user persona generator: https://www.hubspot.com/make-my-persona
										- Very customisable for the section content, layout
								- **Create 3 user stories, 1 user persona**
									- Cooking bootcamp for beginners
										- 1) As a busy working parent
											- I want to feed my kids healthy food
											- So that I can have healthy happy chidren
										- 2) As a gym rat
											- I want lead a healthy lifestyle
											- So that I can see more progress in the gym
										- 3) As a complete novice
											- I want to learn how to cook
											- So that I can cook for myself to save money rather than ordering food
									- Persona 1
									  collapsed:: true
										- Name: Dave Parent
										- Job title: Insurance Salesman
										- Age: 35-44
										- Highest degree of education: Bachelors
										- Hobbies: none
										- Goals or objectives:
											- Try to keep themselves healthy
											- Try to feed their children healthy food via learning meal prep, cooking, shopping
											- Perform well in their job to retain it
											- Keep their relationship with their partner alive
										- Biggest challenges
										  collapsed:: true
											- Time management
											- Probably not having a lot of sex
											- Lack of personal time
											- Lack of friends
											- Keeping up motivation despite busy lifestyle
										- Things that make them happy
										  collapsed:: true
											- Loving partner
											- Cute kids
										- Recipes they'd want to see
										  collapsed:: true
											- Healthy
											- Easy and quick to make
											- Tasty enough for kids to eat
										- Frustrations
										- Bio
										- Core Needs
										- Personality: Extrovert, Family-oriented, Possibly Religious, Patient
									- Persona 2
									  collapsed:: true
										- Name: Bob Worksoutalot
										- Job title: Personal Trainer
										- Age: 25
										- Highest degree of education: A levels
										- Hobbies:
										  collapsed:: true
											- Football
											- Gym
											- Goes out often with friends
											- Into yoga or stretching
										- Goals or objectives
										  collapsed:: true
											- Learn how to make healthy food to teach that to his clients + for his own health
											- Maintain a good physique and fitness
											- Wants to live a balanced, not too stressful lifestyle
										- Biggest challenges
										  collapsed:: true
											- Limited finances
											- Balancing this bootcamp against their other social activities
											- Making time for own fitness whilst helping many others' maximise theirs
										- Things that make them happy
										  collapsed:: true
											- Good social interactions
											- Encouraging others and seeing them grow
											- Seeing positive feedback for your efforts e.g. Instagram likes on your recipes
										- Recipes they'd want to see
										  collapsed:: true
											- Not too expensive
											- Great appearance
											- Healthy
											-
										- Personality: Extrovert, Confident, Positive,
								- **What are the user's needs?**
									- Bootcamp needs to teach how to cook healthy food
									- Bootcamp needs to teach how to cook relatively cheap food
									- " " appealing foods for kids (taste, texture, appearance)
									- "" appealing foods for Instagram (appearance)
									- " " ideally quick to make foods
								- **What are your business needs?**
									- Eager to learn students who will stick around for the whole course (so that we can get feedback, word of mouth recommendations)
									- Premium course so that you can expand with marketing budget, franchising, better course quality/teachers
									- Needs to be high quality enough course that it warrants being premium
								- **What does the application need to be in order to be effective?**
									- Either state prices upfront, or ask for a deposit, or in some way filter out those who aren't able to pay
									- Lay out a schedule for when lessons are to take into account the students are likely to be busy workers already
									-
							- **Ideate:** challenge assumptions and create ideas.
							  collapsed:: true
								- Spend time in multiple rooms in sequential order
									- Dreamer
										- No limits - what could be the best solution given unlimited time, money, employees etc
										- What if?
										- Brainstorming
										- No judgement
										- Divergent thinking
										- What are our dreams and wishes?
										- No limitations
										- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665492038389_0.png)
									- Realist
										- Actionable plans
										- Select the best plans
										- Convergent thinking
										- Follow-up actions and dependencies
										- How would we make this happen
										- Starting to form a plan
										- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665493190587_0.png)
									- Critic
										- Address objections
										- Identify weaknesses
										- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665494178011_0.png)
										- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665494213439_0.png)
								- Workshop with our example of a shopping bootcamp application process
									- Dreamer
										- How do we imagine the solution
											- Instant chat/video call whilst on application page, to help them through the process
										- What are the benefits of applying this solution
											- Social proof from having celebrity endorsements
											- Having campus interviews in each city, or sending VR headsets if they aren't nearby
									- Realist
									  collapsed:: true
										- How can we apply this idea in the real world
											- Event day either virtual or in one city
											- Instant chat box supported by bots on the application page
											- Videos from alumni with testimonials about how great the course is
										- aciton plan
											- Hire a venue
											- Send a survey to find out when are free, or if they'd prefer it online-based
											- Email alumni and ask them to record a video testimonial
										- How do we evaluate
											- Split-testing across the entire cohort, or per year to try different approaches
											- Survey the applicants after to see what they liked, what they disliked, where to improve
											- Evaluate the Return on Investment (ROI) to see the profit made from applicants compared to how much spend to acquire
									- Critic
										- What could go wrong with this plan?
											- We could find out our application process is too expensive
											- It could dissuade applicants by appearing to require too much effort, time, or
										- How to prevent any problems?
											- Budget to ensure we don't overspend
											- Practice the application process so we can see how long it takes to complete
										- Why is this plan risky/unsure
											- There could be a wide variety of application process times - so test with multiple applicants timing their process
											- Budget could be very inappropriate which hinders the quality of the application, by being too large or too small
										- What are the weaknesses
											- We could over or underestimating the amount of applicants
											- We're not taking into account our competitors bootcamps and how their process differs, nor any alternatives
											- We're not taking into account applicants willingness and ability for the premium bootcamp
											- We could be over or under supplying applicants with course info before they've started, in contrast to uni vs School of Code
											- We may not gather sufficient enough or accurate enough applicant info in this process, which could impact course completion, etc
							- **Prototype:** start to create solutions.
							- **Test:** try your solutions out.
						- Non-linear. So you can return to previous stages as needed
					- #quote
						- The Grand Old Man of User Experience, Don Norman, who also coined the very term User Experience, explains what Design Thinking is and what’s so special about it:
						- “…the more I pondered the nature of design and reflected on my recent encounters with engineers, business people and others who blindly solved the problems they thought they were facing without question or further study, I realized that these people could benefit from a good dose of design thinking. Designers have developed a number of techniques to avoid being captured by too facile a solution. They take the original problem as a suggestion, not as a final statement, then think broadly about what the real issues underlying this problem statement might really be (for example by using the "Five Whys" approach to get at root causes). Most important of all, is that the process is iterative and expansive. Designers resist the temptation to jump immediately to a solution to the stated problem. Instead, they first spend time determining what the basic, fundamental (root) issue is that needs to be addressed. They don't try to search for a solution until they have determined the real problem, and even then, instead of solving that problem, they stop to consider a wide range of potential solutions. Only then will they finally converge upon their proposal. This process is called "Design Thinking." — Don Norman, Rethinking Design Thinking
					- Creating own bootcamp and empathise
						- Tips during the application not to feel overwhelmed, it's going to be a challenge
						- Saying your expectations for applicants, you're looking for a positive hard-working personality, not necessarily competence already
					-
				- CSS Variables
					- Similar to JavaScript variables - you can assign it to different things later
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```css
						  main {
						    --backgroundColour: red; /* variable declaration */
						  }
						  
						  main {
						    background-color: var(--background);
						  }
						  ```
					- `:root`/`html` is the highest parent selector in a HTML file.
					- `*` also works, but it's convention to use `:root` instead to achieve the same purpose
					- Defining variables in the `:root` allows you to change it across the whole CSS file
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```css
						  :root {
						    --background-color: whitesmoke;
						    --accentOne: #ffd6ce
						    --accentTwo: #ceefff;
						    --borders: lightcoral;
						  }
						  ```
					- Convention is using camel case for the variable name
					- Overriding CSS variables
					  collapsed:: true
					  AKA reassigning
						- ```css
						  :root {
						    --blue: #1e90ff;
						    --white: #ffffff;
						  }
						  
						  body {
						    background-color: var(--blue);
						  }
						  
						  h2 {
						    border-bottom: 2px solid var(--blue);
						  }
						  
						  .container {
						    color: var(--blue);
						    background-color: var(--white);
						    padding: 15px;
						  }
						  
						  button {
						    --blue: #0000ff; /* local variable will override global */
						    background-color: var(--white);
						    color: var(--blue);
						    border: 1px solid var(--blue);
						    padding: 5px;
						  }
						  ```
						- `toggle` can be used to add/remove a class from an element, use it instead of `add`/`remove` because they don't work like expected - they'll still retain the changes made to their CSS properties by the class
						  id:: 63642a2c-4332-42ad-9695-95a1d0a3866b
							- https://www.w3schools.com/jsref/prop_element_classlist.asp

				- Wireframing
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 6346c70a-fcf1-496c-80e8-933b99e74967
					- Types of fidelity
						- Low-fidelity wireframing
							- Pros
								- Quicker to get feedback from (compatible with Agile) by using
							- Relevant software
								- Miro
								- InVision
						- Animations and transitions
							- Relevant software
								- Principle
						- High-fidelity wireframing
							- Pros
								- Colour
								- Typography
								- Icons
							- Relevant software
								- ((6345af5c-353c-4aed-9b5a-572bd52cc70a))
								- Proto.io
								- Adobe Xd

					- **Fundamentals of UX**
					  collapsed:: true
						- **Goals**
							- Distinguish between User Interface Design and User Experience Design
							- Understand problem solving using User Experience Design
							- Differentiate between "Good UX" and "Bad UX"
							- Know the different areas of UX (career-wise)
							- Have a good understanding of some laws of UX
						- **Books**
							- UX Fundamentals for Non-UX Professionals: User Experience Principles for Managers, Writers. [Download](https://www.pdfdrive.com/ux-fundamentals-for-non-ux-professionals-user-experience-principles-for-managers-writers-designers-and-developers-e195284370.html)
							- UX Design Principle Fundamentals for Non-Designers. [Download](https://www.pdfdrive.com/ux-design-principle-fundamentals-for-non-designers-e17862156.html)
						- **Articles**

							- Laws of UX. [View](https://lawsofux.com/)
							- UI, UX: Who Does What? A Designer’s Guide To The Tech Industry. [View](https://www.fastcompany.com/3032719/ui-ux-who-does-what-a-designers-guide-to-the-tech-industry)
							- What Is User Experience (UX) Design? Everything You Need To Know To Get Started. [View](https://careerfoundry.com/en/blog/ux-design/what-is-user-experience-ux-design-everything-you-need-to-know-to-get-started/)

					- **Design Thinking**
					  collapsed:: true
						- **Goals**
							- Understand the UX Design process end to end
							- Know the basics of the design thinking
							- Empathize with users
							- Figure out user pain points
							- Understand and build personas
						- **Books**
							- Basics Design: Design Thinking. [Download](https://www.pdfdrive.com/basics-design-design-thinking-e12052373.html)

					- **Information Architecture**
					  collapsed:: true
						- **Goals**
							- Digest and understand what information architecture
							- Understand concept like site mapping, user flow, user journey etc.
							- Understand and write user stories and epics

					- **User Testing and Heuristics Evaluation**
					  collapsed:: true
						- **Goals**
							- Importance of usability testing
							- Carry out usability testing
							- Have understanding of usability methods and how to carry them out
							- Use heuristics evaluation to test products
							- Carry out competitive analysis

				- CSS Organisation
				  collapsed:: true
					- Why organise CSS
						- Maintability - preserve exiting styles without introducing new bugs
						- Scalability - add new styles safely and predictably
						- Reusability - be able to use the same classes anywhere
					- Naming
						- Classes should be easy to read and relative
						- This helps you and your team understand the CSS codebase
					- Avoid duplication
						- Whichever class is lower in the stylesheet will overwrite the former if they both have the same name
					- CSS Specificity
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665587031596_0.png)
						- `*` has `0` specificity value, whereas `!important` has `100` specificity value
						- This is ignores the ordering of the selectors in the CSS document
						- **Specificity Values table**
							- |**Selector Type**|**Example**|**Specificity Value**|**Calculation**|
							  |--|--|--|--|
							  |`:root`/`html`|`*`|0|0 (the universal selector is ignored)|
							  |Element|`p`|1|1|||
							  |Element + Class|`p.test`|11|1 + 10||
							  |Element + ID|`p#demo`|101|1 + 100||
							  |Class|`.test`|10|10|
							  |Element + Class + Class|`p.test1.test2`|21|1+10+10|
							  |ID|`#demo`|100|100||
							  |ID, and Element + ID|`#navbar p#demo`|201|100 + 1 + 100|
							  |Inline style|`<p style='color: pink;';>`|1000|1000||
								- Element + Class means that `p.test` only applies to `p` elements with the `test` class
								- See ((63317807-bd1e-430c-bf63-985d824439f1)) for more examples of selector types e.g. ((633852d9-a42c-4d95-a507-d3107214895f))
						- Other notes
							- ID selectors have a higher specificity than attribute selectors
							- Equal specificity: the latest rule wins - If the same rule is written twice into the external style sheet, then the latest rule wins
							- Contextual selectors are more specific than a single element selector - The embedded style sheet is closer to the element to be styled. So in the following situation
							- A class selector beats any number of element selectors - a class selector such as .intro beats h1, p, div, etc:
							- The universal selector (*) and inherited values have a specificity of 0 - The universal selector (*) and inherited values are ignored!
					- Tips
						- Consider alternatives to styling with `#id` e.g. classes, element + ID, etc
						- Avoid using `!important`
						- There's no error console for CSS
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1665589203695_0.png)

						  main > div.buttons > div { /* buttons class that is the child of div, div and main */

						  	-webkit-text-size-adjust: 100%;
						  }
						  input,button,textarea{font-family:inherit;}
						  ​
						  input::-ms-clear{display: none;}
						  button{cursor: pointer;}
						  button::-moz-focus-inner {padding:0;border:0;}
						  a, a:visited{text-decoration: none;}
						  a:hover{text-decoration: none;}
						  ul li{list-style: none;}

						- 4) Results #2
					- Presentation creation
					- User interview
						- You want to get to know your future users and their current workflows better so you can create a better experience for them. You need to understand things like: What do they use digital notes for? What is their current note-taking process like? Where are the pain points in that process? How do they feel about their current note-taking options?
						- Make a plan for, and carry out, three real user interviews on the subject of digital note-taking. Aim to pull out specific insights that would inform the features and design of a new note-taking app like the one in this challenge. Analyze your findings and put together some recommendations based on what you've learned.
						- [Learning Resources]
							- https://www.nngroup.com/articles/user-interviews/?ref=uxtools-challenges
							- https://stephaniewalter.design/blog/a-cheatsheet-for-user-interview-and-follow-ups-questions/?ref=uxtools-challenges
							- https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2020/06/user-interview-questions/?ref=uxtools-challenges
							- {{video https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=auQCdrRfYas}}
								- {{youtube-timestamp 108}} Bring a script so you can remember to ask each question, though note you might end up doing follow-up questions instead
								- {{youtube-timestamp 172}} Customer intro questions to break the ice. What's your typical day look like? On an average day, when do you start using technology?
								- {{youtube-timestamp 377}} When was the last time you X
								- {{youtube-timestamp 385}} Whens the last time you did a workaround, an alternative solution etc. This indicates they're open to a new solution
								- {{youtube-timestamp 422}} What other tools have you used to do X?
								- {{youtube-timestamp 434}} Have you paid for any of these tools? How did you hear about them?

			- Day 1
			  collapsed:: true
				- Debugging
				  collapsed:: true
					- Console error messages are structured like:
						- 1) Error message
						- 2) `filename-with-error`:`line-number`:`column-number`(?)
					- Debugging for beginners:
						- Put `console.log` and `console.dir` at different points in code in order to check values at different points of time in your code
					- Note: `<script src='main.js'></script>` needs to be the last line in a `<body>` element
					  ```html
					  <body>
					    <p>Hello</p>
					    <script src='main.js'></script>
					  </body>
					  ```
					- Network tab in Dev Tools can give info on why `fetch` fails and give HTTP errors
					- Related: ((63a450af-9341-4a18-9ae9-c5e430efa9f1))
				- Using ((629ccb26-3115-4d41-b4db-a686fe51a4a2))
				  collapsed:: true
					- Modules
					  collapsed:: true
						- Pros
							- Allows us to split our codebase into multiple files
							- Allows us to hide complexity and simply expose a function interface instead
						- Module Systems = the syntax and tooling that allows us to create and use modules in our projects
						  id:: 635eb25f-8124-4d74-8c42-bfc5541ff543
							- 2 main ones:
							  id:: 635eb25f-23a8-4c71-92fd-55bb015d8505
								- CommonJS (CJS)
								  id:: 634d520e-84a9-467b-adc2-b8c5c32fd91d
								  collapsed:: true
									- Importing modules
									  ```javascript
									  const { red, blue } = require('colors');
									  ```
									- Exporting modules
									  id:: 635eb25f-3096-4fd1-881f-c735e2cb711e
									  ```javascript
									  module.exports = {
									    red: "red",
									    blue: "blue"
									  };
									  ```
								- ECMAScript Modules (ESM)
								  id:: 634d5214-1dc4-46d2-84c4-d03c7da376cc
								  collapsed:: true
									- Newer, expected to become the main standard
									- Documentation
										- Importing modules
											- Syntax
											  ```javascript
											  d
											  ```
											- (four different examples)
											  ```javascript
											  // my-app/file.mjs
											  // Static: top of file, only string imports
											  import colors from "colors";          // _Only_ `default` export (`{ green }`)
											  import { red, blue } from "colors";   // Other named exports
											  
											  // Dynamic: can call anywhere, but _must_ be asynchronous
											  const colors = await import("colors");         // This is an obj like CJS!
											  // Object shape: `colors = { red, blue, default: { green } };`
											  const { red, blue } = await import("colors");  // Destructure fields like CJS
											  ```
											- Importing a default (see ((635fa170-6a77-4d10-abbb-845732215314)) )
											  ```javascript
											  import DefaultExport from './named-export.js'
											  ```
												- Defaults means you can still destructure to import other objects from a module, but if you don't specify a different object then it'll just import the default
												  ```javascript
												  // Importing just the default
												  import DefaultExport from './named-export.js'
												  
												  // Importing the default AND other objects
												  import DefaultExport, {
												    food,
												    Bar,
												    PI,
												    person
												  } from './named-export.js'
												  ```
										- Exporting modules
											- Syntax
											  ```javascript
											  export <custom-name-to-be-used-for-imports> <what-data-to-export>
											  ```
											- Example
												- Exporting default
												  id:: 635fa170-6a77-4d10-abbb-845732215314
												  ```javascript
												  function foo(){console.log('hello')};
												  
												  // Either
												  export {foo as default}
												  // or
												  export default { green: "green" };
												  ```
													- If you export a default this means
												- Named exports
												  ```javascript
												  export const red = "red";
												  export const person = {
												    name: 'Zura'
												  }
												  export default {
												    name: 'John'
												  }
												  export function testName() {}
												  export class Bar{}
												  // or exporting them all at once
												  export {red, person, testName, Bar};
												  
												  // exporting as a different name
												  export {red as balloon, person, testName, Bar};
												  ```
												- ![image.png](../assets/image_1667229075989_0.png)
													-
										- Importing CommonJS modules as ESM modules
											- ```javascript
											  import pkg from 'express';
											  const { app } = pkg;
											  ```
									- Converting
									- Warning: You must add a new line to `package.json` to force ESM For your Node.js/Express apps, you'll need to add the following line in your package.json file to use ESM.
									  ```javascript
									  "type": "module"
									  ```
										- Replace  `"main": "index.js"`  with  `"exports": "./index.js"`  in your `package.json`.

										- Import data in CommonJS using `require`
										  id:: 634e7450-e8e4-40c2-aa95-03d5935eb632
											- ```javascript
											  const users = require("./users.json");

								- Destructuring for more succinct code
								  id:: 63642a2b-028e-460f-aed5-9d6bf0113e1e
									- Object destructuring ((63679853-25dd-4d52-85a8-466697c801b8))
									- Array destructuring ((63679853-46c2-4992-ab73-5c27acc7ce2e))
									- [Destructuring assignment](https://javascript.info/destructuring-assignment)
									- Changing the variable name allows you to
									- Example
										- ```javascript
										  const user = {
										    name: 'bob',
										    age: 10,
										    skills: [],
										    address: '999 other st',
										  };
										  let {name, age} = user;
										  console.log({name, age});
										  // expected output: 'bob', 10
										  ```
									- ```javascript
									  const users = require("./users.json"); 
									  const { gréet } = require("./greeter.js");
									  
									  const jane = users[1];
									  
									  console.log(greet(jane));
									  ```
									- ```javascript
									  const greetingText = "Hi";
									  
									  function greet({ firstName, lastName }) { 
									    return `${greetingText} ${firstName} ${lastName}`; 
									  }
									  module.exports = { greet };
									  ```
									- Destructuring in ES6 - video in freeCodeCamp
									  id:: 634d55c2-c0e5-4126-b819-8be4151dbd5f
									  collapsed:: true
										- {{video https://youtu.be/-vR3a11Wzt0}}
											- Allows extracting data from arrays and objects into distinct variables
											- Examples
												- Assign variables from objects
												  ```javascript
												  let voxel = {x: 3.6, y: 7.4, z: 6.54};
												  
												  // Regular version
												  let x = voxel.x // x = 3.6
												  let y = voxel.y // y = 7.4
												  let z = voxel.z // z = 6.54
												  
												  // Destructuring version. Much more concise than regular version
												  const {x, y, z} = voxel;
												  ```
												- How to assign these variables from an object to new values instead
												  ```javascript
												  let voxel = {x: 3.6, y: 7.4, z: 6.54};
												  
												  // Regular version
												  let x = voxel.x // x = 3.6
												  let a = x;
												  let y = voxel.y // y = 7.4
												  let b = y;
												  let z = voxel.z // z = 6.54
												  let c = z;
												  
												  // Destructuring version. Much more concise than regular version
												  const {x : a, y : b, z : c} = voxel;
												  ```
												- {{youtube-timestamp 159}} Assign variables from nested objects 
												  ```javascript
												  const a = {
												    start: { 
												      x: 5, 
												      y: 6
												    },
												    end: { 
												      x: 6, 
												      y: -9}
												  };
												  
												  // Regular version
												  a.start.x = startX; // returns '5'
												  a.start.y = startY; // returns '6'
												  
												  // Destructuring version
												  const {
												    start : { 
												      x: startX, 
												      y: startY 
												    }
												  } = a;
												  ```
												- Assign variables from arrays
												  ```javascript
												  const [q, r] = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];
												  console.log(q, r); // returns `1, 2` because it assigns just the first two values of the array
												  ```
												- Commas can be be used to assign later values in an array
												  ```javascript
												  const [q,,, r] = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];
												  console.log(q, r); // returns `1, 4` because it assigns the first and then fourth item of the array
												  ```
												- Rest operator can be used to reassign array elements
												  ```javascript
												  const [q, r, ...restVar] = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5];
												  console.log(q, r); // returns `1, 42` because it assigns just the first two values of the array
												  console.log(restVar); // logs `[3, 4, 5]` because rest operator `...` assigned 3 values to restVar. It only works as the last var of the list
												  ```
												- Pass an object as a function's parameters
												  ```javascript
												  const profileUpdate = (profileData) => {
												    const { name, age, nationality, location } = profileData;
												    // do something with these variables
												  }
												  
												  // Alternative method using more destructuring
												  const profileUpdate = ({ name, age, nationality, location }) => {
												    // do something with these variables
												  }
												  ```
									- ((63679853-25dd-4d52-85a8-466697c801b8))

									- ((636ac4ee-356d-4f2d-be6f-00f0343addd9)) in a find function

						  const webpack = require('webpack');

						- Related: ((634e7450-e8e4-40c2-aa95-03d5935eb632))
							- {{embed ((634e7450-e8e4-40c2-aa95-03d5935eb632))}}
					- Stringification and parsing are examples of serialising and deserialising
					- Workshop example
						- ```javascript
						  // Import the module. `fs` is the filesystem, which allows us to read/write files. 
						  // the new data will overwrite the entire file when we do writeFile
						  const fs = require("node:fs/promises");
						  
						  // get Users function
						  async function getUsers() {
						    // Read the JSON data and store it in a variable
						    const usersJSON = await fs.readFile("./users.json");
						    // Convert/Deserialise the JSON data to a JS array
						    const users = JSON.parse(usersJSON);
						    // Log out the result
						    console.log(users);
						  }
						  
						  // add a user
						  async function addUser(user) {
						    // Read in the existing JSON data
						    const usersJSON = await fs.readFile("./users.json");
						    // Convert/Deserialise the JSON data to a JS array. Otherwise we can't use functions like manipulate it with JS
						    const users = JSON.parse(usersJSON);
						    // Push the user that the function takes in to the users array
						    users.push(user);
						    // Converting/Serialising that array back to a JSON string
						    const JSONdata = JSON.stringify(users);
						    // Writing JSON data which contains the new user to file. Otherwise the changes won't be saved to the file
						    await fs.writeFile("./users.json", JSONdata);
						  }
						  ```

				- Node - introduction to Express
				  id:: c901c50e-8262-4a97-b480-1b78b0bb639d
				  collapsed:: true
					- HTTP communication between client-server
						- Client requests a service <-> Server responds with a provided service
							- Usually browser <-> Node.js-based server in our example
						- Client tries to reach `IP-ADDRESS`:`PORT-NUMBER`
						- {{embed ((634eb68e-0e05-471b-944a-7c6dec69331d))}}
					- [How to do "hello world" with Express](https://expressjs.com/en/starter/hello-world.html)
					  id:: 634fd63d-54c5-4085-9627-8c4b524d210f
						- ```javascript
						  // Import the Express module
						  const express = require('express')
						  // Initialise our Express app
						  const app = express()
						  // Set the port the server listens on
						  const port = 3000 
						  
						  // Create a route handler for a GET request / root path
						  app.get('/', function(req, res) { // the request asks for the / endpoint, rather than for example /about-me. res = response object
						    res.send('Hello World!')
						  })
						  
						  // Set up the listener
						  app.listen(port, function() { // enables web server to listen for client requests
						    console.log(`Example app listening on port ${port}`)
						  })
						  ```
						- Can check on http://localhost:3000
						- Related: ((634fd63d-5c76-475b-a55f-5aca80b10559))

						- To setup you need to add a command in a `start` property in `package.json`
						  id:: 634ebb11-c258-4494-8ec1-c2816a336d24
						- Same command that you'd
						- ```javascript
						  {
						    "scripts": {
						      "start": "node foo.js"
						    }
						  }
						  ```
					- How to use async functions with this (incase you're trying to utilise JSON data for example)
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666107840276_0.png)
						- ```javascript
						  async function getGreetings() {
						      let jsonData = await (fs.readFile('greetings.json'))
						      let data = JSON.parse(jsonData)
						      let num = (Math.floor(Math.random() * data.length))
						      return data[num].message
						  }
						  
						  app.get('/', async(req, res) => { // Serve this webpage URL
						      let greeting = await getGreetings()
						      res.send(`${greeting}`)// Add this string onto that webpage URL
						  })
						  
						  ```

			  collapsed:: true
				- CRUD + Rest operations
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 637210c4-a1c0-466f-a6a4-c1efcecd62d8
					- CRUD = Create, Read, Update, Delete
					- CRUD apps are linked to various HTTP methods
						- Create = POST
						- Read = GET
						- Update = PUT, PATCH
						  id:: 634fbed1-47ea-470c-b1e9-e2a9dc8f9781
							- PUT = Update (replace) a customer specified by ID
							- PATCH = Update (partial) a customer specified by ID
						- Delete = DELETE
						- HEAD = Meta information only (no body) for customer specified by ID
							- Check if the endpoint exists. 200 if it exists
					- Example API endpoints
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666171012009_0.png)
					- Web browsers automatically use GET requests when visiting URLs. Postman allows us to specify the request type
					- ((634fc54e-480b-4c74-97b3-6b1686a8a26b)) e.g. ((634fc55c-c6d3-4547-8fd0-92e2da0c8132)) allow us to send HTTP requests for testing APIs, without having to build our own frontend to test APIs

				- Using GET/POST/PATCH/DELETE in Express
				  collapsed:: true
					- Standard setup steps for a NPM project
					  id:: 634fd63d-dd88-4a10-a762-9bc0fa467de6
						- Either
							- `npm init --yes`
							  NPM initialise project (if it isn't already setup)
							- `npm i` or `npm install`
							  NPM install (will pull from `package.json` if there is one)
						- `const packagename = require(packagename");`
						  Import the NPM package
						- In `package.json` change `scripts.start` = `node main.js` so that `npm start` will execute the main JS file
						- Install nodemon as a dev dependency to make development easier
							- ```bash
							  npm i nodemon --d
							  
							  // AKA
							  npm install nodemon --save-dev
							  ```
							- It'll save it
						- ```json
						  "scripts": {
						    "start": "node app.js",
						    "dev": "nodemon app.js",
						    "test": "echo \"Error: no test specified\” && exit 1"
						  }
						  ```
							- `scripts.dev` will allow `npm run dev`
						- In your `app.js` import modules you want to make available for usage
							- ```javascript
							  const packagename = require("packagename");
							  ```

					- Setup for Express project
					  collapsed:: true
						- {{embed ((634fd63d-54c5-4085-9627-8c4b524d210f))}}
					- Presentation example
					  id:: 63503849-cc98-4075-abc6-21103f70ab89
						- ```javascript
						  const express = require('express')
						  const app = express()
						  const port = 3000
						  
						  // Parse the JSON body
						  app.use(express.json());
						  
						  // Create a route handler for a POST request / root path
						  app.get('/', function(req, res) { 
						  	// access and log the query data
						    	console.log(res.query.text);
						    
						  	// Create an object
						  	const myObject = {
						        name: "Loz",
						      }
						  	// return a response in JSON and set the status code
						    	res.status(418).json(myObject)
						  });
						  
						  // Create a route handler for a POST request / path
						  app.post("/", function() {
						    // Create an object
						    const myObject = {
						      text: "This message is from the POST request handling"
						    };
						    // return a JSON 
						    res.json(myObject);
						  });
						  
						  // Create a PATCH request handler /api/quotes/:id
						  // patch(URL, function)
						  app.patch("/api/quotes/:id", function(req, res) {
						    // log out the ID / query parameter
						    console.log(req.params.id);
						    // log out the quoteText from the body
						    console.log(req.body.quoteText);
						    
						    // return a JSON response with an object we created on the fly
						    res.json({
						      text: "This patch request was hit up!";
						    });
						  });
						  
						  
						  // Create a DELETE request handler /quotes that takes in an id as a URL parameter
						  app.delete("/api/quotes/:id", function(req, res) {
						  	// Accessing and logging out an ID from the URL parameters
						  	console.log(req.params.id);
						    
						    	// Send a JSON response with an object created on the fly
						  	req.json({
						    		text: "This is the DELETE HANDLER",
						      });
						  });
						  
						  
						  // Set up the listener
						  app.listen(port, function() { 
						  	console.log(`Example app listening on port ${port}`)
						  })
						  ```
					- devDependencies in `package.json` are for dependencies only used in development environments
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666184207631_0.png)
						- and in `package.json`
							- scripts.dev = `nodemon app.js`
					- Whatever name you use in `package.json` in the `"scripts"` object will be executed when you do `npm <scripts-name>` e.g. `npm run dev` can execute `"node app.js"`
					- ((634ff3c9-3f9a-4414-9c7d-0f20fa8ab3ae)) used so you don't have to `CTRL + C` and `npm start` every time
					- JSON is inspired by JavaScript objects, but isn't the same. This is because it was made to be language-agnostic, so many languages are able to interact with it
					  id:: 63503849-038a-4f04-9c75-d1289765d0ef
					- ((634fc55c-c6d3-4547-8fd0-92e2da0c8132))
					- Workshop example
						- Note: you may need to make it an `async function` if for example you're calling a function within it that does `JSON.parse`
						- ```javascript
						  // Create a route handler for a POST request / root path
						  app.get('/', async function(req, res) { 
						  ```
					- Our full workshop answer
						- ```javascript
						  const express = require("express");
						  const app = express();
						  const port = 3000;
						  
						  const {
						    getQuotes,
						    addQuote,
						    getRandomQuote,
						    editQuote,
						    deleteQuote,
						  } = require("./quote.js");
						  
						  app.use(express.json());
						  
						  app.get("/", function (req, res) {
						    res.send("Welcome to cwissy.rest");
						  });
						  
						  app.get("/api/quotes", async function(req, res) {
						    if (req.query.type === 'random') {
						      let randomQuote = await getRandomQuote()
						      res.json(randomQuote)
						    } else {
						      let quotes = await getQuotes()
						      res.json(quotes)
						    }
						  });
						  
						  app.post("/api/quotes", async function(req, res) {
						      console.log(req)
						      let newQuote = req.body.quoteText;
						      console.log(newQuote);
						      addQuote(newQuote)
						      res.status(418).json(newQuote)
						  });
						  
						  app.patch("/api/quotes/:id", async function(req, res) {
						    let id = req.params.id
						    let newQuote = req.body.quoteText
						    editQuote(id, newQuote)
						    res.json({
						      text: "This Patch request was hit up!",
						    });
						  });
						  
						  app.delete("/api/quotes/:id", async function(req, res) {
						    let id = req.params.id
						    deleteQuote(id)
						    res.json({
						      text: "Quote deleted!!",
						    });
						  });
						  
						  app.listen(port, function () {
						    console.log(`Server listening on port ${port}`);
						  });
						  ```
					- Notes
						- `res` object
							- `req.body` = a JSON object that gets passed via the Body in podman
							- `req.param` =
							- `req.query` =
							  id:: 63974fb0-690b-4b9a-b836-b52e1b14737d
						- `console.log(req)` can give useful info within your POST/GET/etc functions to see what it being passed on. Always examine this `res` object if confused
						- Postman
					- Quiz
						- What would you add to the end of the path in an endpoint for a URL parameter (param) of id? `:id`
						- How would a client (the person sending the request) add a query of name to their request URL? `localhost:3000/users?name=bob`
						- How could you access the name query above in your route on the server side (in your route handler/endpoint)? `const name = req.query.name;`
						  id:: 63974fb0-d819-450f-afad-3c79acd06fe0
					- Unrelated:
						- Look into `for each loop`
						- [Kumospace](https://www.kumospace.com/schoolofcode) - every tuesday and thursday kumospace. 5:30-6:30, but you can stay longer

						- About ((63503849-5c0c-4fb9-a65d-deac9a1e02e3)) in week 2, day 3
						- 1) Introduce yourself
						  id:: 63515210-4649-4c14-89f4-fa47ea1c799b

When I was 20 I made my first website in WordPress and since then I've always been fascinated by tech. I started programming about 2 years ago contributing to an open-source game I was playing a the time using a niche language called DreamMaker, and slowly found my way to JavaScript and this bootcamp.

						- 2) I'm going to talk about
							- About ((63503849-5c0c-4fb9-a65d-deac9a1e02e3)) in week 2, day 3
							- This is how server-side programs open a public interface so that client programs can interact with it
						- 3) Why this is useful
							- It's useful because there are so many public APIs
							- I play tabletop RPGs online with some friends and we use a very popular platform called roll20. They have an API
							- I also use a notetaking software called Logseq. It offers an API so you can create a plugin
						- 4) How it works
							- ```javascript
							  async function getQuote() {
							    let response = await fetch("https://api.kanye.rest");
							    console.log(response);
							  }
							  ```
							- ((63503849-47d8-4851-afc5-abdc758eb0d6))
								- {{embed ((63503849-47d8-4851-afc5-abdc758eb0d6))}}
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666258327907_0.png)
					-
				- ((634bc0c1-71ec-40e0-ba94-bf79e177b591)) and middleware
					- Adding middleware via `app.use`
					  id:: 635119de-a4e0-45d5-8e20-7a5fb61f1319
						- `app.use` allows us to move functions out of the main `app.get` or other route handlers to make it more modular. Allows multiple route handlers to use the same function for example
						- [Yesterday's example](((63503849-cc98-4075-abc6-21103f70ab89)))
							- ```javascript
							  // Parse the JSON body
							  app.use(express.json());
							  ```
						- Today's example
							- ```javascript
							  // Middleware
							  app.use(function (req, res, next) {
							    console.log('This is my middleware function');
							    next(); // needs to be called otherwise there'll be no response
							  })
							  ```
						- In the full example:
							- Using ((63512137-0fc0-4ea9-897e-cb92d4ffa8af))
							  id:: 635120f8-f9c9-4815-b2ad-f3b8dca80405
							- ```javascript
							  const express = require('express')
							  // Import the morgan module
							  const morgan = require('morgan');
							  const app = express()
							  const port = 3000
							  
							  // 1) Use morgan (3rd-party middleware)
							  app.use(morgan("dev"));
							  
							  // 2) Middleware - standard template 
							  app.use(function (req, res, next) {
							    console.log('This is my middleware function');
							    next(); // needs to be called otherwise there'll be no response
							  })
							  
							  // 3) Middleware - different syntax (passing a function in as an argument)
							  app.use(logRequestTime);
							  
							  // 3) Example function which you can call via app.use
							  function logRequestTime(req, res, next) {
							    // Attach the request time to the request object with a new property
							    req.requestTime = new Date().toLocaleString();
							  }
							  
							  // Create a GET route handler
							  app.get('/', function(req, res) { 
							  	// Send a JSON response
							    	res.json({
							  		text: 'This is the root path'
							  	});
							  });
							  
							  // Set up the listener
							  app.listen(port, function() { 
							  	console.log(`Example app listening on port ${port}`)
							  })
							  ```
						- You can optionally specify the middleware to only apply to a specific path
							- ```javascript
							  // Use morgan (3)
							  app.use('/quotes', morgan('dev'));
							  ```
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666262797840_0.png)
					- ((63512738-c687-4161-b976-4b66f7a9791d))
					- Workshop
						- Doing `app.use(morgan(""));` will show the available options to put in the string as a pop-up
						-
				- Serving static files with ((634bc0c1-71ec-40e0-ba94-bf79e177b591))
				  collapsed:: true
					- Create a folder called `public`, and inside it put your CSS, JS and HTML file for webpage. Then use this expression to serve these files
					  id:: 63515210-89b9-442a-8896-506fcdc5f667
					  ```javascript
					  // Tell Express to serve static files
					  app.use(express.static('public'))
					  ```
					- Note: on Linux may have to increase `fs.inotify.max_user_instances`
					  id:: 63515210-4fb1-47f9-a45a-cd315960bfb2
						- Check the current inotify user instance limit
						  ```bash
						  cat /proc/sys/fs/inotify/max_user_instances
						  ```
						- Check the current inotify user watch limit
						  ```bash
						  cat /proc/sys/fs/inotify/max_user_watches
						  ```
						- Update the limits
						  ```bash
						  sudo sysctl fs.inotify.max_user_instances=8192
						  sudo sysctl fs.inotify.max_user_watches=524288
						  sudo sysctl -p
						  ```
						- In order to make the changes permanent, i.e. to persist a reboot, you can set  `fs.inotify.max_user_instances=8192`  and  `fs.inotify.max_user_watches=524288`  in the file  `/etc/sysctl.conf` .
						  ```bash
						  sudo nano /etc/sysctl.conf
						  ```
					- Using ((20601e62-d46b-40e1-af45-f64e727d2757)) to allow our frontend to access our API
						- index.js (client JS)
						  ```javascript
						  async function getQuotes() {
						    	// API needs to be on a URL path other than the root /
						      let response = await fetch('http://localhost:3000/api/quotes')
						      console.log(response)
						      let jsonData = await response.json();
						      console.log(jsonData)
						      return jsonData;
						  }
						  ```
						- app.js (server JS)
						  ```javascript
						  // Middleware to serve static files
						  app.use(express.static('public'));
						  
						  app.get("/", function (req, res, next) {
						    res.send("Welcome to cwissy.rest")
						    console.dir(next)
						  });
						  
						  app.get("/api/quotes", async function(req, res) {
						    if (req.query.type === 'random') {
						      let randomQuote = await getRandomQuote()
						      res.json(randomQuote)
						    } else {
						      let quotes = await getQuotes()
						      res.json(quotes)
						    }
						  });
						  ```

				- How to modularise our code
				  collapsed:: true
					- Split it into models and routes
					- Diagram
					  collapsed:: true
						- ![Requestresponse visual example (1).png](../assets/Requestresponse_visual_example_(1)_1666279517746_0.png)
						  id:: 6351684d-550a-4d4d-af79-86ed4edc1253
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666277034288_0.png)
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666277044134_0.png)
						- 8) The router uses what's returned from the model function to create a JSON response
						- 9) The server sends the response JSON to the client
					- Steps
						- ```javascript
						  /*
						  Separate the logic of getting the data into models
						  	These are lots of functions e.g. getRandomQuotes, getQuotes, editQuote etc
						  Separate the logic of routing into a router
						  	These are the Express route handling
						  */
						  ```
						- Create a `models` folder and create a `quotes.js` inside
							- Change
								- ```javascript
								  const fileName = '../quotes.json'; // go up or down a folder depending on amount of dots?
								  ```
								- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666277331798_0.png)
								- Test via Postman - do a GET request to `http://localhost:3000/api/quotes`
						- Create a `routes` folder and create a `quotes.js` inside
							- ```javascript
							  //ROUTER
							  
							  const express = require('express');
							  const router = express.Router();
							  
							  const {
							    getQuotes,
							    addQuote,
							    getRandomQuote
							  } = require('../models/quotes.js');
							  
							  // Quotes GET handler
							  router.get("/", async function(req, res) {
							    if (req.query.type === 'random') {
							      let randomQuote = await getRandomQuote()
							      res.json(randomQuote)
							    } else {
							      let quotes = await getQuotes()
							      res.json(quotes)
							    }
							  });
							  
							  // Export this object so that app.js can use it
							  module.exports = router;
							  ```
						- Meanwhile our `app.js` will keep other data. Some of the `app` expressions are not routing, because ALL route handlers will use it
							- ```javascript
							  const express = require("express");
							  const morgan = require("morgan")
							  const app = express();
							  const port = 3000;
							  const router = require('./routes/quotes.js');
							  
							  // Use our Router
							  app.use('/api/quotes', router); // note: because the full path is here, router.get in quotes.js can be just "/"
							  
							  // Middleware to serve static files
							  app.use(express.static('public'));
							  
							  // GET handler
							  app.get("/", function (req, res, next) {
							    res.send("Welcome to cwissy.rest")
							    console.dir(next)
							  });
							  ```
					- ((635161eb-4317-4c8a-a2e6-b137445663a3))
					- Note: if trying to export just one object, make sure it's not wrapped in curly brackets
						- ```javascript
						  module.exports = { router }; // incorrect
						  
						  module.exports = router; // correct
						  
						  module.exports = { router, otherthingie }; // correct
						  ```

					- Site Reliability Engineer (SRE)
						- Typical
							- They find performance bottlenecks
							- Fix them to increase the reliability of services
							- They try to embody DevOps and empower them to deploy their own code
							- Design, build and maintain resilient core infrastructure
						- His job
							- Works with MERN (MongoDB, ExpressJS, ReactJS, and NodeJS) stacks, JavaScript/TypeScript, Python, PostgreSQL, many kinds of stacks. Finds it transferable knowledge

						- What kind of technology stacks do you work with on a daily basis, and how much of this knowledge would you say is transferable between jobs?

							  const filePath = path.resolve(__dirname, fileName);

							  const filePath = path.resolve(process.cwd(), "data", "recipes.js");

					- Presentation
						- Problems we ran into
							- We were able to console.log query and body objects but not params, because we didn't create separate GET route handlers. we instead used two app.use middleware with different paths
							- We also ran into a problem where we incorrectly diagnosed our function as not working because the formatting of our JSON lost all indentation due to parsing and stringify. We then learnt how to pass additional parameters with stringify so that it would keep indentation
							- Forgot to change the HTTP mode in Postman when testing

						- Give free talks and other free work, but if you're contributing to a profit-making venture, then demand a share of that profit. If they're just making enough to just cover their costs then that's a different story
						- Writing tests for your software is high ROI because it saves you time, ensures high code quality, helps you better cover edge cases, get confidence in your code quality because you're more sure it won't break to edge cases,
				- Postgres and CRUD
				  collapsed:: true
					- Persistent Storage with Databases
					- Non-relational databases e.g. MongoDB uses a single document file
					- Relational databases e.g. PostgreSQL organises data fields into defined columns
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666616758537_0.png){:height 581, :width 1116}
					- [Workshop db-fiddle](https://www.db-fiddle.com/f/dg2RJvBX1aZda3ECwQUSoe/81)
					  collapsed:: true
						- Examples
							- This works kinda like `console.log` since it shows our data
							  ```sql
							  SELECT * FROM users; // users is the name of our table
							  ```
								- `;` is mandatory unlike JavaScript
							- Select everything from users 
							  id:: 63568fec-9c27-487b-aa7f-cfbcdc286b8b
							  ```sql
							  SELECT * FROM users WHERE user_id = 126;
							  ```
								- Unlike JavaScript `=` isn't an assignment operator in SQL, it actsmore like `===`
							- ```sql
							  SELECT email FROM users WHERE user_id = 1;
							  ```
							- CRUD statements
								- INSERT INTO statements
								  AKA Create
									- Relational databases will throw an error if you try to `INSERT INTO` a field that doesn't exist
									- ```sql
									  INSERT INTO users (name, email, username) VALUES ('Tao', 'tao@tao.com', 'BegoniaFan');
									  ```
								- RETURN statements
									- Also works like `console.log` and shows all our data
									  ```sql
									  RETURNING *; 
									  ```
										- e.g. 
										  ```sql
										  DELETE FROM users WHERE user_id = 124 RETURNING *;
										  ```
									- It requires a valid WHERE? e.g.
									  ![image.png](../assets/image_1666621871627_0.png)
								- UPDATE statements
								  AKA Update
									- Update <table> and change <column> to new value IF user_id column = 127
									  ```sql
									  UPDATE users SET username = 'Ta-yoyo' WHERE user_id = 127;
									  ```
								- DELETE statement
									- ```sql
									  DELETE FROM users WHERE user_id = 124;
									  ```
							- `GENERATED AS IDENTITY`
								- Replaces SERIAL
								- e.g. `GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY`
								- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666622916773_0.png)
								- This guarantees that the `user_id` column has a unique value for each row
								  ![image.png](../assets/image_1666623043166_0.png)
								-
					- Workshop
						- Examples
							- ```
							  CREATE TABLE music (
							    artist_id INT GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY PRIMARY KEY,
							    artist_name TEXT,
							    genre TEXT,
							    fav_song TEXT,
							    is_active BOOL
							  );
							  
							  INSERT INTO music (artist_name, genre, fav_song, is_active) VALUES ('My Bloody Valentine', 'Shoegaze', 'Sometimes', false), ('Tom MacDonald', 'Hip Hop', 'Brainwashed', true), ('Kendrick Lamar', 'Hip Hop', 'Money Trees', true), ('Chris Webby', 'Hip Hop', 'Raw Thoughts V', true), ('Nirvana', 'Alternative', 'Drain You', false);
							  ```
							- Query SQL
							  ```sql
							  SELECT * FROM music;
							  
							  INSERT INTO music (artist_name, genre, fav_song, is_active) VALUES ('Angels and Airwaves', 'Alternative', 'Heaven', false) RETURNING *;
							  
							  DELETE FROM music WHERE artist_name = 'Chris Webby' RETURNING *;
							  ```
					- [SQLBolt](https://sqlbolt.com) - lots of SQL statement learning via an interactive game, similar to CSSDiner
					  id:: 6356a6af-0197-4196-a6ab-258c05ba4f27
					  collapsed:: true
						- 1) SELECT queries 101
						  collapsed:: true
							- Select query for a specific column
							  ```sql
							  SELECT column, another_column, …
							  FROM mytable;
							  ```
							- Select query for all columns
							  ```sql
							  SELECT * 
							  FROM mytable;
							  ```
							- Select query for two columns
							  ```sql
							  SELECT title, director FROM movies;
							  ```
						- 2) Queries with constraints (Pt. 1)
							- `WHERE` clause can be used to filter data, like an if statement in JavaScript
							  collapsed:: true
								- Select query with constraints
									- Syntax
									  ```sql
									  SELECT column, another_column, …
									  FROM mytable
									  WHERE condition
									      AND/OR another_condition
									      AND/OR …;
									  ```
							- `AND` or `OR` logical keywords can make more complex clauses
							  collapsed:: true
								- Example
								  ```sql
								  num_wheels >= 4 AND doors <= 2
								  ```
							- Operators
								- Some notable ones:
									- `=`, `!=`, `<`, `<=`, `>`, `>=` operators
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|SQL Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |=, !=, < <=, >, >=|Standard numerical operators|col_name != 4|
										- `<=` operator
										  collapsed:: true
											- Example
											  collapsed:: true
												- Find the first 5 Pixar movies and their release year
												  ```sql
												  SELECT title, year FROM movies
												  WHERE year <= 2003;
												  ```
									- `BETWEEN` operator
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|SQL Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |BETWEEN … AND …|Number is within range of two values (inclusive)|col_name BETWEEN 1.5 AND 10.5|
										- Example
										  collapsed:: true
											- Find the movies released in the  `year` s between 2000 and 2010
											  ```sql
											  SELECT * FROM movies WHERE year BETWEEN 2000 AND 2010
											  ```
									- `NOT BETWEEN` operator
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|SQL Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |NOT BETWEEN … AND …|Number is not within range of two values (inclusive)|col_name NOT BETWEEN 1 AND 10|
										- Example
										  collapsed:: true
											- Find the movies **not** released in the  `year` s between 2000 and 2010
											  ```sql
											  SELECT * FROM movies WHERE year NOT BETWEEN 2000 AND 2010
											  ```
									- `IN` operator
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|SQL Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |IN (…)|Number exists in a list|col_name IN (2, 4, 6)|
									- `NOT IN` operator
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|SQL Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |NOT IN (…)|Number does not exist in a list|col_name NOT IN (1, 3, 5)|
						- 3) Queries with constraints (Pt. 2)
							- TEXT (string)-specific operators
								- `||` string concatenation operator
								- `+=` string concatenation assignment character
								- `=` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |=|Case sensitive exact string comparison (notice the single equals)|col_name = "abc"|
								- `!=` or `<>` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |!= or <>|Case sensitive exact string inequality comparison|col_name != "abcd"|
								- `LIKE` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |LIKE|Case insensitive exact string comparison|col_name LIKE "ABC"|
									- Example
										- ((6358147d-8a32-4073-831d-58d2f34cff43))
								- `NOT LIKE` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |NOT LIKE|Case insensitive exact string inequality comparison|col_name NOT LIKE "ABCD"|
								- *Wildcard operators*
									- `%`(any sequence of zero or more characters) operator
									  id:: 63581b59-17dc-4aec-9db5-0286ee388786
									  collapsed:: true
										- # (Wildcard - Character(s) to Match)
										- # (Wildcard - Character(s) to Match)
										- |Operator|Condition|Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |%|Used anywhere in a string to match a sequence of zero or more characters (only with LIKE or NOT LIKE) |col_name LIKE "%AT%" (matches "AT", "ATTIC", "CAT" or even "BATS")|
										- Example
										  id:: 63581b59-c25e-40c8-8090-347f56f9eb00
											- id:: 6358147d-8a32-4073-831d-58d2f34cff43
											  ```sql
											  SELECT * FROM movies WHERE title LIKE "Toy Story%"
											  ```
											  Find all the Toy Story movies
												- Syntax
												  ```sql
												  SELECT ALL-DATA FROM <tablename> WHERE <fieldname> LIKE <string>
												  ```
									- `_`(any single character) operator
									  collapsed:: true
										- |Operator|Condition|Example|
										  |--|--|--|
										  |_|Used anywhere in a string to match a single character (only with LIKE or NOT LIKE)|col_name LIKE "AN_" (matches "AND", but not "AN")|
									- *Not supported in PostgreSQL?*
										- `[]`
										  collapsed:: true
											- Wildcard character/s to match. Matches any single character within the specified range or set that is specified between brackets [ ]
										- `[^]`
										  collapsed:: true
											- Wildcard character/s not to match. Matches any single character that is not within the range or set specified between the square brackets [^]
								- `IN` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |IN (…)|String exists in a list|col_name IN ("A", "B", "C")|
								- `NOT IN` operator
								  collapsed:: true
									- |Operator|Condition|Example|
									  |--|--|--|
									  |NOT IN (…)|String does not exist in a list|col_name NOT IN ("D", "E", "F")|
						- 4) Filtering and sorting Query results
							- `DISTINCT` keyword
							  collapsed:: true
								- Can be used to discard rows that have a duplicate column value
								- Select query with unique results
								  ```sql
								  SELECT DISTINCT column, another_column, …
								  FROM mytable
								  WHERE condition(s);
								  ```
								- Example
									- List all directors of Pixar movies (alphabetically), without duplicates
									  ```sql
									  SELECT DISTINCT director FROM movies ORDER BY director ASC
									  ```
							- `ORDER BY` keyword
							  id:: 63581678-5b85-4683-b18e-d0499af26836
							  collapsed:: true
								- way to sort your results by a given column in ascending or descending order
								- Syntax
									- ```sql
									  Select query with ordered results
									  SELECT column, another_column, …
									  FROM mytable
									  WHERE condition(s)
									  ORDER BY column ASC/DESC;
									  ```
								- Example
									- ```sql
									  SELECT title FROM movies ORDER BY title ASC LIMIT 5 OFFSET 5
									  ```
									  List the **next** five Pixar movies sorted alphabetically
							- `LIMIT` clause
							  collapsed:: true
								- Often used with ((63581678-5b85-4683-b18e-d0499af26836)) to reduce the number of rows to return
								- Syntax
									- ```sql
									  SELECT column, another_column, …
									  FROM mytable
									  WHERE condition(s)
									  ORDER BY column ASC/DESC
									  LIMIT num_limit OFFSET num_offset;
									  ```
							- `OFFSET` clause
							  collapsed:: true
								- Often used with ((63581678-5b85-4683-b18e-d0499af26836)) to specify what number (index?) to begin counting the number rows from
								- Syntax
									- ```sql
									  SELECT column, another_column, …
									  FROM mytable
									  WHERE condition(s)
									  ORDER BY column ASC/DESC
									  LIMIT num_limit OFFSET num_offset;
									  ```
						- 5) Simple SELECT Queries
						  collapsed:: true
							- SELECT query
							  collapsed:: true
								- Syntax - intermediate
									- ```sql
									  SELECT column, another_column, …
									  FROM mytable
									  WHERE *condition(s)*
									  ORDER BY column ASC/DESC
									  LIMIT num_limit OFFSET num_offset;
									  ```
								- List all
								  ```sql
								  SELECT city, population FROM north_american_cities WHERE country = 'Canada';
								  ```
						- 6+7) Multi-table queries with JOINs
						  collapsed:: true
							- Related: ((63581b8a-35f9-4523-acdd-f1f6a296e8ea))
						- 8) A short note on NULLs
							- `IS NULL` and `IS NOT NULL` constraint
							  collapsed:: true
								- Can be used to check if there is/isn't null values in a database
						- 9) Queries with expressions
						  collapsed:: true
							- These are for more complex logic
							- Example query with expressions
							  ```sql
							  SELECT particle_speed / 2.0 AS half_particle_speed
							  FROM physics_data
							  WHERE ABS(particle_position) * 10.0 > 500;
							  ```
							- Expressions can be useful but they can damage readability, so it's recommended that when using expressions in the `SELECT` part of the query, also give them a descriptive alias using the AS keyword
							- `AS` alias keyword
							  collapsed:: true
								- Syntax
									- ```sql
									  SELECT col_expression AS expr_description, …
									  FROM mytable;
									  ```
								- Example
									- Example query with both column and table name aliases
									  ```sql
									  SELECT column AS better_column_name, …
									  FROM a_long_widgets_table_name AS mywidgets
									  INNER JOIN widget_sales
									  ON mywidgets.id = widget_sales.widget_id;
									  ```
									- List all movies and their combined sales in **millions** of dollars 
									  (creating an AS out of SELECT)
									  ```sql
									  SELECT title, (domestic_sales + international_sales) / 1000000 AS gross_sales_millions
									  FROM movies
									    JOIN boxoffice
									      ON movies.id = boxoffice.movie_id;
									  ```
						- Next:
						- [10) Queries with aggregates (Pt. 1)](https://sqlbolt.com/lesson/select_queries_with_aggregates)

				- SQL JOINS
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 63e40d9f-1155-418b-83dc-aa5caa4654fe
					- A JOIN clause allows us to combine rows from 2 or more tables joined via a  a related column
					- Using a JOIN is quicker and more efficient than doing two separate queries
					- "Normalisation allows us to keep data redundancy low so that we can decrease the amount of data anomalies in our application when we delete or update a record."
					- Entity Relationship Diagram
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666689903250_0.png)
							- This diagram shows that row `user_id` in the left `Users` table can be linked to many matching values labelled `author` in the `Posts` database
						- Related: ((63ff4fed-088c-4540-83f4-24954cd3b566))
					- [Workshop exercise 1](https://www.db-fiddle.com/f/4FJEKAFU4SS5uECGdLeXgM/0)
					- JOIN statements
					  id:: 63581b8a-35f9-4523-acdd-f1f6a296e8ea
						- Meta
						  id:: 635827cd-302a-4bdf-83dc-ae4ec18779fb
							- What
								- SQL joins are used to combine data from two or more tables in a relational database based on a related column between them. The result of a join operation is a new table that contains all the columns from the original tables.
								  collapsed:: true
									- Joins are useful for retrieving data from multiple tables that are related to each other. They allow you to combine data from different tables in a single result set, which can help simplify queries and make it easier to analyze data.
									- Foreign keys are preferred for the related column, but not mandatory
										- SQL joins do not strictly require a foreign key to be used, although foreign keys are often used in conjunction with joins to enforce referential integrity and ensure that data is consistent across tables.
										- The columns used for the join do not necessarily have to be foreign keys, although they often are. In fact, a join can be performed using any column or combination of columns that have matching values in both tables.
										- However, using foreign keys can be beneficial for several reasons. Firstly, it ensures that the related data between tables is consistent and accurate. Secondly, it can improve the performance of the query since indexing foreign keys can speed up the join operation. Finally, using foreign keys can also make the database schema easier to understand and maintain.
							- Template
								- ```sql
								  SELECT field1
								  FROM table1
								  INNER JOIN table2
								  ON table1.field2 = table2.field2
								  WHERE table2.field3 = 'Something'
								  ```
									- Ideally using the foreign key `table1.field2` which references the primary key of `table2.field2`
							- `SELECT` can target two fields of different tables
							  collapsed:: true
								- e.g.
								  ```sql
								  SELECT DISTINCT building_name, role 
								  FROM buildings 
								  ```
								- Related: ((6358279b-91ab-4f95-9f73-d156139eb5f1))
							- `ON` keyword doesn't require table names to be mentioned
							  collapsed:: true
								- e.g. {{embed ((6358279b-91ab-4f95-9f73-d156139eb5f1))}}
								- Related: ((6358279b-91ab-4f95-9f73-d156139eb5f1))
						- `INNER JOIN`
						  id:: 63f33e8e-678b-41e0-a798-adfc895e30c9
						  collapsed:: true
						  Returns only the rows that have matching values in both tables
							- Syntax
								- ```sql
								  SELECT column, another_table_column, …
								  FROM mytable
								  INNER JOIN another_table 
								    ON mytable.id = another_table.id
								  WHERE condition(s)
								  ORDER BY column, … ASC/DESC
								  LIMIT num_limit OFFSET num_offset;
								  ```
							- Examples
								- Select everything from `users` table, joi `posts` table if there's a match on the `user_id` and `author` field
								  ```sql
								  SELECT * -- ensures we can see all columns, not just one
								  FROM users
								  INNER JOIN posts
								  ON users.user_id = posts.author;
								  ```
								- Find the domestic and international sales for each movie
								  ```sql
								  SELECT * FROM movies
								  INNER JOIN boxoffice 
								  ON movies.id = boxoffice.movie_id
								  ```
								- Show the sales numbers for each movie that did better internationally rather than domestically
								  ```sql
								  SELECT * FROM movies
								  INNER JOIN boxoffice 
								  ON movies.id = boxoffice.movie_id
								  WHERE boxoffice.domestic_sales < boxoffice.international_sales
								  ```
						- `LEFT OUTER JOIN`
						  collapsed:: true
						  AKA `LEFT JOIN` | Returns all the rows from the left table and the matching rows from the right table. If there is no match in the right table, it returns NULL values.
							- Returns what matches both AND what matches the left table (even if it is `null` on the right table)
								- "First, an inner join is performed. Then, for each row in T1 that does not satisfy the join condition with any row in T2, a joined row is added with null values in columns of T2. Thus, the joined table always has at least one row for each row in T1."
							- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666695592161_0.png)
							- Example
								- ```sql
								  SELECT * 
								  FROM users
								  LEFT JOIN posts
								  ON users.user_id = posts.author;
								  
								  SELECT * FROM users;
								  ```
								- ```sql
								  SELECT posts.content, comments.content
								  FROM users
								  LEFT JOIN comments
								  ON posts.post_id = comments.post_id
								  
								  SELECT * FROM users;
								  ```
								- List all buildings and the distinct employee roles in each building (including empty buildings)
								  id:: 6358279b-91ab-4f95-9f73-d156139eb5f1
								  ```sql
								  SELECT DISTINCT building_name, role 
								  FROM buildings 
								    LEFT JOIN employees
								      ON building_name = building;
								  ```
						- `RIGHT OUTER JOIN`
						  collapsed:: true
						  AKA `RIGHT JOIN` | Returns all the rows from the right table and the matching rows from the left table. If there is no match in the left table, it returns NULL values.
							- Example
								- ```sql
								  SELECT * 
								  FROM users
								  LEFT JOIN posts
								  ON users.user_id = posts.author;
								  
								  SELECT * FROM users;
								  ```
						- `FULL OUTER JOIN`
						  collapsed:: true
						  AKA `FULL JOIN` | Returns all the rows from both tables. If there is no match in either table, it returns NULL values.
							- Will return each rows that match on either table, as well as ones where there is `null` on either side
							- Syntax
								- Select query with LEFT/RIGHT/FULL JOINs on multiple tables
								  ```sql
								  SELECT column, another_column, …
								  FROM mytable
								  INNER/LEFT/RIGHT/FULL JOIN another_table 
								    ON mytable.id = another_table.matching_i
								  WHERE*condition(s)
								  ORDER BY column, … ASC/DESC
								  LIMIT num_limit OFFSET num_offset;
								  ```
							- Example
								- ```sql
								  SELECT *
								  FROM posts
								  FULL JOIN comments
								  ON comments.author = posts.author;
								  ```

							- 1) How can you produce a list of the start times for bookings by members named 'David Farrell'?
							  id:: 63f62339-286e-456b-b8e0-de11e85336db
								- Simplejoin1 ((63f33e8e-678b-41e0-a798-adfc895e30c9))
								- With ((63f624a9-c52b-4a4c-8db5-0d5656346c36))
								  ```sql
								  SELECT starttime
								  FROM cd.bookings bks
								  INNER JOIN cd.members mems
								  ON bks.memid = mems.memid
								  WHERE mems.firstname = 'David' 
								  AND mems.surname = 'Farrell'
								  ```
								- Without ((63f624a9-c52b-4a4c-8db5-0d5656346c36))
								  ```sql
								  SELECT starttime
								  FROM cd.bookings
								  INNER JOIN cd.members
								  ON cd.bookings.memid = cd.members.memid
								  WHERE cd.members.firstname = 'David' 
								  AND cd.members.surname = 'Farrell'
								  ```
							- 2) How can you produce a list of the start times for bookings for tennis courts, for the date '2012-09-21'? Return a list of start time and facility name pairings, ordered by the time

						  collapsed:: true
							- Example
								- ```sql
								  CREATE TABLE authors (
								    id INT PRIMARY KEY GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY,
								    first_name TEXT,
								    last_name TEXT
								  );
								  
								  INSERT INTO authors
								  	(first_name, last_name)
								  VALUES
								  	('Henry', 'Fielding'),
								  	('Jane', 'Austen'),
								  	('Marie-Henri', 'Beyle');
								  
								  CREATE TABLE books (
								    id INT PRIMARY KEY GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY,
								    author_id INT REFERENCES authors(id),
								    title TEXT,
								    published_date DATE
								  );
								  
								  INSERT INTO books
								  	(author_id, title, published_date)
								  VALUES
								  	(1, 'The History of Tom Jones, a Foundling', '2/28/1749'),
								  	(2, 'Pride and Prejudice', '1/28/1813'),
								  	(3, 'The Red and the Black', '11/1/1830');
								  ```
