				- Presentation based on research task:   **Pagination (Offset vs cursor-based)**
				  collapsed:: true
					- Presentation notes
						- Research notes
							- Pagination
							  collapsed:: true
								- What it is
									- Typically in an application with a database, you might have more records than you can fit on a page or in a single result set from a query. When you or your users want to retrieve the next page of results
							- Offset
							  collapsed:: true
								- Pros
									-
								- Cons
								  id:: 635a7cc8-4049-4c2b-98ab-8107511ce481
									- More complex
									- Performance - This gets slower as the number of records increases because the database still has to read up to the offset number of rows to know where it should start selecting data.
									- Skipping data
									- Duplicate data
									- In datasets that change frequently as is typical of large databses with frequent writes, the window of results will often be inaccurate across different pages in that you will either miss results entirely or see duplicates because results have now been added to the previous page.
									-
								- What it is
									- Clients can supply 2 additional parameters in their query: an offset, and a limit.
									  An offset is simply the number of records you wish to skip before selecting records
										- ```sql
										  SELECT * FROM books ORDER BY id ASC LIMIT 15 OFFSET 4
										  ```
							- Cursor-based
							  collapsed:: true
								- Pros/Cons
									- Pros
									- Cons
								- What it is
									- ```sql
									  SELECT * FROM books WHERE id >= 12345 ORDER BY id ASC LIMIT 15
									  ```
									- A cursor is a unique identifier for a specific record
									- Use `WHERE` TO
						- 1) Keira - introduction
						- *Offset*
							- Taylor - pros
							- Aaron - cons
								- Speech
									- ~~Offset-based pagination has some pretty major cons however that make it difficult to scale~~
									- Imagine you had a database with 10 thousand records. If you wanted to return just the last 15 records then you'd need OFFSET 9985, which would mean your database has to read 99% of your records before it knows what data to select, which is really poor performance
									- Next you have to take into account that most large databases are constantly receiving new records
										- This means whilst your query is reading through each record to where to start the OFFSET, new records are being created both *before* and *after* **that point**
										- The data **before** that point won't be read, so it'll **skip** reading that data
										- And that also means some records will be **read multiple times**, because new inserts are shifting forward the rest of the data
										- **and now I'll pass onto Ryan**

					  id:: 635a9ef8-c2e6-44bb-ae34-fde234128a67

						- Arrow functions introduced in ES6. Compact form of traditional functions
						  They're anonymous, can't be referred to unless they're saved to a variable

					- SQL vs NoSQL
					  collapsed:: true
						- SQL databases are vertically scalable i.e. add more server size, RAM etc
						- NoSQL has horizontal scalability - add more servers/nodes
					- API versioning
					  collapsed:: true
						- Breaking changes are ones that force the client to also make changes to their application
						- Ways to avoid this
							- Add new properties/endpoints rather than changing existing ones
							- Create a new major version rather than minor version if there's a breaking change. New endpoints or response parameters don't require a change to the major version number
						- Types of API versioning
							- 1) URL path
							- 2) use version number as a query parameter
					- CORS
					  collapsed:: true
						- Same-origin security policy is used by browsers to prevent
						- CORS is a npm package which allows you to restrict which domains can interact

					- REST vs ((63a2bb46-8e4a-4d42-b382-f116c0f77196))
					  id:: 63974faf-fdba-4490-8561-85f7099c6db7
					  collapsed:: true
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1666885749411_0.png)
						- What is GraphQL?
							- GraphQL is an specification query language, and open-source data query. In order words, a syntax for requesting data that lets you specify precisely what you need.
							- GraphQL was developed internally by Facebook in 2012.
							- Uses its query language to tailor the request to exactly what you need, from multiple objects down to specific fields within each entity.
							- GraphQL servers are available for multiple languages, including JavaScript, Python, Ruby, Java, C++, Go, PHP, and R.
						- Differences
							- GraphQL is a query language and uses HTTP and can work on a single endpoint which increases speed an flexibility
							- REST is more based on the architecture of the network and is serve side focused. It has been typically used to develop new APIs
							- In GraphQL, data is organized into a graph, and objects are structured by fields that are predefined - like books.title etc.
							- Since the client side now has more freedom in the fetched data, development is much faster with GraphQL than what it would be with REST.
							- In GraphQL, a single query can call various resolvers to provide a response with multiple resources. In REST, one query usually calls one route handler function.
						- [Learning Resources]
							- [GraphQL vs REST | GraphQL Tutorial](https://hasura.io/learn/graphql/intro-graphql/graphql-vs-rest/)
							- https://roadmap.sh/graphql

				- Look into why you would use `query.search` vs `params.id`
				  id:: 63581b59-b74e-401b-aba5-de2419e1d828
					- id:: 63974faf-e48f-41e0-9ab7-985f98ed0e35
					  ```javascript
					  
					  router.get("/", async function (req, res) {
					    if (req.query.search !== undefined) {
					      const result = await searchBooksByTitle(req.query.search);
					      return res.json({ success: true, payload: result });
					    }
					  });
					  
					  router.get("/:id", async function (req, res) {
					    const book = await getBookById(req.params.id);
					    res.json({ success: true, payload: book });
					  });
					  ```
						- ```javascript
						  // when using GET postman for 
						  // http://localhost:3000/api/halloween_movies?t4t4=321312
						  query:  { t4t4: '321312' }
						  params:  {}
						      console.log(`query: `, req.query);
						      console.log(`params: `, req.params);
						  ```

				- Code Testing
				  collapsed:: true
					- Pros of testing
					  id:: 635fd754-bcfd-4b5e-bd8a-07f3dd778521
						- Tests let other people collaborate on your code, make changes, or add to it with confidence
						- Code written in easily testable pieces is more understandable by humans as well
						- Tests give you a head start on documenting the functionality of your code
						- Good tests help ensure that when you refactor your code that it still performs all it's needed functions
					- Cons
						- Time-consuming

			- Day 2
			  collapsed:: true
				- Test-Driven Development
				  collapsed:: true
					- Pros/Cons
					  collapsed:: true
						- Pros
							- Helps ensure planning step is done by combining it with test creation
							- Helps developers better analyze and understand client requirements and request clarity when they are not adequately defined.
							- Helps prevent refactoring from breaking the code
							- Test coverage under TDD is much higher compared to the conventional development models
								- This is because the TDD focuses on creating tests for each functionality right from the beginning, rather than having to write a whole bunch right at the end
							- Saves time writing documentation
						- Cons
						  id:: 6360f5dd-529d-4267-b7db-6ada9ecbd638
					- 3 levels of tests
					  id:: 646349f6-d814-4277-ae6d-8a2d143c7e09
						- End-to-End tests
						  id:: 6360f0ed-dac2-4efc-941e-631b8015a8a6
						  collapsed:: true

											- Getting started
											  collapsed:: true
												- Opening the app
													- Either
														- In ((63209272-1088-4824-a762-4ac7ded04b0a)) terminal
														  ```bash
														  npx cypress open
														  ```
														- or
														- In `package.json` add, which then allows you to run `npm run cypress:open` to open it
														  ```javascript
														  {
														    "scripts": {
														      "cypress:open": "cypress open"
														    }
														  }
														  ```

										- Common issues and how to troubleshoot
											- Having a verification error? Use the advice on this Stack Overflow page: [https://stackoverflow.com/questions/63667880/cypress-verification-timed-out-after-30000-milliseconds](https://stackoverflow.com/questions/63667880/cypress-verification-timed-out-after-30000-milliseconds).

										  collapsed:: true
										  [No-code](((629ccb26-fc95-4474-af25-e941969486e0))) tool. Provides a visual way to generate tests within Cypress, by recording interactions against the application under test.
											- Pressing the magic wand will run the app with all the previous commands up to that point. then you can manually interact with the browser some more
											- It'll write tests for you

									- Examples
									  collapsed:: true
										- Visit our webpage, check if there's a H1
										- `demo.spec.js`
										  ```javascript
										  describe("Home Page", () => { 
										    it("the hl contains the correct text", () => {
										      cy.visit("http://localhost:3000/")
										      cy.get('h1') 
										    }) 
										  })
										  ```
										- ```javascript
										  describe('An Example user journey', () => {
										    it('should pass if the user can add a new to-do and delete an old to-do', () => {
										      cy.visit('http://localhost:3000/')
										      // adds a new todo to the list
										      cy.wait(100);
										      cy.get('li > span').contains('Walk the dog').should('exist');
										      cy.get('#taskDesc').type("Eat a banana")
										      .should('have.value', "Eat a banana");
										      cy.get("#taskDate").type('2022-11-30')
										      .should('have.value', '2022-11-30')
										      cy.get('#create-new-todo').click();
										      cy.wait(300);
										      cy.get('li > span').contains('Eat a banana');
										      cy.get('button').last().click();
										      cy.get('li > span').contains('Eat a banana').should('not.exist');
										      cy.reload();
										      cy.get('li > span').contains('Walk the dog').should('exist');
										    })
										  })
										  ```
										- ```javascript
										  describe("Home Page", () => {
										    beforeEach(() => { 
										      cy.visit("/") 
										    })
										    
										    it("the hl contains the correct text", () => { 
										      cy.get(‘'h1').should('contain', "Testing Next.js Applications with Cypress")
										    })
										    
										    it("the hero contains the correct features", () => { 
										      cy.get("dt").its(0).should("contain", "4 Courses") 
										      cy.get("dt").its(1).should("contain", "25+ Lessons") 
										      cy.get("dt").its(2).should("contain", "Free and Open Source")
										    })
										  
										    it.only("the hero contains the correct features", () => { 
										      cy.get('[data-test="courses-dropdown"] > span').click() 
										      cy.get('[data-test="courses—dropdéwn-menu"]')
										    })
										  })
										  ```
										- ```javascript
										  describe('User Journey', () => { 
										    it('user adds to list', () => { 
										      cy.visit('http://localhost:3000/') 
										      cy.get('#todo-field').type("New item added") 
										      //YYYY-MM-DD date format 
										      cy.get("#completion-date").type("2022-10-04") 
										      cy.get('#create-List').click()
										    })
										    
										    it('validates user input', () = { 
										      cy.visit('http://localhost:3000/')
										      cy.get('#todos > li: last-child').contains('New item added')
										    })
										    
										    it('delete the first item', () => { 
										       cy.get('#todos > li:nth-child(1) > button').click() 
										    })
										    
										    it('validate the deletition of first item', () => { 
										      // check the length after deletion 
										      cy.get('li').should('have.length', 3)
										    }) 
										  })
										  
										  //document.querySelector("#todos > li:nth-child(1) > span")
										  ```

				- API Testing with SuperTest
				  id:: c7f9dca3-5195-41b9-baab-71af9a9ad153
				  collapsed:: true
					- Installing
					  ```javascript
					  npm install supertest -D
					  ```
					- ```javascript
					  import request from "supertest";
					  ```
					- Importing globals is optional but is good for getting Intellisense, auto completion etc
					  ```javascript
					  import { expect, test } from '@jest/globals';
					  ```
					- Creating a test using supertest
						- ```javascript
						  test('GET /users', async function () {
						    const response = await request(app).get('/users');
						    console.log(response.body);
						    // Is the status code as expected?
						    expect(response.status).toEqual(200);
						    // Does it contain the right data?
						    expect(response.body.success).toBe(true);
						    // Does it have the right structure
						    expect(response.body.payload).
						    
						    expect(response.body).toStrictEqual({
						    	success: true,
						   	// jest generics
						  	payload: expect.any(Array),
						      });
						  });
						  ```
					- Testing whether the status code sends properly
						- ```javascript
						  res.json()
						  // change it to this, otherwise it's 200 by default
						  res.status(201).json()
						  ```

				- Couchbase talk - Introduction to NoSQL database - by Asmaa Damoh, Perry Krug, Georgina Martin
				  collapsed:: true
				  id:: 63904f46-8a89-4b1d-a2dc-634d38cba4fc
					- Databases have programmatic access mechanisms, unlike if you were trying to access a random shared file. It also ensures integrity of data by only being accessible through a restricted API
					- Question
						- One usecase for databases that quite interested me was running a database just in user's browser rather than having one large centralised cloud database
							- which I believe IndexedDB, Postgres now can run via WebAssembly in the browser, and I think pouchDB as well can all run in the browser
							- Is it possible to do this with couchbase today, and if not are you aiming to target this sort of usecase

			- Day 1
			  collapsed:: true
				- React introduction
				  collapsed:: true
					- It's a library not framework
					- Virtual DOM
					  collapsed:: true
						- Virtual representation of UI kept in memory
						- Quicker to update
						- Reconciled with actual DOM (compares changes and updates accordingly)
						- AKA diffing
					- Example 1
						- ```javascript
						  const element = React.createElement(
						    'h1', // first argument: what type of element
						    {className: 'greeting'}, // second arg: what HTML class to add
						    'Hello world!' // third arg: what text to add
						    )
						  ```
							- ```javascript
							  // const element = document.createElement("h1"); const h1 = React.createElement (
							  //element type
							  "Hi",
							  //any attributes - object!
							  { className: "greeting" },
							  //text content
							  “Hello world!" );
							  ```
						- ```javascript
						  ReactDOM.createRoot(document.querySelector('#root')).render(element);
						  ```
					- [Workshop](https://github.com/SchoolOfCode/bc13_w7d1_react_101_intro_to_react-aaron-and-ben-room-40)
					- SOP:
					  id:: 63690bd6-b228-4b18-b39d-9031d39ae5e8
						- ((636c117a-e623-445d-8449-613edad2ae94))
						  collapsed:: true
							- {{{embed ((636c117a-e623-445d-8449-613edad2ae94))}}}
						- Select the `root` div and create `root` in ReactDOM
							- ```javascript
							  // Select root element and create root in ReactDOM
							  const rootElement = document.querySelector('#root');
							  const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(rootElement);
							  ```
						- Add to the bottom of `<script>` element a `root.render()` to render
							- ```javascript
							  root.render(Heading)
							  ```
							- Render element in the root
						- Using `createElement`
						  collapsed:: true
							- ```javascript
							  function Heading(text, className) {
							    return React.createElement( 
							    // 1st arg: element type 
							    "h1", 
							    // 2nd arg: any attributes in an object
							    { 
							      className: "greeting",
							  	onClick: handleClick, 
							    }, 
							    //text content
							    text
							    );
							  }
							  //Render element in the root 
							  root.render(Heading("New React project!", "heading"));
							  ```
					- JSX
					  id:: 6369219c-7bc6-42d7-8c31-62c5731a049e
					  collapsed:: true
					  AKA JavaScript XML
						- What is it
							- Syntax extension
							- Declarative API for writing components
						- Example
							- Comparing JSX vs non-JSX:
							  id:: 636922cc-1a50-43fa-97b4-cd1ab777cba8
								- Without JSX:
								  ```javascript
								  const element = React.createElement( 
								    'h1',
								    { className: 'greeting' }, 
								    'Hello world!'
								  )
								  ```
								- With JSX:
								  ```jsx
								  const element = (
								    <hl className="greeting">
								      Hello world!
								    </h1>
								  )
								  ```
						- How to use JSX
						  id:: 63692527-206a-4987-83f9-18970d12058a
							- ((63690bd6-b228-4b18-b39d-9031d39ae5e8))
							  collapsed:: true
								- {{embed ((63690bd6-b228-4b18-b39d-9031d39ae5e8))}}
							- 1) Add the following script to the head of the html document (below the React scripts if fine). This will load babel which allow us to write JSX. Our JSX will be converted back to the raw api.
								- ```javascript
								  <script src="[Attention Required! | Cloudflare](https://unpkg.com/babel-standalone@6/babel.min.js"></script>)
								  ```
							- 2) In the `<body>` tag, below the root `<div>`, add `<script type="text/babel">` to store all the JSX
							- 3) Some closing tags are now optional, whereas they're normally mandatory in HTML (`</script></body></html>` aren't needed)
							  collapsed:: true
								- `<html>`
								- `<body>`
								- `<script type="text/babel">`
								- Example template
									- ```jsx
									   ...
									  </head>
									  <body>
									    <div id="root"></div>
									    <script type="text/babel">
									      const rootElement = document.querySelector("#root");
									  ```
										- i.e. `</script></body></html>` aren't needed
							- Convert React syntax to JSX
							  collapsed:: true
							  id:: 636a77ab-804f-490b-9651-aae872921269
								- Notable differences
									- Ensure that any vanilla JavaScript you write is now wrapped in `{ }`
									  collapsed:: true
										- Examples
											- ```jsx
											  // Standard React
											  function Button() {
											    return React.createElement('button',
											    { className: 'container', onClick: handleClick }, 
											    'Click Me!'
											    )
											  }
											  
											  // JSX
											  function Button() {
											    return <button className='container' onClick={handleClick} >Click me</button>
											  }
											  ```
											- ```jsx
											  function Heading(number) { 
											    return <h1 className="heading">{number+1}</h1>;
											  }
											  ```
											- ```jsx
											  function Heading(text, className) {
											    return <h1 className={className}>{text}</h1>;
											  }
											  ```
								- Example
									- ```jsx
									  // Standard React
									  const element = React.createElement(
									    "h1",{ className: 'container' }, 'Hello World');
									  
									  // JSX
									  const element = (
									    <div className='container'>Hello World</div>
									  );
									  ```
									- Using `function` rather than `const` here because it allows you to call it multiple times, use `prop`, etc. It's also known as "components" because it's reusable. Shouldn't really need `const` except for the most basic static elements?
									  ```jsx
									  // Standard React
									  function Title() {
									    return React.createElement("h1", {}, "JSX Rules!");
									  }
									  
									  // JSX
									  function Title() {
									    return <h1>JSX Rules!</h1>;
									  }
									  ```
									- ```jsx
									  // Standard React
									  function Button() {
									    return React.createElement('button',
									    { className: 'container', onClick: handleClick }, 
									    'Click Me!'
									    )
									  }
									  
									  // JSX
									  function Button() {
									    return <button className='container' onClick={handleClick} >Click me</button>
									  }
									  ```
							- [Babel](https://babeljs.io/)
							  collapsed:: true
								- It's a transpiler
								- It gets automatically imported along with React
								- It converts JSX to browser-compatible JavaScript
								  collapsed:: true
									- e.g. ((636922cc-1a50-43fa-97b4-cd1ab777cba8))
										- {{embed ((636922cc-1a50-43fa-97b4-cd1ab777cba8))}}
						- `render`
						  collapsed:: true
							- Show 'Hello world' on the page
							  ```javascript
							  root.render('Hello world')
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  root.render(<Heading text='Hello world' />);
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  root.render(
							    [
							      <ListItem text="Milk" />
							      <ListItem text="Eggs" />
							      <ListItem text="Flour" />
							    ]
							  );
							  ```
						- Whatever you name the parameter becomes a new JavaScript object. `prop` is the convention and React treats it specially
							- ```jsx
							  function Heading(props) {
							    // props
							    // { text: ‘JSX', name: ‘lewis', animal: ‘cat'}
							    return <h1 className="heading">{props.text}</h1>;
							  }
							  //Render element in the root 
							  root.render(<Heading text='JSX' name='lewis' animal='cat' />);
							  
							  // expected HTML output: a H1 with the text 'JSX'
							  ```
						- Props and Children
						  id:: 636a5bf8-c596-42c4-be70-4e813acb346c
						  collapsed:: true
							- Props
								- `props` as the parameter for a component is a convention, but it can be named anything
								- The parameter allows us to access everything passed with the same name component in `root.render` (line 8-10)
								- Example
									- All the ListItem get added to the `props` object
									  ```jsx
									  function ListItem(props) {
									    return <li>{props.text}</li>
									    console.log(props) // will show the ListItem objects
									  }
									  
									  root.render(
									    [
									      <ListItem text="Milk" hasBorder={true} key='1'/>,
									      <ListItem text="Eggs" hasBorder={true} key='2'/>,
									      <ListItem text="Flour" hasBorder={true} key='3'/>,
									    ]
									  );
									  ```
								- ((636cf19d-e2d4-420d-9b86-9b519fbb7dde))
									- add notes
								- Child components can't pass information back up to their parent components directly via props. It's only from parents > child
							- Children
							  id:: 6369513a-a8f7-4672-acc2-51737bb780eb
								- Example - how to define parent-child relationship?
									- ```jsx
									  function ListItem(props) {
									    return <li>{props.text}</li>
									  } 
									  
									  function ListItem(props) {
									    console.log(props);
									    return <ul>{props.children}</ul>;
									  }
									  
									  root.render(
									    <List>
									      <ListItem text="Milk" />
									      <ListItem text="Eggs" />
									      <ListItem text="Flour" />
									    </List>
									  );
									  ```
									- With destructuring
										- ```jsx
										  function ListItem({ text }) {
										    return <li>{text}</li>
										  } 
										  
										  function ListItem({ children }) {
										    console.log(props);
										    return <ul>{children}</ul>;
										  }
										  ```
								- Not sure why/the difference
									- ```jsx
									  // Previously was defining
									  root.render(
									    <List>
									      <ListItem text="Milk" />
									      <ListItem text="Eggs" />
									      <ListItem text="Flour" />
									    </List>
									  );
									  
									  // 
									  root.render(
									    [
									      <ListItem text="Milk" />
									      <ListItem text="Eggs" />
									      <ListItem text="Flour" />
									    ]
									  );
									  ```
							- s
								- ```javascript
								  function ListItem(props) { 
								    // code here 
								    console.log(props) 
								    return <li>{props.text}</li>
								  } 
								  
								  let myProp={text:"milk", hasBorder: true}
								  
								  myProp.hasBorder
								  
								  root.render( 
								    [ 
								      <ListItem text="Milk" />, 
								      <ListItem text="Eggs" />, 
								      <ListItem text="Flour" />,
								    ]
								  );
								  ```

				- Morning
					- Morning quiz
					  collapsed:: true
						- JSX
							- JSX is transpiled into raw React.createElement thanks to tools like Babel.
							- JSX is syntactically similar to HTML and lets you hand in props like attributes.
							- JSX is a syntax extension that provides a declarative API for components.
						- React's virtual DOM
							- A virtual representation of the UI kept in memory and reconciled with the actual DOM to keep track of any changes and update the view accordingly
							- When an element rerenders on the actual DOM due to changes on the virtual DOM, that element rerenders and its children.
						- React components
							- They can be written as functions that return React elements
							- You can customize them by handing in props (that function in a similar way to attributes in HTML)
							- You can scale your application by reusing your components as many times as you want

					- Array methods
					  collapsed:: true
					  id:: 636a77ab-9100-4564-b0f6-324cd53f42b9
						- Some mutable array methods i.e. they alter the original array
							- ((63679853-975f-4456-b131-53731a001078))
							- ((63679853-27fd-40a4-bd44-b836c61f4394))
							- ((63679853-dcb7-4c52-a93f-2ba70da4b082))
							- ((63679853-f388-4ef7-98f4-ccfcebf4cd89))
							- ((634bc0c2-38ee-4fdb-b785-2b19b9d2a6e1))
							- ((635eb08f-48a4-41e1-9112-9f62670ddec2))
						- Some immutable array methods i.e. doesn't alter the original array
							- ((6350374d-fdba-4eb4-b17a-f80e5e5a3fcb)) - creates a new array which we can then run code on
							- ((63470fd1-ed0f-45f6-9b63-2ee67c3c152f)) -
							- ((63679853-1c98-454a-8932-e67322c119d9))
							- ((63679853-a37a-4046-8ccc-7841dfaa48a9))
							- ((63679853-bf0c-44a1-9fba-087790dbcc45))
							- ((63679853-9c2f-4df7-859c-e2278dbebdb7))
							- ((63470fd1-5c6c-4c4d-aea0-9d111a003975))
						- Use array method that don’t mutate the original array with React, because mutating arrays in React can lead to issues because it might be comparing that in a virtual dom

					- React Components and Create React App
					  collapsed:: true
						- [Create React App](https://create-react-app.dev/docs/getting-started/)
						  id:: 636a77ab-fbaf-4088-8f12-12f334ce25b7
						  collapsed:: true
						  quick way to setup new React projects
							- Pros/Cons
							  collapsed:: true
								- Cons
									- [Stop Using Create React App - YouTube](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kvkAoCbTM3Q)
									  collapsed:: true
										- {{video https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kvkAoCbTM3Q}}
											- {{youtube-timestamp 35}} Slow for development, no production-ready features, no server-side generation, TypeScript support is poor
											- {{youtube-timestamp 86}} Vite - faster, good plugin support, production-ready features available, post-CSS built-in, supports Svelte/Vue too, built-in TypeScript support
											- {{youtube-timestamp 138}} Con - server-side rendering not built-in,
											- {{youtube-timestamp 150}} Astro or Gatsby - use them for static site generation
											- {{youtube-timestamp 297}} Next.js - both server-side rendering and static site generation - can make different pages different types
											-
									- [7 better ways to create a React app - YouTube](https://youtu.be/2OTq15A5s0Y)
									  collapsed:: true
										- Why
											- It's slow during development builds
											- Poor support for popular tools like Tailwind, TypeScript
											- There are better alternatives like Vite, Next.js, etc
										- Alternatives
											- 1) Instant environment e.g. StackBlitz, CodeSandbox
											- 2) ((63a048ff-b45a-4636-af0a-339ae76bbf8f)) - way faster than CRA
											- 3) ((63dbe535-1504-4fc1-82d4-752f5cf485d3))
											- 4) React rendering frameworks AKA metaframeworks e.g. ((635fba79-b85e-4821-810a-ce45a268a6ad))
											- 5) ((63c581b3-6a07-4a44-a48b-ea879c312890)) doesn't use server components atm but does have stable nested layouts and streaming
											- 6) ((63a1bad2-0908-45c5-bcce-4a720870aba3))
											- 7) ((63c581b3-da76-4fdb-851c-42dfad80ba9d))
								- Differences compared to ((636c117a-e623-445d-8449-613edad2ae94))
									- No longer need to update `index.html` with a bunch of additional code
									- Can store components easily across multiple files (as long as you import/export correctly)
							- Documentation
								- Most important info
									- ((636a77ab-ab83-4a5a-9a49-877b0661ae40))
								- *Getting Started*
									- *Intro*
									  collapsed:: true
										- ((6377ecf1-94e1-4a98-9bf4-9be3ee183db1))
										- Run `create-react-app`script to install all required dependencies
											- Either
												- `npx create-react-app frontend` or `npx create-react-app .` for current folder
												  id:: 6374cf1a-2046-492c-aeca-84942ae4a92b
												  collapsed:: true
													- `my-app` = directory to use. Can use `.` for the same directory
													- ((636a7232-bbde-4f4c-80d5-0ef4c9c21da5))
													- Alternatively `npm init react-app my-app`
													- `npx create-react-app .`?
												- TypeScript version
												  id:: 63999485-a0cc-405c-8de3-5cb744848de2
												  collapsed:: true
												  `npx create-react-app frontend --template typescript`
													- [Adding TypeScript | Create React App](https://create-react-app.dev/docs/adding-typescript/)
													- Alternative: how to add TypeScript to an existing project
													  `npm install --save typescript @types/node @types/react @types/react-dom @types/jest`
													- Notable differences
														- `as HTMLElement` in order to account for elements possibly being null
														  id:: 63999a64-a72c-4a0d-80e1-8aeca36aedc7
															- `/src/index.tsx`
															  ```typescript
															  const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById('root') as HTMLElement);
															  ```
																- Uses aliasing `as` in order to get around the issue that an element can possibly be null if it isn't found
																- ((63f8fe5a-255e-4682-b228-cc2790a41d73)): The `as` keyword is used to cast the element to the `HTMLElement` type, which is the type of the `root` element in an HTML document.
																- In contrast, JSX version:
																  ```jsx
																  const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById("root"));
																  ```
																- In a previous workshop we had to use an `if` statement to take into account it may be null, however this `as` version sounds better
																  ```typescript
																  let button = document.querySelector("#button");
																  if (button) {
																    button.addEventListener("click", handleQuote);
																  }
																  ```

										  Run the 'start' script, which will launch the app and make it accessible via http://locahost:3000

											- ```javascript
											  import React from "react";
											  import ReactDOM from "react-dom/client";
											  import "./index.css";
											  import App from "./components/App/App";
											  import reportWebVitals from "./reportWebVitals";
											  
											  const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById("root"));
											  root.render(
											    <React.StrictMode>
											      <App />
											    </React.StrictMode>
											  );
											  
											  // If you want to start measuring performance in your app, pass a function
											  // to log results (for example: reportWebVitals(console.log))
											  // or send to an analytics endpoint. Learn more: [Measuring Performance | Create React App](https://bit.ly/CRA-vitals)
											  reportWebVitals();
											  
											  ```
										- `/src/App.js`
										  id:: 636a7e3c-1b76-4fb1-9c76-380c59295fbe
										  AKA `/src/components/App/index.js` as taught in bootcamp
											- ```javascript
											  import "./App.css";
											  
											  function App() {
											    return <div className="App"></div>;
											  }
											  
											  export default App;
											  ```
											- Default path is `/src/App.js`, but it's better to split up our components into multiple files so it's good to have a `components/App/` folder
											-
								- *If using create-react-app*
									- `npm start` will run a script to launch the React app, which becomes accessible at http://localhost:3000

						- Per component (function) there should be one parent e.g. a div. Children should be inside that div
						- Component types
							- Class Component (older method)
							  id:: 636a77ab-749a-46fc-a3e4-eef69f620ca8
							  collapsed:: true
								- Rarely used in modern React development but we still sometimes see them if we work on old projects
								- Example
									- ```jsx
									  class Welcome extends React.Component {
									    render() {
									      return <hi>Hello, {this.props.name}</h1>;
									    }
									  }
									  ```
							- Function Component (newer, modern standard method)
							  id:: 636a77ab-b77d-42e3-b10c-663b40a68510
							  collapsed:: true
								- Pros
									- More concise than ((636a77ab-749a-46fc-a3e4-eef69f620ca8))
									- Has new life cycle hooks
								- Example
									- ```jsx
									  function Welcome(props) { 
									    return <hi>Hello, {props.name}</hi>;
									  }
									  ```

									    console.log(prop2)

						- Nested Components
							- Example
								- Add the component inside another component
								  ```jsx
								  function App() {
								    return (
								      <div className='App'>
								        <Card />
								      </div>
								    )
								  }
								  ```
						- Note: all components are self-closing because they're not HTML elements, they're React components/functions
						- `root.render`
							- ```javascript
							  const root = ReactDOM.createRoot(document.getElementById("root"));
							  root.render(
							    <React.StrictMode>
							      <App />
							    </React.StrictMode>
							  );
							  ```
						- `props` workflow
							- 1) Within the parent component create a HTML attribute for the child element
								- Example
									- ```javascript
									  function Card() {
									    return <div className="card">
									     <Button buttext = "whatever"/>
									     <Button buttext = "hell2222oo"/>
									     <Button/>
									    </div>;
									  }
									  ```
							- 2) Within the child component you need to use `props` as a parameter and destructure to utilise the object
								- Example
									- ```javascript
									  function Button (props){
									      return <button>{props.buttext}</button>
									  }
									  
									  ```

			  collapsed:: true

						- ```javascript
						  function Card({heading, text}){ 
						    return <sectton> 
						      <h2>{heading}</h2> 
						      <p>{text }</p> 
						    </section>
						  };

						- `<Card heading='React' text='A helpful library for making scalable, quick applications' />`
					- ((6377ecf1-624c-402c-b1ea-8aa7e20b0c3c))
					  id:: 6377ecf1-2caf-4d9d-bf21-a5472bbf26ff
						-
					- When we use create-react-app to spin up our React app, our app itself is still rendered by ReactDOM - it's just abstracted away into a different file so we no longer have to manually render each component.
					- JSX is a syntax extension, and a declarative way to call components and hand in props
				- React's useState hook
				  collapsed:: true
					- Using a state is a way of giving your app memory and managing anything you want to control or track.
					- State = memory of how many clicks
					  Behaviour = functionality to track and update the state with each click
					- Smart component vs dumb components
						- Smart components = controls it's own state
						- Dumb component = reacts to what it's handed
					- We generally want dumb components for predictability
					- Example code of state in React before the useState hook was created
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```javascript
						  class Login extends Component { constructor(props) { super(props); this.state = { user: { email: ‘', password: ‘', }, };
						  this.onChange = this.onChange.bind(this); this.onSubmit = this.onSubmit.bind(this); }
						  
						  onChange(e) { const { user } = { ...this.state }; const currentState = user; const { name, value } = e.target; currentState[name] = value;
						  this.setState({ user: currentState });
						  ```
						- ```javascript
						  import React, { Component } from ‘react’; export default class Greeting extends React.Component { constructor(props) { super(props); this.state = {
						  }i this. handleFirstNameChange = this.handleFirstNameChange.bind(this);
						  this.handleLastNameChange = this.haendleLastNameChange.bind(this); }
						  handleFirstNameChange = (e) => this.setState({ firstName: e.target.value }); handleLastNameChange = (e) => this.setState({ lastName: e.target.value });
						  render() { return (
						  <div> ‘input value={this.state.firstName} onChange={this.handleFirstNameChange} ><br /
						  <input valuee{this.state.lastName} onChange={this.handleLastNameChange} ‘> <p? <span>{this.state.firstName} {this.state.lastName}</span> < /p : </div> );
						  ```
					- React Hooks
						- They were first released in October 2018.
						- They're built-in functionality in React that brings power to functional components.
						- They start with use (useState, useEffect, useReducer, etc.).
						- You can write custom hooks as well - you aren't limited to what's built in.
					- ((636b8509-62c3-4b08-ae56-fcc1e632600c))
					  collapsed:: true
						- {{embed ((636b8509-62c3-4b08-ae56-fcc1e632600c))}}
					- Using `useState` with split up components
					  collapsed:: true
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1668001211722_0.png)
						-
							- In this example `App` is a smart component, whereas `Display`, `PlusButton` and `MinusButton` are dumb components. Basically wherever a `useState` variable lives
							- Components
								- `App`
								  ```jsx
								  const [count, setCount] = useState(®);
								  
								  function incrementCount() { 
								    setCount(count+1);
								  }
								  function decrementCount() { 
								    setCount(count-1); 
								  }
								  ```
								- *Child components*
									- `Display` (display the app)
									  ```jsx
									  <Display count={count) />
									  ```
									- `PlusButton`
									  ```jsx
									  <PlusButton handleClick={incrementCount} />
									  ```
									- `MinusButton`
									  ```jsx
									  <MinusButton handleClick={decrementCount} />
									  ```
						- > "Lifting state: Finding the lowest common parent shared between the two [or more!] components and placing state management there, and then passing the state and a mechanism for updating that state down into the components that need it." - Kent C. Dodds
						- Props are the only way to send the state/functionality down to the components... and these props are the only way the component can update the state higher up.
						- Example component tree diagram
						  id:: 6377ecf1-624c-402c-b1ea-8aa7e20b0c3c
							- A tree diagram that shows the relationships between the components in an app (parents and children) and how information flows through the app, enabling easier development and documentation
							- ![image.png](../assets/image_1668002360797_0.png)
							- App
							  `/src/components/App/App.js`
								- ```jsx
								  import { AddSquatsButton } from '../'
								  import AddSquatsButton from "../AddSquatsButton/index.js";

								      //

				- Spread and Slice (immutable methods)
				  id:: 636cce43-e74d-482c-ab56-df86054a8e43
				  collapsed:: true
					- Mutable methods
						- Mutates the object (changes the same object)
						- Storage-efficient
						- Unpredictable and brittle
						- Complex to compare
					- Immutable methods
						- Creates a new object with any changes made
						- Less storage-efficient
						- More predictable
						- Easier to compare
					- Many new examples added to ((63642a10-4e86-4101-961a-8311efb8ae4f))

						  import PostDisplay from "./PostDisplay"; 
						  import myPosts from "./posts"; 
						  
						  function App() {
						    const [posts, setPosts] = useState(myPosts)
						    
						    return ( 
						      <main>
						        {posts.map((post) => { 
						          return <PostDisplay title={post.title} author={post.author} text={post.text} key={post.id}/> 
						        })} 
						      </main> 
						    ) 
						  }
						  ```

						    { id: 3, fontName: "Trebuchet MS" },
						    { id: 4, fontName: "Garamond" }

						  function handleChange(userinput) {
						    setText(userInput); 
						  } 
						  return ( 
						    <main className="App"> 
						      <h1>Font Viewer</h1> 
						      <Input text={text} handleChange={handleChange} /> 
						      {fonts.map(({ fontName }) => {

							  // first iterates over `fonts` object and returns a <Display> component for each
							  {fonts.map(fontObject) => { return <Display /> }
							  
							  // for each font property visited, pass down these values for each prop
							  <Display text={text} font={fontObject.fontName} key={fontObject.id} />
							  ```

					- An Introduction to RESTful APIs
						- **RE**presentational **S**tate **T**ransfer
						- Main four constraints:
							- Uniform Interface (HATEOS)
							- Client-Server
								- Separation of concerns - each doesn't need to know the implementation of the other
							- Stateless
							- Cacheable
					- HTTP Response Status Codes
					  id:: 663a57a0-9907-4f0d-ad33-087b77542e76
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1668426357368_0.png)
							- 1xx codes can be ignored
						- ![image.png](../assets/image_1727171375440_0.png)
						- Related: ((5ac78cbc-ed2d-4914-ab68-b05c2d27e278))
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1668426729023_0.png)
					- Always make a versioned API, so API consumers know your API isn't going to break
					- Types of API versioning:
						- e Resource Versioning:
							- Accept Header: = application/vnd.schoolofcode.v3+json
						- URI Versioning:
							- version is part of the URI as either a prefix or a suffix
							- /vl/customers
							- Most commonly used as it works across a variety of tools that might not support customised headers
							- downside: the URI changes with each new version
						- Hostname versioning:
							- version is part of the hostname rather the URI:
							- https://v3.schoolofcode.com/customers
					- Summary
						- Model your API based on the Resources it exposes
						- Your REST server must be client-state agnostic (all context should be in the request)
						- Use nouns but not verbs in the path/URI i.e. `POST /users` instead of `POST /createUser` and use plural nouns i.e. `/users/` instead of `/user/`
						- GET method should never alter state (use PUT/POST/PATCH/DELETE)
						- Handle Errors with HTTP Status Codes
						- Version your API
					- Related: ((637210c4-a1c0-466f-a6a4-c1efcecd62d8))
				- `useEffect` hook
				  id:: 6377ecf1-7342-4415-bca4-70688780c97d
				  collapsed:: true
					- ((6391cf38-8655-4c31-9539-0f62cd7542ba))
					- https://beta.reactjs.org/learn/keeping-components-pure
						- Pure functions don't edit variables outside of the function, unless they're imported as a prop
						- `useEffect` can be used
					- e
						- ```javascript
						  /*
						  Hand useEffect an anon callback fn 
						  Inside the anon fn -> do the fetch! 
						  Console.log the data from the fetch
						  
						  Make a state to hold the joke (useState) 
						  In the useEffect, set the joke state to the joke 
						  In the JSX we can then show the joke using the state
						  */
						  ```
						- id:: 6385f33b-96d0-4329-9560-ad1e091cee4d
						  ```jsx
						  const [joke, setJoke] = useState("");
						  
						  useEffect(() => { 
						    async function getJoke() { 
						      const response = await fetch("[icanhazdadjoke](https://icanhazdadjoke.com/",) { 
						        headers: { accept: "application/json" }, 
						      }); 
						      const data = await response.json(); 
						      console.log(data);
						      setJoke(data.joke); 
						    } 
						    getJoke();
						  });
						  ```
						- ```jsx
						  return (
						  	<div className='App'>
						      	<h1>{joke}</h1>
						  )
						  ```
					- Dependency argument
						- ` ` (no dependency argument) = activate this function constantly (multiple times per second)
						- `[]` = call it only on mount (page load)
						- `[joke]` i.e. name of state = call this only when the state changes
					- Example 1
						- ```jsx
						  export default function App() {
						    const [resourceType, setResourceType] = useState('posts')
						    
						    useEffect(() => {
						      console.log('resource type changed')
						    }, [resourceType])
						    
						    return ( 
						      <> 
						        <div> 
						          <button onClick={() => setResourceType('posts')}>Posts</button> 
						          <button onClick={() => setResourceType('users')}>Users</button> 
						          <button onClick={() => setResourceType('comments')}>Comments</button> 
						        </div> 
						        <h1>{resourceType}</h1>
						      </>
						    )
						  }
						  ```
				- Next day quiz:
				  id:: 63735841-a8f0-4256-a782-ac0ff9c314f9
					- Pure functional components
						- To be considered pure, React functional components must always return the same JSX given the same inputs.
						- React assumes that every component you write is a pure function by default.
						- To make a component function pure, when you want to change something in response to user input, you should set state instead of writing to a variable. You should never change preexisting variables or objects while your component is rendering.
						- If you’ve exhausted all other options and can’t find the right event handler for your side effect, you can still attach it to your returned JSX with a useEffect call in your component. This tells React to execute it later, after rendering. However, this approach should be your last resort.
					- What do we mean by side effects in React?
						- Anything happening/changing outside the scope of the component function (such as a fetch request) or isn't part of its rendering logic, which can make that component no longer pure and affect how React's virtual DOM keeps things in sync
					- When would use `useEffect`?
						- If you want to control when an effect happens and when the component rerenders in response
						- If you're depending on something outside of React (such as the result of a fetch request when a component renders), directly interacting with the DOM (e.g. updating the document's title), or synchronising with some system outside of React
						- If you want something to only happen when the component loads (on mount)
					- In order to specify the conditions when a useEffect should be triggered, you need to add a dependency array as the second argument.
					- What do I need to put in the blank in my dependency array below (where the _?_ is) so that the document title changes every time the count state is updated?
						- ```javascript
						  useEffect(( )=>{ 
						    document.title = “You've clicked ${count} times.’ ;
						  }, [_?_])
						  ```
						- Answer: `count`
					- If I want the action in my useEffect's callback function to happen ONLY when the component initially renders (on mount), what should I put as the second argument of useEffect, where the _?_ blank is in the code below?
						- ```javascript
						  useEffect(( )=>{ /f/actton I want to happen on mount
						  }, _?_)
						  ```
						- Answer: `[]` (empty array)
					- Have a look at the following code using fetch in a useEffect that fetches a joke on mount (when the component first renders). There are three errors. Can you find them? Tick all that apply.
						- ```jsx
						  const [joke, setJoke] = useState("");
						  
						  useEffect(( )=>{ 
						    function getJoke(){ 
						      let response = await fetch("[icanhazdadjoke](http://icanhazdadjoke.com/",) { 
						        headers: {accept: "application/json"}
						      });
						      let data = response.json(); 
						      setJoke(data.joke)
						    };
						    getJoke;
						  }, []);
						  ```
						- The function getJoke on line four should be async.
						- The getJoke function is not being called properly after it's defined.
						- There should be an await before response.json() on line eight.
			- Day 2
			  collapsed:: true
				- `useReducer` hook
				  id:: 63904f45-3135-4957-8487-b22e6b434b30
				  collapsed:: true
					- See ((6396f76e-d0d4-4d27-b88d-8faf03d8a429))
					- Why
						- Separates logic which changes state from the components which use it.
						- Ensures state can only be changed in an explicit number of ways
					- Syntax
						- ```javascript
						  const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(reducer, initialState)
						  ```
							- *Array destructured out of `useReducer`*
								- `state` = variable that holds the latest current value of our state that we can use in JSX, just like a `useState` state
								- `dispatch` = function we call in our code ourselves, passing in an action object (with an action type and any other data that the reducer function needs to change the state)
							- `useReducer()` = calls the reducer function under the hood, using the action object when dispatch is called
							- `reducer` = reducer function. Logic to change the state only through defined action types (we define this function elsewhere in code)
							- `initialState` = starting value for the state (similar to how we pass an initial state into `useState`)
					- FINITE STATE MACHINE: A system where particular inputs cause particular changes in state.
					- DRAW TURNSTILE DIAGRAM: STATES AND ACTIONS
						- {{renderer code_diagram,plantuml}}
						  collapsed:: true
							- ```plantuml
							  @startuml
							  hide empty description
							  [*] -> Locked
							  Locked --> Unlocked : Coin
							  Unlocked --> Locked : Push
							  Locked --> Locked : Push
							  Unlocked --> Unlocked : Coin
							  
							  @enduml
							  ```
					- What we're used to
						- ```javascript
						  const [list, setList] = useState([]);
						  function addToList(newListItem) { 
						    const ListObject = { 
						      name: newListItem, isCompleted: false 
						    }; 
						    const updatedList = [
						      ...list, lListObject
						    ]; 
						    > setList(updatedList);
						  }
						  ```
					- {{renderer code_diagram,plantuml}}
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```planttuml
						  @startuml
						  hide empty description
						  App -> useReducer : dispatch action
						  App : this is a string
						  useReducer -> ReducerFunction : action + state
						  useReducer : this is a string
						  ReducerFunction -> App : new state
						  ReducerFunction : this is a string
						  @enduml
						  ```
					- [Extracting State Logic into a Reducer](https://beta.reactjs.org/learn/extracting-state-logic-into-a-reducer)
						- Many examples and explanation
					- Examples
						- Example 1
						  collapsed:: true
							- Instead of `useState`,  we use `useReducer` for the main state
							  ```jsx
							  const initialState = { isOpen: false, money: 0 };
							  
							  // useState version
							  const [state, setState] = useState(initialState);
							  
							  // useReducer version. We pass a `reducer` function as another argument
							  const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(reducer, initialState) ;
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  function reducer(state, action) {
							    switch (action.type) {
							      case "ADD _COIN":
							        console. log("ADD_COIN");
							        return { ...state, isOpen: true, money: state.money + 1 };
							      case "PUSH": {
							        console. Log("PUSH");
							        return { ...State, isOpen: false };
							      }
							      default:
							        console.log("weeewooo");
							        console.log(state);
							        return state;
							    }
							  }
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  function App() { 
							    const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(reducer, initialState);
							    
							    return ( 
							      <main> 
							        <button onClick={() => dispatch({type: "ADD_COIN" })}>coin</button>
							        <button onClick={() => dispatch({ type: "PUSH" })}>push hand</button> 
							        <Turnstile isOpen={state.isOpen} money={state.money} /> 
							      </main> 
							    ); 
							  }
							  ```
						- Example 2
						  collapsed:: true
							- Changing a `useState`-based component to `useReducer`
							- Original code which used `useState`:
								- ```javascript
								  function Counter() { 
								    // const [counter, setCounter] = useState(0);
								    
								    // function incrementCounter() {
								    // const updatedCounter = counter + 1; 
								    //  setCounter(updatedCounter);
								    // }
								    
								    // function decrementCounter() {
								    // const updatedCounter = counter - 1; 
								    //  setCounter(updatedCounter);
								    // }
								  ```
							- Plan:
							  ```javascript
							  /*
							  - Starting point for the state - initial state (define in a variable): @
							  - Reducer function:
							  - Take in current state and action object
							  - Switch based on a condition —> action type (property in the action object)
							  - First action type: INCREMENT
							  	— Add 1 to current count state
							  	— Return new state
							  - Second action type: DECREMENT
							  	- Subtract 1 from current count state
							  	—- Return new state
							  - default -> return the state unchanged
							  
							  - Call useReducer:
							    - Import useReducer from React
							    - Call it at the top level of our component
							  	- Get out the state and the dispatch function (dispatch fn will allow us to pass an action obj to the reducer fn) -> use an array
							      
							  */
							  ```
							- ```javascript
							  const initialState = 0;
							  
							  function reducer(state, action) {
							    
							  }
							  
							  function Counter() {
							    
							  }
							  ```

				  collapsed:: true
					- Review of a typical test format
						- ```javascript
						  function addTwoNumbers(a, b) { 
						    return a + b;
						  }
						  
						  test("add two numbers and returns the sum", () => {
						    // ARRANGE: Setup
						    
						    // ACT: the thing that we're testing 
						    let actual = addTwoNumbers(1, 2); 
						    let expected = 3;
						    
						    // ASSERT: checking whether what we expected actually happened 
						    expect(actual).toBe(expected);
						  });
						  ```
					- ((6374cf1a-2046-492c-aeca-84942ae4a92b)) adds a `test` script for us
						- dependency: `@testing-library`
					- `npm t -- demo` to invoke it on `/src/components/App/demo.test.js`
					-
					- [React Testing Library](https://testing-library.com/docs/react-testing-library/cheatsheet)

						- Pros/Cons
						  collapsed:: true
							- Pros

							- Unsure
								- It doesn't load a real browser, it runs in NodeJS
									- Pros
										- Lightweight
									- Cons
										- May have limitations or bugs
						- `npm install --save-dev @testing-library/react` or it comes included with ((6374cf1a-2046-492c-aeca-84942ae4a92b))
						- It's similar to Cypress in that it interacts with the DOM, however there are differences
							- Unlike Cypress it tests only a component rather than the whole frontend, databases, etc
							- Cypress does have component test now but it's still only in beta
						- About
							- Lightweight solution for testing React components
							- Uses actual DOM nodes, querying the DOM in the same way the user would
							- Works with Jest but includes its own React-specific matchers as well
						- Built on
							- [GitHub - testing-library/dom-testing-library: 🐙 Simple and complete DOM testing utilities that encourage good testing practices.](https://github.com/testing-library/dom-testing-library)
							- [GitHub - testing-library/jest-dom: Custom jest matchers to test the state of the DOM](https://github.com/testing-library/jest-dom)
								- [jest-dom | Testing Library](https://testing-library.com/docs/ecosystem-jest-dom/)
						- Documentation

								- What is React testing library?
									- Most of it is built on [GitHub - testing-library/dom-testing-library: 🐙 Simple and complete DOM testing utilities that encourage good testing practices.](https://github.com/testing-library/dom-testing-library)
									- It's a library, not a testing framework
									- Testing framework-agnostic, but Jest is recommended

							    screen.debug(); // works like a console.log
							    const element = screen.getByText("All hail", { exact: false }); // doesn't have to be an exact match
							    
							    expect(element).toBeInTheDocument();
							    expect(element).toHaveTextContent("All hail Alice, the attentive one.");
							    
							    // ASSERT 
							    // select the element that contains the message
							    
							    // assert that it contains the right text
							  });

							  import userEvent from '@testing-library/user-event' 
							  import '@testing-library/jest-dom'
							  import Fetch from './fetch'
							  
							  test('loads and displays greeting', async () => {
							       // ARRANGE
							       render(<Fetch url="/greeting" />)
							  
							  	// ACT
							  	await userEvent.click(screen.getByText('Load Greeting')) 
							  	await screen.findByRole('heading')
							  
							  	// ASSERT 
							  	expect(screen.getByRole('heading')).toHaveTextContent('hello there') 
							      expect(screen.getByRole('button')).toBeDisabled()
							  })
							  ```

			- Day 5 - presentations
			  collapsed:: true
				- My part - project management 2 mins
				  collapsed:: true
					- **My personal intro**
						- Hi my name's Aaron, I've got a background in behaviour analysis therapy, as well as online marketing with pharmaceutical companies. I started hiring programmers for both professional and then later personal projects, which got me interested enough in the subject to then join the School of Code
					- **Project management tools overview**
						- For project management we had a preference for using just a small number of tools that we were familiar with, opting for ones with real-time collaboration so that we could asynchronously note our ideas and then later on synchronously discuss them together
					- **1) Google Docs**
						- Google Docs was very powerful tool for us, especially at the start of our project. It enabled us to all simultaneously note down our ideas for things like our team manifesto, then synchronously come to a conclusion with the aid of dot voting
						- Having everything we wanted to discuss on the first day written down on this document helped us make sure we were not forgetting anything important, and to gave us structure for our daily routines like our morning stand-up and afternoon energisers
					- **2) Excalidraw**
						- Excalidraw was a very useful tool that we used for our Disney ideation, user flow diagram, component diagram, database schema and our HTTP routes table
						- It was a constant valuable reference when planning out our design and code having a visual overview of how it is all expected to work together
					- **3) GitHub Projects**
						- We used the Kanban board view in GitHub Projects for task creation and assigning to specific pairs. We split up up our project into tasks that might take up to a whole day for a pair to complete, and then within them had subtasks to keep better track of progress
						- We quickly realised we had to split up our initial singular to-dos board into one board for todos for our MVP and another for bonus tasks, in order to stay on track for our goals. We went through our board multiple times a day, during our morning stand-up, midday meeting and end of day retrospective to make sure we were hitting our goals and to change them as needed

					- Most complicated ((629ccb26-62cc-426a-9616-4d8969f32580)) component:
					  id:: 63a47815-76b6-4989-b764-be3950f14c6e
						- `/src/components/Display/index.js`
						  id:: 63a4782d-6006-4a07-8405-7852d5332ad6
						  collapsed:: true
							- ```jsx
							  /**
							  * When the "voters" variable changes, the following code is run to update the votersArr
							  */
							  useEffect(() => {
							    let newArr = [];
							    for (let i = 0; i < voters; i++){
							      // Old array plus a new object
							      newArr = [...newArr, {name: i + 1, vote: "", voted: false}]
							    }
							    // Set the state to the value computed above
							    setVotersArr(newArr);
							  }, [voters])
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  /**
							  * Make an array of objects
							  * Each object contains the name of the select and its value
							  * This array is converted to a map to remove duplicates and then converted back into an array
							  */
							  function selectProCon(event){
							    let proConObj = {};
							    proConObj[event.target.name] = event.target.value;
							    proConSelect = [...proConSelect, proConObj]
							    const proConMap = new Map();
							    proConSelect.map(object => proConMap.set(Object.keys(object)[0], Object.values(object)[0]))
							    proConSelect = Array.from(proConMap, key => {
							      return {[key[0]]:key[1]};
							    });
							  }
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  /**
							  * Update the options array to contain the pros and cons comments
							  */
							  function handleProConClick(){
							    //Create a copy of the optionsArr
							    let newOptions = [...optionsArr]
							    //Loop through the proConSelect array (contains the data about whether or not it is a pro or con and which option it refers to)
							    for (let i = 0; i < proConSelect.length; i++){
							      //Loop through the proConInput array (contains the pro or con comment and which option it refers to)
							      for (let j = 0; j < proConInput.length; j++){
							        //If both the "pro / con" select and the comment are referring to the same option...
							        if (Object.keys(proConSelect[i])[0] === Object.keys(proConInput[j])[0]){
							          //Whether or not it's a "pro" or a "con" is stored in a variable
							          let proOrCon = Object.values(proConSelect[i])[0];
							          //Which option it refers to is stored in a variable
							          let compare = Object.keys(proConSelect[i])[0].replace(/["0-9"]/g, "");
							          //Loop through the options array (contains all options and the data relating to them)
							          for (let k = 0; k < newOptions.length; k++){
							            //If the option matches the "compare" variable...
							            if (newOptions[k].option === compare){
							              //And if its comment is a "pro"
							              if (proOrCon === "Pro"){
							                //Make a variable to store the comment
							                let newPro = Object.values(proConInput[j])[0]
							                //Make a copy of the pros array to store the comment into 
							                let newPros = [...newOptions[k].prosCons.pros, newPro]
							                //Make a copy of the prosCons object to store the newPros array into
							                let newObj = {...newOptions[k].prosCons, pros: newPros}
							                //Make a copy of the entire option object to store the new options object into 
							                let newOption = {...newOptions[k], prosCons: newObj}
							                //Store the entire new object into the copy of the options array
							                newOptions = [...newOptions.slice(0, k), newOption, ...newOptions.slice(k + 1)]
							              }
							              if (proOrCon === "Con"){
							                let newCon = Object.values(proConInput[j])[0]
							                let newCons = [...newOptions[k].prosCons.cons, newCon]
							                let newObj = {...newOptions[k].prosCons, cons: newCons}
							                let newOption = {...newOptions[k], prosCons: newObj}
							                newOptions = [...newOptions.slice(0, k), newOption, ...newOptions.slice(k + 1)]
							              }
							            }
							          }
							        }
							      }
							    }
							    //Set the optionsArr to be the newOptions array
							    setOptionsArr(newOptions)
							  }
							  ```
							- ```jsx
							  /**
							  * Update the options array to include the number of votes each option received
							  */
							  function proConSubmitVote(){
							    let newArr = [...optionsArr];
							    for (let i = 0; i < votes.length; i++){
							      for (let j = 0; j < newArr.length; j++){
							        if (Object.values(votes[i])[0] === newArr[j].option){
							          const newObj = {...newArr[j], votes: newArr[j].votes + 1}
							          newArr = [...newArr.slice(0, j), newObj, ...newArr.slice(j + 1)]
							        }
							      }
							    }
							    setOptionsArr(newArr)
							  }
							  ```
				- Code Reviews
				  collapsed:: true
					- Initial task
						- Resources for this afternoon:
						  collapsed:: true
							- Thoughtbot guide for code reviews: [guides/code-review at main · thoughtbot/guides · GitHub](https://github.com/thoughtbot/guides/tree/main/code-review#code-review)
							  collapsed:: true
								- Full document
								  collapsed:: true
									- # Code Review
										- A guide for reviewing code and having your code reviewed. Watch a presentation
										  that covers this material from [Derek Prior at RailsConf
										  2015](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PJjmw9TRB7s).
									- ## Everyone
										- Accept that many programming decisions are opinions. Discuss tradeoffs, which
										  you prefer, and reach a resolution quickly.
										- Ask good questions; don't make demands. ("What do you think about naming this
										  `:user_id`?")
										- Good questions avoid judgment and avoid assumptions about the author's
										  perspective.
										- Ask for clarification. ("I didn't understand. Can you clarify?")
										- Avoid selective ownership of code. ("mine", "not mine", "yours")
										- Avoid using terms that could be seen as referring to personal traits. ("dumb",
										  "stupid"). Assume everyone is intelligent and well-meaning.
										- Be explicit. Remember people don't always understand your intentions online.
										- Be humble. ("I'm not sure - let's look it up.")
										- Don't use hyperbole. ("always", "never", "endlessly", "nothing")
										- Don't use sarcasm.
										- Keep it real. If emoji, animated gifs, or humor aren't you, don't force them.
										  If they are, use them with aplomb.
										- Talk synchronously (e.g. chat, screen-sharing, in person) if there are too
										  many "I didn't understand" or "Alternative solution:" comments. Post a
										  follow-up comment summarizing the discussion.
										- If you learned something new, share your appreciation. 
										   ("I did not know about this. Thank you for sharing it".)
									- ## Having Your Code Reviewed
										- Be grateful for the reviewer's suggestions. ("Good call. I'll make that
										  change.")
										- Be aware that it can be [challenging to convey emotion and intention online]
										- Explain why the code exists. ("It's like that because of these reasons. Would
										  it be more clear if I rename this class/file/method/variable?")
										- Extract some changes and refactoring into future tickets/stories.
										- Link to the code review from the ticket/story. ("Ready for review:
										  https://github.com/organization/project/pull/1")
										- Push commits based on earlier rounds of feedback as isolated commits to the
										  branch. Do not squash until the branch is ready to merge. Reviewers should be
										  able to read individual updates based on their earlier feedback.
										- Seek to understand the reviewer's perspective.
										- Try to respond to every comment.
										- Wait to merge the branch until continuous integration (TDDium, Travis CI,
										  CircleCI, etc.) tells you the test suite is green in the branch.
										- Merge once you feel confident in the code and its impact on the project.
										- Final editorial control rests with the pull request author.
										  
										  [challenging to convey emotion and intention online]: https://thoughtbot.com/blog/empathy-online
									- ## Reviewing Code
									  
									  Understand why the change is necessary (fixes a bug, improves the user
									  experience, refactors the existing code). Then:
									- Communicate which ideas you feel strongly about and those you don't.
									- Identify ways to simplify the code while still solving the problem.
									- If discussions turn too philosophical or academic, move the discussion offline
									  to a regular Friday afternoon technique discussion. In the meantime, let the
									  author make the final decision on alternative implementations.
									- Offer alternative implementations, but assume the author already considered
									  them. ("What do you think about using a custom validator here?")
									- Seek to understand the author's perspective.
									- Sign off on the pull request with a 👍 or "Ready to merge" comment.
									- Remember that you are here to provide feedback, not to be a gatekeeper.
									- ## Style Comments
									  
									  Reviewers should comment on missed style guidelines. Example comment:
									  
									    > Order resourceful routes alphabetically by name.
									  
									  An example response to style comments:
									  
									    Whoops. Good catch, thanks. Fixed in a4994ec.
									  
									  If you disagree with a guideline, open an issue on the guides repo rather than
									  debating it within the code review. In the meantime, apply the guideline.
									  It's often helpful to set up a linter like [standard] to format code automatically.
									  This helps us have more meaningful conversations on PRs rather than debating
									  personal style preferences.
									  
									  [standard]: https://github.com/testdouble/standard
								- My notes
									-
							- Google guide for code reviews: [Code Review Developer Guide | eng-practices](https://google.github.io/eng-practices/review/)
							- General guide for code reviews: [https://www.evoketechnologies.com/blog/code-review-checklist-perform-effective-code-reviews/](https://www.evoketechnologies.com/blog/code-review-checklist-perform-effective-code-reviews/)
							- ~38-minute video exploring code reviews (probably best to watch on at least 1.25x speed):
								- {{video https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PJjmw9TRB7s}}
									- First 20 mins
										- {{youtube-timestamp 131}} The purpose of code reviews is to catch bugs
										- {{youtube-timestamp 217}} Knowledge transfer, increased team awareness and finding alternative solutions were the main benefits that Microsoft found with code review
										- {{youtube-timestamp 393}} "If content is king, then context is God" -Gary Vaynerchuck
										- {{youtube-timestamp 466}} Insufficient context is an obstacle for good code reviews
										- {{youtube-timestamp 493}} PR author needs to include "why" the PR exists. "Fixes #1337" is insufficient, also include a TLDR
										- {{youtube-timestamp 605}} PR body should be descriptive enough in itself to describe all the why for the benefit of the PR
										- {{youtube-timestamp 657}} PR author doing descriptive PR body means that the reason why (context) is saved in the commit, and it saves the PR reviewer and author engaging in discussion for a while trying to describe the context
										- {{youtube-timestamp 774}} Ask don't tell
										- {{youtube-timestamp 862}} Ask "What do you think about extracting a service to reduce some of this duplication?" rather than "extract a service to reduce some this duplication
										- {{youtube-timestamp 891}} Ask, don't tell:
										  collapsed:: true
											- Examples
											  id:: 6384c352-7a23-47a0-8e84-f627b2fac7f4
												- What do you think about...
												- Did you consider...?
												- Can you clarify... ?
										- {{youtube-timestamp 984}} Don't say something like "why don't you just...?". It makes people feel dumb, accused, judged, aggressive
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1036}} Be positive instead e.g. ((6384c352-7a23-47a0-8e84-f627b2fac7f4))
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1077}} Socratic Method - ask questions to stimulate valuable discussions
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1117}}
											- Rules of engagement
											- As an author, provide context.
											- As a reviewer, ask questions rather than making demands.
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1144}} How to handle conflict
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1197}} It's fine to disagree about implementation, everybody does something different
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1228}} If they don't agree on the process of PR reviewing, then address it in advance
									- {{youtube-timestamp 1315}} If people repeatedly commit to master then revert the commit and instead open a PR review
									- *What to review*
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1409}} SOLID/Single Responsibility Principle (UNIX as well? do one thing well)
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1443}} Naming, Complexity
										- {{youtube-timestamp 1485}} Test Coverage
									- {{youtube-timestamp 1577}} last
							- Useful tools to enforce formatting/styling/linting rules (computers are better at picking up on formatting/linting issues, humans can instead focus on the problem/review): [[ITNEXT](https://itnext.io/keep-code-consistent-across-developers-the-easy-way-with-prettier-eslint-60bb7e91b76c](https://itnext.io/keep-code-consistent-across-developers-the-easy-way-with-prettier-eslint-60bb7e91b76c))
						- There's a lot of information there, you don't need to take it all in at once. If you're about to do a code review and need a starting point, think about:
							- can this code be written in a simpler way?
						- Then you can start to have look for things like:
							- readability
							- simplicity
							- correctness
							- does it solve the problem/ticket
							- are there automated tests to give confidence that the implementation is correct

					- In frontend repo

					- Backend
						- `npm i cors`
						  Add the CORS middleware
						- In main `app.js`
						  ```javascript
						  import cors from 'cors';
						  
						  // Put this new code
						  app.use(cors('*'));
						  ```
						- Use `"*"` wildcard initially rather than localhost
					- Frontend
						- Inside one of the components
						  ```javascript
						  useEffect(() => {
						    async function fetching () {
						      const data = await fetch('http://localhost:3000/api/snap?type=options',
						                               {
						        method: 'GET',
						        headers: {
						          'Content-Type': 'application/json',
						  
						        }
						      }
						                              )
						      const response = await data.json()
						      console.log(response)
						    } fetching();
						  })		
						  ```
