		- Step A:  [Create your own Pixel Character](https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSeZLc3_1JgJB2nQcPtgG__KPJPVdZ3FSv3egCqggDyykCPPMA/viewform?entry.1929390493=y3qagebylvaawy3qaje9uhlp4tycj57j)
		  id:: 62ac6520-170c-4f24-afd5-6335e9f6c562
		  collapsed:: true
			- HTML
				- HTML self-closing tags
				  collapsed:: true
					- We already know that `/` tells the computer that this is the end. Well, it turns out that if your element doesn't have anything in between the opening and closing tags, then you can shorten:
					  `<word></word>`
					  to
					  `<word />`
					- We call that shortened tag a self-closing tag, as it doesn't need a separate closing tag anymore.
				- The order of HTML elements affects which is drawn first. The first element is the first drawn
				  collapsed:: true
					- This example would drawn the eyes on top of the character face, rather than behind it
					- ```html
					  <character>
					    <face />
					    <eyes />
					  </character>
					  ```
				- Adding special attributes makes elements easier to style in CSS
				  collapsed:: true
					- `id` attribute
						- Example
						  `<character id="chris">`
						- Used for single-use, unique elements
						- Calling this via CSS
							- Example
							  ```css
							  #chris {
							    fill: green;
							  }
							  ```
								- The `#` means it refers to an element with an `id` called `chris`
					- `class` attribute
						- Example
						  ```html
						  <character id="chris">
						    <face class="skin" />
						    <eyes />
						    <arms class="skin" />
						  </character>
						  ```
						- Can use it multiple times for different elements, used for grouping/categorising
						- Calling this via CSS
						  collapsed:: true
							- Example
							  ```css
							  .skin {
							    fill: lightgreen;
							  }
							  ```
								- The `.` means it refers to an element with a `class` called `skin`
							- Example - multiple classes (`head`, `body`, `skin`)
							  ```html
							  <character id="chris">
							    <face class="head skin" />
							    <eyes class="head"/>
							    <arms class="body skin" />
							  </character>
							  ```
					-
			- CSS
				- Choose colour using `fill`
				  collapsed:: true
					- eg
					  ```css
					  hairstyle {
					      fill: green;
					  }
					  ```
				- Can use colours by name, hex code or RGB value
				  collapsed:: true
					- Name
					  ```css
					  hairstyle {
					      fill: green;
					  }
					  ```
					- RGB
					  ```css
					  hairstyle {
					      fill: rgb(200,50,10);
					  }
					  ```
					- Hex
					  ```css
					  bottoms {
					      fill: #001295
					  }
					  ```
				- `.background` class
				  collapsed:: true
					- Example1
					  ```css
					  .background {
					      background-position: 0% 0%, 0px 64px; 
					      background-repeat: repeat, repeat; 
					      background-attachment: scroll, scroll; 
					      background-image: linear-gradient(135deg, rgb(112, 128, 144) 21px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 22px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 24px, transparent 24px, transparent 67px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 67px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 69px, transparent 69px), linear-gradient(225deg, rgb(112, 128, 144) 21px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 22px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 24px, transparent 24px, transparent 67px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 67px, rgb(217, 236, 255) 69px, transparent 69px); 
					      background-origin: padding-box, padding-box; 
					      background-clip: border-box, border-box; 
					      background-color: rgb(112, 128, 144); 
					      background-size: 64px 128px; --darkreader-inline-bgimage: linear-gradient(135deg, #596673 21px, #232627 22px, #232627 24px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 24px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 67px, #232627 67px, #232627 69px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 69px), , linear-gradient(225deg, #596673 21px, #232627 22px, #232627 24px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 24px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 67px, #232627 67px, #232627 69px, rgba(0, 0, 0, 0) 69px); --darkreader-inline-bgcolor: #596673;
					  }
					  ```
					- Example2
					  ```css
					  .background {
					      background:
					      linear-gradient(135deg, #ECEDDC 25%, transparent 25%) -50px 0,
					      linear-gradient(225deg, #ECEDDC 25%, transparent 25%) -50px 0,
					      linear-gradient(315deg, #ECEDDC 25%, transparent 25%),
					      linear-gradient(45deg, #ECEDDC 25%, transparent 25%);
					      background-size: 100px 100px;
					      background-color: #EC173A;
					  }
					  ```

				- Review - Very good intro to how computers work, and pseudocode via Scratch
				- Notes
					- {{youtube-timestamp 426}} base10 refers to the fact that we humans use 10 numbers typically (0-9). We can use this to make bigger numbers e.g. 10
					- {{youtube-timestamp 475}} Computers instead use binary AKA base2 AKA 0 and 1 AKA bits (binary digits)
					- {{youtube-timestamp 628}} we use binary because it makes it easy to translate the physical electricity that powers electronics into data. e.g. morse code-like, or turning on a lightbulb for 1 and off for 0
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1041}} 100 = $$10^2$$, 10 = $$10^1$$, 1 = $$10^0$$ in base10. This is how all any possible number can be represented in base10
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1049}} in base2 however it's 100s column = $$2^2$$ (4), 10s column = $$2^1$$ (2), 1s column = $$2^0$$ (1)
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1126}} In binary numbers are represented like this: 0 =  `001`, 1 = `010`, 2 = `011`, etc. Bigger table below
					  collapsed:: true
						- |Actual number|Hundreds (4)|Tens (2)|Units (1)|
						  |--|--|--|--|
						  |1|0|0|1|
						  |2|0|1|0|
						  |3|0|1|1|
						  |4|1|0|0|
						  |5|1|0|1|
						  |6|1|1|0|
						  |7|1|1|1|
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1256}}  `110010` represents 50 because it's equivalent to `32 + 16 + 2` (see full table below)
					  collapsed:: true
						- |Actual number|100k (32)|10k (16)|Thousands (8)|Hundreds (4)|Tens (2)|Units (1)|
						  |--|--|--|--|--|--|--|
						  |50|1|1|0|0|1|0|
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1354}} we started to represent letters and puncutation in binary by using ASCII. We use 8 bits to represent these characters
					  collapsed:: true
						- `A` = 65 = `00100001`
						- `B` = 66 = `00100010`
						- `C` = 67 = `00100011`
						- ...
						- `!` = 33 = `00100001`
						- asciichart.com
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1672}} these binary bits are small, and they're what make up bigger programs. We tend to measure these days in terms of kilobytes, megabytes, gigabytes etc
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1752}} 8 bits can only represent 256 different symbols. This is limited because it doesn't have accented characters, non-English characters like Cyrillic, emojis,
					- {{youtube-timestamp 1938}} Unicode is the successor to ASCII, and is supposed to encompass all human language, emojis, etc. For example the crying laughing :joy: face emoji is `128514`
					- {{youtube-timestamp 2025}} Colours are represented by hex colour code or RGB usually, which also easily represented in binary
					- {{youtube-timestamp 2348}} Video is just many still images (frames)
					- {{youtube-timestamp 2819}} Algorithms just mean the way your program is designed, particularly the correctness and efficiency (speed and scalability) of it
					- {{youtube-timestamp 3361}} Write psuedocode to help design segments of code sometimes, if not already using code comments
					  collapsed:: true
						- ```
						  Pick up phone book
						  If person is on page
						      Call person
						      Go back to line 3
						  ```
					- {{youtube-timestamp 3666}} Scratch

			- Task - completing your very own Scratch game
				- standard CS50 requirements:
					- Your project must be a playable game.
					- Your project must take the name of the user as input.
					- Your project must track the score.
					- Your project must have at least two sprites, at least one of which must resemble something other than a cat.
					- Your project must use at least one condition, one loop, and one variable.
					- Your project must use at least one sound.
					- Your project should be more complex than most of those demonstrated in the lecture.

		- Step C: Being a good communicator is an essential skill for a modern developer, especially in a world of remote working. We'd like you to submit a video response to our interview questions and show us that you can confidently express yourself.  Instructions can be [found here](https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSfOzr4k_8pF70jOiqLOtXgnIovTIUgaktcUXPm9JQv76tV8sw/viewform?entry.1239495852=y3qagebylvaawy3qaje9uhlp4tycj57j).
		  collapsed:: true
			- **Tell us about a time you had to work in a team to solve a problem (1m)**
				- One time I was working for an online pharmacy and one morning we started getting a few emails from customers that they weren't getting order confirmation emails. I tasked one team member to check the email outbox, another to check our website backend for the orders themselves and I checked the database. I was the technical lead so I told them how to properly do the testing, and from this we discovered that the orders existed in our database but not on the website backend, so emails weren't being delivered. We had to revert a recent software change that we figured out had bugged the orders as well as manually fix those recent orders, but once that was done it was all fine and we suffered no data loss.
			- **Tell us about something which is a passion of yours (can be anything, from stamps, to TV, to pets, to watching clouds) - why do you like it, and what’s the most interesting part? (1m)**
				- I'm really passionate about Linux because firstly when I switched my laptop operating system to it a few years ago I was already in a period of swapping over all the proprietary software I was using to open-source alternatives, and I was doing that for privacy reasons basically. Windows was one of the last big proprietary software I was relying on, so it felt so freeing to finally move to Linux and stop feeling like all my usage data was being sent to Microsoft. So that was the first big thing I liked about it that it gave me a feeling of privacy, but nowadays since I've been using it a few years I think a far more interesting aspect is that it helps you adopt new technology more easily.
					- So most of the time if a new interesting open-source project gets posted on GitHub it'll only be available on Linux, and usually isn't hard to compile it or deploy it in a Docker container. If I was still using Windows it wouldn't quite be so easy to do, so I think it's led me to try out a lot of interesting new technology early such as Vorta, which is a backup app.
			- **Explain something that is complex to us (but that you know well) in a simple way (1m)**
				- The Tor Browser is the most private browser in the world, and a big part of how it achieves this is through something called State Partionining. So for example, say you're on the Telegraph website, not only do you store their own first-party cookies but also that of third-parties like Facebook. If you go to a different website that also embeds Facebook trackers then when it tries to store it's third-party cookie it'll be able to tell that it's been stored before, and it can correlate that in order to track you across the web.
					- State Partionining however when go to the Telegraph website, all first AND third party cookies are stored in a single isolated container, so when you go to a second website that has Facebook trackers it'll also store it's first and third party cookies in a second isolated container, and thus be unable to check the cookies of the first container and thus is unable to use those cookies to correlate and track your browsing habits across the web.
			- **Why do you want to join the School of Code? Why is it right for you? (1m)**
				- I like that you don't just focus on teaching coding because if I'm honest I think I could find very good educational materials from various online courses. I like instead that you have things like a personal mentor, hands-on workshops and pair programming, which I can see as them all being very beneficial for providing feedback about what my weaknesses are, how to address them and all-in-all how to learn faster.
				- Industry talks and Networking aspects I can see being very useful to get a better understanding of what industry wants, so I know how to ensure that across the long-term past this bootcamp that I'm developing in a way that will keep me always valuable to industry.
				- And probably the biggest factor is the high employment rate after bootcamp completion, I like to think I'd be part of that 85% so yeah it's very attractive to me to know that by going through this bootcamp I'll be able to be employed in the industry after this.
			- So yeah that's all my answers, thanks for your time and I hope to see you on the bootcamp soon.
		- Once you've completed steps A, B and C we'll send you an application form - this is the part where we need to collect some more details from you - it's also where you get the chance to tell us anything else you want that will help give us a sense of who you are :)
		- When your application is complete will be reviewed by the team - successful submissions are followed up with interview(s) & pre-course activities and then finally, shortlisted applicants will be invited to join our next bootcamp to leap into their technology journey with us!
		- To definitely be considered for the Autumn bootcamp you'll need to make sure your application is complete and submitted by 23:59h on 5th July 2022

		- Application ID

		- 1) Setting up your development environment
		  collapsed:: true
			- Interactive Development Environment (IDE) apps increase programmer productivity by combining common activities of 
			  writing software into a single application: editing source code, building executables, and debugging.
			- 3 Aspects:
				- Editing Source Code - has syntax highlighting, autocomplete, etc for better productivity
				- Building Executables - can compile from within IDE
				- Debugging - tools to better examine and detect bugs. Also can provide hints before compilation of incorrectly formatted code
			- Learn [Visual Studio Code full keybindings](((62d69b1f-e70c-44f4-9e96-50033d4c3ca0)))
		- 2) Preparing your filesystem
		  collapsed:: true
			- Variable naming conventions
			  id:: 646349f4-ccf3-42d4-be86-bbafbec3e0ad
				- The standard naming conventions used in modern software development are as follows:
				  id:: 6320936d-bef4-4732-892c-d22cffda40fa
					- Camel case
					  id:: 6320936d-0261-4020-95d8-3cb6163e9d66
					  collapsed:: true
					  `thisIsDromedaryCamelCase`
						- Same as ((b2c095a3-5b8c-49e3-a10d-877a42e8cb78)) except the first letter of the first word is not required to be uppercase
						- Two subtypes:
							- Upper camel case AKA ((b2c095a3-5b8c-49e3-a10d-877a42e8cb78))
							- Lower camel case AKA dromedary case
								- Example
									- thisIsDromedaryCamelCase
						- Used in JavaScript
					- Pascal case
					  id:: b2c095a3-5b8c-49e3-a10d-877a42e8cb78
					  collapsed:: true
					  `ThisIsPascalCase`
						- The first letter of a variable be uppercase, along with the first letter of every new word compounded together to create the variable.
						- Examples:
							- ThisIsPascalCase
							- AnotherPascalCaseExample
						- Used in class cases
					- Snake case
					  id:: 646349f4-5cfc-4661-b1be-43cb99c9e073
					  collapsed:: true
					  `this_is_snake_case`
						- Words are separated by `_`
						- Example:
							- this_is_snake_case
						- When every letter is capitalised, it's known as screaming snake case
						  id:: 6320936d-462e-4fff-846c-f50395bf227e
							- Example:
								- THIS_IS_SCREAMING_SNAKE_CASE
						- Used in Python
					- Kebab case
					  id:: 35bbc920-4ed2-4fe5-ae32-c53baf26746e
					  collapsed:: true
					  `this-is-kebab-case`
						- Words are separated by `-`
						- Example:
							- this-is-kebab-case
						- When every letter is capitalised, it's known as screaming kebab case
							- Example:
								- THIS-IS-A-SCREAM-KEBAB
						- Used in CSS
		- 3) How the internet works
		  id:: 6737679e-c152-4ffe-87c0-9e3ff698c152
		  collapsed:: true
			- WAN = a regional router run by your ISP usually one for your neighbourhood, which in turn may connect to one for your whole city
			- IP address gets the packets to the right computer, UDP gets the packet to the right program on that computer by checking what port an application is running on
			- UDP is good for high-bandwidth activities like video calling, as long as the application can gracefully handle a high amount of corrupted packets (e.g. video call quality gets lower)
			- TCP on the other hand is better for activities which require no corrupted packets e.g. XMPP, web browsing. Lower bandwidth but far more accurate
			- Layers of the ((67c04773-403c-4533-b89c-a96c041ee084))
			  collapsed:: true
			- World Wide Web runs ontop of the Internet. Applications like Skype, Minecraft etc also run on Internet
			- To access a webpage first you make a DNS request to a DNS server, which tells you the IP address for the domain you're looking up
				- Next the web server on the IP address forms a TCP connection with your computer
				- Then using HTTP your web browser is redirected to the correct URL for the webpage you're trying to access
				- The web server then sends the hypertext markup in HTML for the webpage
			-

			-
		- 5) Command line
		  collapsed:: true
			- https://schoolofcode.github.io/line-commander/
			- `cat`
			  collapsed:: true
				- Concatenate AKA print the plaintext in the file to the terminal
			- `pwd`
			  collapsed:: true
				- Print Working Directory. Prints the full path of the current folder you're in
			- `ls`
			  collapsed:: true
				- List the file contents of the current folder
				- _Arguments_
					- `-a` - Also list hidden dotfiles too
					- `-l` to find the biggest folder, cd into it and read secret_plans.txt
						- If you're not sure which is biggest try `ls -lh` which makes the results more human readable or `ls -lS` which will order the largest file first.
			- ((673767a8-7e32-448c-aea4-2424824fbb19))
			  collapsed:: true
			- `mv`
			  collapsed:: true
				- Move files
				- `mv folder-name ../folder-name` will move `folder-name` up a folder.
			- `mkdir`
			- `touch`
			  collapsed:: true
				- Create a file
				- Example
					- `touch README.md`
			- `grep`
			  collapsed:: true
			  id:: 636a77ab-1b7d-410e-8f03-fd427f329770
				- Search documents
				- The syntax is: `grep option "pattern" file`
				- Use the `-c` option to count on how many lines the pattern occurs.
				- To count on how many lines "hello" occurs in a file called greeting.txt we would write `grep -c "hello" greeting.txt`
				- Cheatsheet
				  collapsed:: true
					- ![image.png](../assets/image_1724696719567_0.png)
				- Related: ((635eb109-10e0-4dd6-9757-5002dab41b9f))
		- 6) [[Git]] and version control
		  id:: 62e8e1f6-01f5-46f6-89bd-a44d188cac3a
		  collapsed:: true
			- https://vimeo.com/632147610/b261a71a83
				- Basic step loop
					- `git init` - done once
					- `git add notes.txt` - stage the changes in this file
						- or `git add *`?
					- `git commit -m "started my project"`
				- Create a directory and Git repo for each separate project
			- How to setup Git via CLI
			  collapsed:: true
				- First set your identity, your name and email address like so:
					- ```
					  git config --global user.name "bolajiayodeji"
					   git config --global user.email mailtobolaji@gmail.com
					  ```
					- the  `--global`  option makes sure these values are used throughout your system
				- Next set up the default text editor you’ll use whenever you need to enter a message in Git. This is not compulsory, if you don’t configure this, Git will use your default editor. If you want to use something else, configure like so:
					- `git config --global core.editor emacs`
				- Check your settings
					- ```
					  git config --list
					  ```
			- [Learning Resources]
				- https://docs.github.com/en/get-started/onboarding/getting-started-with-your-github-account
				- https://product.hubspot.com/blog/git-and-github-tutorial-for-beginners
				- https://ohmygit.org/
				- https://hackernoon.com/understanding-git-fcffd87c15a3
				- https://education.github.com/git-cheat-sheet-education.pdf
				- https://dangitgit.com/en
				- [Personal Access Tokens](https://youtu.be/kHkQnuYzwoo)
